Prohibited Acts for Junub and Muhdith
Enumeration of prohibited acts for major and minor impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five prohibited acts for a junub and the two (or three) prohibited acts for a muhdith, and cite the Quranic or hadith evidence for each prohibition.

Core Idea
Purity is mandatory for prayer, tawaf, and touching the Qur'an; impurity makes these acts haram.

This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies the specific acts that become prohibited when a person is in a state of major impurity (janabah) or minor impurity (hadath). These prohibitions are based on clear proofs from the Quran and Sunnah, emphasizing the importance of ritual purity before engaging in acts of worship. Understanding these distinctions is fundamental to correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two states of impurity
Identify the difference between janabah (major impurity requiring ghusl) and hadath (minor impurity requiring wudu). The prohibitions differ in scope.
Junub has five prohibitions; muhdith has at least two (prayer and tawaf) and by extension touching the mushaf.
2
Memorize the five prohibitions for junub
List: prayer, reciting any verse of the Quran, touching the mushaf (even with a barrier? Actually without a barrier), entering a mosque, and tawaf.
Recitation includes any verse, even for dhikr.
3
Memorize the prohibitions for muhdith
The text explicitly mentions prayer and tawaf. Touching the mushaf is also prohibited by the same evidence (hadith and verse).
Some scholars list three, including touching the mushaf.
4
Match each prohibition to its dalil
For each prohibited act, identify the specific proof: prayer (Quran 5:6 and hadith 'no prayer without purification'), recitation (hadith 'do not recite'), touching mushaf (Quran 56:79 and hadith 'only pure'), entering mosque (hadith 'I do not permit'), tawaf (hadith 'tawaf is like prayer').
The proofs are explicit and decisive.
5
Recognize the authority level
These rulings are from Zahir al-Riwayah and are universally accepted in the Hanafi school. There is no significant intra-madhhab dispute.
The authority weight is 'zahir_riwayah' and cross-madhhab status is 'muttafaq alayh'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحرم بالجنابة خمسة أشياء : الصَّلاة، وقراءة آية من القرآن، ومسها إلا بغلاف، ودخول مسجد، والطُّواف. ويَحْرُم على المُحْدِثِ ثلاثة أشياء: الصَّلاةُ، والطُّواف. الدليل: الصلاة للأمر بالطهارة في آيتها (قوله تعالى: ﴿إِذَا قُمْتُمْ إِلَى الصَّلاةِ فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ﴾) ولحديث: «لا يقبل الله صلاة بغير طهور». وقراءة آية من القرآن لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن». ومس المصحف إلا بغلاف لقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَّرُونَ﴾ ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا يمس المصحف إلا طاهر». ودخول مسجد لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا أُحِلُّ المسجد لجنب ولا حائض». والطواف بالكعبة لشرفها ولقوله: «الطواف حول البيت مثل الصلاة».
And five things are prohibited for the junub: prayer, reciting a verse of the Quran, touching the Quran except with a cover, entering a mosque, and tawaf. And three things are prohibited for the muhdith: prayer and tawaf. The evidence: prayer due to the command for purity in its verse (Allah's statement: 'When you stand for prayer, wash your faces') and the hadith: 'Allah does not accept prayer without purification.' Reciting a verse of the Quran due to his saying: 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran.' Touching the mus-haf except with a cover due to Allah's statement: 'None touch it except the purified' and his saying: 'No one should touch the mus-haf except a pure person.' Entering a mosque due to his saying: 'I do not permit the mosque for a junub or a menstruating woman.' Tawaf around the Kaaba due to its honor and his saying: 'Tawaf around the House is like prayer.'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage lists the prohibited acts for a person in a state of major impurity (junub) and a person in a state of minor impurity (muhdith, i.e., without wudu). For the junub, five things are haram: (1) prayer, because the Quran commands wudu and ghusl for prayer; (2) reciting any verse of the Quran, even if it is for dhikr, based on the hadith; (3) touching the mus-haf (Quran) except through a cover, based on the verse and hadith; (4) entering a mosque, as per the hadith; (5) tawaf around the Kaaba, because tawaf is considered like prayer in terms of requiring purity. For the muhdith (one who has lost wudu), two things are prohibited: (1) prayer and (2) tawaf. Note that the text does not list all three for muhdith here; it mentions only two (salah and tawaf), but in the earlier summary it says 'three things' – the third would be touching the mus-haf (though that is implied). The proofs are provided from Quran and Sunnah, indicating that these prohibitions are based on clear textual evidence. The Hanafi school considers these prohibitions as binding, and violation without a valid excuse incurs sin.

Key Terms
جنابة (janābah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, usually due to sexual intercourse or seminal emission.
محدث (muḥdith): A person in a state of minor impurity (without wudu), needing wudu to become pure.
طواف (ṭawāf): Circumambulation around the Kaaba, a rite of Hajj and Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence (dalil) for the prohibition of tawaf without purification?
A) The verse 'When you stand for prayer, wash your faces'
B) The hadith 'Tawaf around the House is like prayer'
C) The hadith 'Do not recite the Quran when junub'
D) Consensus of the scholars

Key Takeaway
The five prohibitions for a junub and the two (or three) for a muhdith are firmly established by Quran and Sunnah, underscoring the necessity of purity before these acts of worship.

Prohibitions for Junub and Hadath with Proofs
Lists five prohibitions for junub and three for hadath with textual proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five prohibitions for a person in major impurity (janabah) and three for minor impurity (hadath), and explain the textual proofs for each.

Core Idea
Junub has five prohibitions; hadath has three — prayer and tawaf are common, but only junub is barred from Quran recitation, touching the mushaf, and entering the mosque.

In this passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition, we learn the specific actions that become prohibited when one is in a state of ritual impurity. The rulings are clearly divided between major (janabah) and minor (hadath) impurity, each supported by direct evidence from the Quran and Sunnah. Understanding these distinctions is essential for daily worship and for upholding the sanctity of sacred acts.

Guided Path
1
List the five prohibitions for junub
Identify each prohibited act from the text: prayer, reciting Quran, touching the mushaf (Quran copy) without a covering, entering a mosque, and performing tawaf.
These prohibitions are explicit in the matn.
2
List the three prohibitions for hadath
Identify the prohibited acts for minor impurity: prayer and tawaf. Note that touching the mushaf and reciting Quran are also prohibited but covered elsewhere in the text.
The text states 'three things' but only lists two explicitly; the third (touching mushaf) is understood from other sections.
3
Locate the dalil for each prohibition
For each prohibition, find the proof cited: Quranic verses (e.g., 4:43 for prayer, 56:79 for mushaf) or hadith (e.g., prohibition of reciting Quran for junub, hadith on mosque entry).
The proofs are given in the sharh portion after the matn.
4
Analyze the dalil's authority
Assess the strength of each proof: most are from ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut) but are accepted by the Hanafi school as binding. Note that consensus (ijma') is cited for the exemption from qada during menstruation.
Shurut and arkan are not directly applicable; the ruling is a prohibition (haram).

(والطواف) بالكعبة لذاتها وشرفها، كما تقدم، ويَحْرُم على المُحْدِثِ ثلاثة أشياء: الصَّلاةُ)، للأمر بالوضوء عند القيام إلى الصلاة)، ولقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد غسل رجليه إتماماً للوضوء: «هذا وضوء لا يقبلُ الله الصَّلاةَ إِلا بِهِ» (١١). كما تقدم. (والطَّواف) لشرف البيت ولكونه مثل الصلاة، كما روينا (١٢).
It is prohibited for the junub (person in major ritual impurity) five things: prayer, reciting a verse of the Quran, touching it except with a covering, entering a mosque, and tawaf. And it is prohibited for the muhdith (person in minor ritual impurity) three things: prayer and tawaf. Prayer: and upon this consensus is concluded. And because hardship is an excuse that drops the duty of making up (qada), just as it drops performance, and in making up fifty prayers every month there is hardship, which is dropped by the text, and there is no hardship in making up fasting ten days in a year. And the duty to make up is obligatory by what makes performance obligatory according to the majority of our scholars, so the existence of the cause is sufficient for the obligation of qada, even if one was not addressed to perform, so she makes up fasting even though she was not addressed to perform it, and there is no problem according to the opinion of some of our scholars that qada is obligatory by a new command. And al-Haruriyya is a sect from the Khawarij attributed to Harura', a village in Kufa where they gathered, and the meaning is that they were extreme in their questioning to the point of being outsiders, because they went to extremes in religion until they left it. And five things are prohibited for the junub: prayer – due to the command for purity in its verse; and reciting a verse of the Quran – due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran'; and touching it except with a covering – due to the Almighty's saying: 'None touch it except the purified' [al-Waqi'a 79] and due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Let no one touch the mushaf except a pure person'; and entering a mosque – due to his saying: 'I do not permit the mosque for a junub or a menstruating woman'; and tawaf around the Kaaba due to its essence and honor. And three things are prohibited for the muhdith: prayer – due to the command to perform wudu when standing for prayer, and due to his saying, peace be upon him, after washing his feet completing wudu: 'This is a wudu that Allah does not accept prayer except with it'; and tawaf – due to the honor of the House and because it is like prayer, as we have narrated.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
The passage is a ruling_with_proof from the sharh of Nur al-Idah. It first states the five prohibitions for a person in major ritual impurity (junub): prayer, reciting any verse of the Quran, touching the mushaf except with a covering (i.e., a cloth or cover), entering a mosque, and performing tawaf (circumambulation) around the Kaaba. For a person in minor ritual impurity (hadath), three things are prohibited: prayer and tawaf (touching the mushaf is also prohibited for the muhdith, as is covered elsewhere). The commentary then justifies the exemption for women from making up missed prayers during hayd (menstruation) by citing consensus (ijma') and the principle of hardship (haraj): hardship is a valid excuse that removes the obligation of qada' just as it removes the obligation of ada'. Since requiring a woman to make up fifty prayers per month would cause great hardship, the exemption is based on textual evidence (nass). Conversely, making up ten days of fasting in a year involves no hardship, so fasting must be made up. The commentator notes that according to the majority of Hanafi scholars, qada' is obligated by the same cause as ada', meaning that the presence of the cause (e.g., entering the state of hayd) triggers the obligation to make up, even if the woman was not addressed to perform the prayer at that time. However, some scholars hold that qada' requires a new command. A brief digression explains that the term 'Haruriyya' refers to the Khawarij sect, named after the village Harura', indicating their extremism. The matn is then restated with proofs: for prayer (Quran 4:43), for Quran recitation (hadith), for touching the mushaf (Quran 56:79 and hadith), for entering the mosque (hadith), and for tawaf (based on the honor of the Kaaba and its resemblance to prayer). For the muhdith, the proof for prayer is the Quranic command to perform wudu (5:6) and the hadith 'prayer is not accepted without wudu'; for tawaf, it is like prayer.

Key Terms
جنابة (janābah): Major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, caused by ejaculation or sexual intercourse.
محدث (muḥdith): Person in state of minor ritual impurity (without wudu or with nullified wudu).
مصحف (muṣḥaf): The written copy of the Quran, as opposed to oral recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many things are prohibited for a person in the state of janabah (major impurity) according to this passage?
A) Three
B) Five
C) Seven
D) Four

Key Takeaway
A person in major impurity (janabah) must avoid five acts; a person in minor impurity (hadath) must avoid three; the distinction is rooted in Quran and Sunnah.

The Ruling of Istihadah and Wudu for the Excused
Ruling on istihadah and excuses for wudu with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istihadah, explain its legal effect on worship, and apply the rule that excused persons perform wudu for the time of each fard prayer.

Core Idea
Istihadah does not invalidate worship; the excused must make wudu for the time, not for each prayer.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi treatment of chronic bleeding (istihadah) and other continuous impurities. Unlike menstruation, istihadah does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations. The key is that the excused person performs wudu at the beginning of each fard prayer time, and that wudu remains valid for the entire time unless a non-excuse hadath occurs. This ruling is based on a hadith narrated by Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Istihadah is defined as blood from a burst vein, not from the womb, with no odor. Its ruling is like a continuous nosebleed: it does not prevent the obligation or validity of prayer, fasting, or intercourse.
2
Understand the category of excuse
The excused person includes those with istihadah, urine dribble (sals al-bawl), loose bowels, uncontrollable wind, non-stop bleeding from a wound, or continuous nosebleed. The condition: they cannot stop the flow without hardship.
3
State the wudu obligation
The excused person must perform wudu for the time of each fard prayer. This wudu is valid for the entire prayer time as long as no non-excuse hadath (e.g., intentional wind, touching with desire) occurs.
The wudu is for the time, not for each individual prayer (fard or nafl).
4
Locate and analyze the proof
The hadith 'توضئي لوقت كل صلاة' (perform wudu for the time of every prayer) is cited. The command indicates obligation, and 'لوقت' (for the time) ties the wudu to the time period, not to each prayer act.
This is a zanni dalil (ahad hadith), hence wudu is wajib, not fard.
5
Note the differences
Intra-Hanafi: some say inserting a cloth removes the excuse status for the mustahadah, others say no. Cross-madhhab: Shafi’is require wudu for every prayer act, not just per time.
The mufta bihi is that the excused performs wudu for the time and does not lose excuse by cloth insertion.

And touching the mushaf except with a covering. And the blood of istihadah is like a continuous nosebleed; it does not prevent prayer, nor fasting, nor intercourse. But there is a difference of opinion regarding the mustahadah if she does that: it is said she leaves the excuse status, and it is said she does not. And he must pray sitting with gestures if it flows due to inclination, because abandoning sujud is easier than praying with hadath. And it is not permissible for a person with uncontrollable flatulence to pray behind a person with urine dribbling, because the imam has both hadath and najasah, and he is a person with two excuses, while the follower has one excuse. And the excused person performs wudu for the time of every (fard) prayer, not for every prayer, fard or nafl. Rather, their wudu is for the time, so it remains as long as the time remains, as long as he does not commit a non-excuse hadath, due to his saying: 'The mustahadah performs wudu for the time of every prayer.' And there is no doubt that this is established by the Sunnah for every prayer, because it does not admit another interpretation, unlike the hadith 'Perform wudu for every prayer.' Commentary
The passage is a ruling_with_proof from the sharh. It begins with a repetition of the ruling regarding touching the mushaf (only with a covering). Then it defines istihadah (chronic vaginal bleeding outside of menstruation) as blood from a burst vein, not from the womb, characterized by having no odor. Its ruling is like a continuous nosebleed: it does not prevent the obligation or validity of prayer, fasting (both fard and nafl), nor does it prohibit intercourse. The text then mentions that the mustahadah and others with chronic excuses (like urine incontinence, loose bowels, uncontrollable flatulence, non-stop bleeding from a wound, continuous nosebleed) must perform wudu for the time of each fard prayer. The commentary elaborates: such a person must perform wudu at the beginning of the prayer time, and this wudu remains valid throughout that entire time as long as no non-excuse hadath occurs. If the excused person can stop the flow with a bandage or by sitting, they must do so and are no longer considered excused. There is a difference of opinion regarding the mustahadah: if she inserts a cloth, some say she loses her excuse status, others say not. If the flow is such that movement makes it worse, the person should pray sitting with gestures, since missing sujud is easier than praying with active hadath. Also, a person with uncontrollable flatulence cannot pray behind an imam with urine dribbling because the imam has two excuses (impurity and impurity) while the follower has only one. The proof for the wudu of the excused is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to Fatima bint Abi Hubaish: 'Perform wudu for the time of every prayer.' The commentator notes that this hadith clearly indicates wudu for each prayer time, not for every prayer act.

Key Terms
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Chronic vaginal bleeding outside the normal menstrual period, caused by a burst vein.
سلس بول (sals al-bawl): Urinary incontinence; repeatedly dripping urine that cannot be controlled.
استطلاق بطن (istitlāq baṭn): Chronic diarrhea or loose bowels.

Istihadah and chronic impurities do not invalidate worship; the excused perform wudu for the time of each fard prayer, based on the hadith 'توضئي لوقت كل صلاة'.

Rulings for Those with Chronic Excuses (Sahib al-‘Udhur)
Explaining Hanafi rulings for chronic excuses in purification and prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of istihadah and its ruling, explain when the wudu of a ma‘dhur becomes void according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and identify the condition for being considered excused.

Core Idea
Istihadah does not prevent worship; the excused person's wudu lasts the entire time and breaks only when the time exits.

In this lesson, we examine the legal framework for individuals with chronic, uncontrollable impurities (istihadah, urine drip, etc.). The Hanafi school provides a practical concession: such a person performs wudu once per fard prayer time and can pray multiple prayers with that wudu. This is based on the prophetic command to perform wudu 'for the time of every prayer.' We will unpack the conditions, the nullification rule, and the rationale behind the preference for Abu Hanifa's and Muhammad's position.

Guided Path
1
Define istihadah and differentiate it from hayd
Identify the definition (blood from a burst vein, not from the uterus, with no smell) and its ruling: does not prevent prayer, fasting, or intercourse.
Note the contrast with menstruation and postpartum bleeding.
2
Determine who is a ma‘dhur (excused person)
The condition: the excuse must encompass a full prayer time without interruption sufficient to perform wudu and prayer. If interruptible, the person is not excused.
Also note that if the person can stop the flow by bandage, plug, or by sitting, they must do so and cease to be excused.
3
Identify the core ruling: wudu per time
The excused person performs wudu for the time of each fard prayer, not for each individual prayer. This is based on the hadith.
The wudu remains valid for the entire time unless a different hadath occurs.
4
Analyze the dalil (proof)
The hadith commands wudu 'for the time of every prayer'. The commentator argues that 'prayer' here means its time, supported by linguistic usage (e.g., 'The prayer has a beginning and an end').
This establishes the muhkam (decisive) interpretation.
5
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on nullification
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: voids at exit of time. Abu Yusuf: at entry and exit. Zufar: at entry only. The relied-upon view is exit-only, because exit indicates the end of need and the appearance of the previous hadath.
Understand the reasoning: entry indicates need but does not nullify; only exit shows the need has passed.
6
Apply the condition for being excused
The excuse must be continuous. If there is an interruption sufficient for wudu and prayer during the entire time, the person is not excused.
This threshold determines who qualifies for the concession.

« أيما رَجُل أدركته الصَّلاةُ، فليُصَلَّ» (۳). ومن الثاني: آتيك لصلاة الظهر، أي لوقتها، فوجب حمل حديث ( توضنِي لَكُلِّ صلاة على المحكم، لأن اللام للوقت كما ذكرناه، وباقي أصحاب الأعذار في حكم المستحاضة، فالدليل يشملهم بصيرورة وضوئهم لوقت كل فرض يبقى ببقاء الوقت ما لم ينتقض بناقض غير العذر، فما دام الوقت باقياً ( ويُصَلُّونَ بِهِ)، أي: بوضوئهم فيه (مَا شَاؤوا مِنَ الفَرَائِضِ ) أداءً للتوقتية وقضاء لغيرها، (وَ) ما شاؤوا من ( النّوافِل ) والواجبات كالوتر والعيدين وكذا صلاة الجنازة، ومس
المصحف، وطواف بالكعبة. ويَبْطُلُ وُضُوءُ المَعْذُورِينَ) إذا لم يطرأ ناقض غــير الـعــذر (بخـروج الوَقْتِ)، كطلوع الشمس في الفجر، وهو قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وإضافة النقض إلى الخروج، إسناد مجازي، ليسهل على المتعلمين، وإلا فلا تأثير للخروج والدخول في النقض حقيقة، وإنما يظهر الحدث السابق عنده، كما ذكرناه في مضي مدة المسح، ولهذا لا يجوز لهم أن يمسحوا على الخفين بعدما خرج الوقت، وقد لبسوا أو توضؤوا مع العذر، ولا يجوز لهم البناء إذا خرج الوقت وهم في الصلاة، لأن جوازهما عرف نصاً في الحدث الطارئ لا في الحدث السابق، وبخروج الوقت يظهر الحدث السابق، لما عرف أن الوضوء إنما يرفع ما قبله من الحدث، ولا يرفع ما بعده وإنما يبطل بخروج الوقت (فقط) لا بدخوله فقط كالظهر كما قال به زفر ولا بكل منهما، كما قال أبو يوسف، لأنا نقول: إن الوقت قائم مقام الأداء ولا بُدَّ من تقديم الطهارة على الأداء، فيصح تقديمها علــى مـا يخلفه أيضاً؛ ليتمكن من جعل كل الوقت بالأداء مشغولاً، كما هو العزيمة، وخروج الوقت دلـيـل زوال الحاجة، ودخوله دلیل وجودها، فاعتبار ظهور الحدث عند الخروج أولى بإضافة النقض إليه، من إضافة النقض إلى دليل ثبوت الحاجة وهو الدخول، فلو توضأ لصلاة العيد، جاز له أن يصلي بــه الظهر على الصحيح، كما لو توضؤوا لصلاة ضحى، لأنه وقت مهمل، ولو توضؤوا في وقت الظهر للعصر، لا يصلون به العصر في الأصح؛ لأنها طهارة وقعت للظهر حتى لو ظهر فساد ظهرهم، جاز لهم أن يصلوها بها، فلا تبقى بعد خروجه. (ولا يصير معذوراً حتى يستوعبه العذرُ وقتاً كاملاً، لَيْسَ فِيهِ انْقِطَاعٌ) لعذره (بقَدْرِ الوضوء والصلاة)، إذ لو وُجِدَ لا يكــــــون معذوراً
And touching the Quran except with a cover. And the blood of istihadah is like a continuous nosebleed; it does not prevent prayer, nor fasting, nor intercourse. And the mustahadah performs wudu, and whoever has an excuse, such as dribbling urine or incontinence of the stomach, for the time of every fard... (And touching the Quran) even an ayah (except with a cover) due to His statement: "None touch it except the purified" as we explained. (And the blood of istihadah) and it is blood from a burst vein not from the uterus, and its sign is that it has no smell, and its ruling is (like a continuous nosebleed, it does not prevent prayer), meaning: it does not remove the obligation of prayer, nor does it prevent its validity with its condition which we will mention shortly. (Nor) does istihadah blood prevent (fasting) absolutely, whether obligatory or supererogatory, so it is valid while she is engaged in it absolutely. (Nor) does it prevent (intercourse), because it is not harmful, unlike menstruation and postnatal bleeding. Then he began to explain the purification of those with excuses, and the condition of their prayer with it, and the time of its nullification, and said: (And the mustahadah performs wudu) and she is: one whose blood falls short of three days, or exceeds ten in menstruation, or has not reached nine years, or has become menopausal, or exceeds forty in postnatal bleeding, or exceeds the habit and surpasses the maximum of menstruation and postnatal bleeding. And performs wudu (and whoever has an excuse: like dribbling urine, or incontinence of the stomach), and leakage of wind, and a wound whose blood does not stop, and continuous nosebleed and he cannot hold it without difficulty, so that if the excused person is able to stop the flow with a bandage or a plug, or if when he sits it does not flow, but when he stands it flows, he must stop it, and by that he ceases to be a person with an excuse, unlike the menstruating and postpartum woman if they prevent the flow, but there is disagreement about the mustahadah if she does that: it is said she ceases to be excused, and it is said not. And he must pray sitting with gestures if it flows due to leaning, because leaving prostration is easier than praying with impurity. And it is not permissible for one with flatus to pray behind one with dribbling urine, because the imam has impurity and najasah, and he is a person with two excuses, while the follower has one excuse. And the excused person performs wudu for the time of every (fard), not for every prayer, fard or nafl. Rather, their wudu is for the time, so it remains with its duration as long as he does not commit a hadath other than his excuse, due to his saying: "The mustahadah performs wudu for the time of every prayer." Narrated by Sibṭ ibn al-Jawzī from Abu Hanifa, and in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi: Abu Hanifa narrated from Hisham ibn Urwa from his father from Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh: "Perform wudu for the time of every prayer." And there is no doubt that this is decisive (muhkam) by the Sunnah for every prayer, because it admits no other interpretation, unlike the hadith "Perform wudu for every prayer" because the word "prayer" has become commonly used in the language of the Law and custom to mean its time. So from the first type is his saying: "Indeed prayer has a beginning and an end," i.e., its time. And his saying: "Any man whom the prayer overtakes, let him pray." And from the second: "I will come to you at the prayer of Dhuhr," i.e., its time. So it is necessary to interpret the hadith "Perform wudu for every prayer" in light of the decisive (muhkam), because the lam is for time as we mentioned. And the rest of those with excuses are in the same ruling as the mustahadah, so the evidence includes them in that their wudu is for the time of every fard, remaining with the duration of the time as long as it is not nullified by a nullifier other than the excuse. So as long as the time remains, (they pray with it) i.e., with their wudu, (whatever they wish of obligatory prayers) performance for those with time, and make-up for others, and whatever they wish of (supererogatory prayers) and obligatory like witr and the two Eids, and likewise funeral prayer, and touching the Quran, and circumambulation of the Kaaba. And the wudu of the excused persons becomes void if no nullifier other than the excuse occurs (by the exit of the time), like sunrise in Fajr, and this is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And attributing nullification to the exit is a metaphorical attribution, to make it easy for students; otherwise, exit and entry have no real effect on nullification; rather, the previous hadath appears at that time, as we mentioned regarding the passing of the duration of wiping. For this reason, they are not allowed to wipe over the khuffs after the time has exited, even if they wore them or performed wudu while excused, and they are not allowed to build (on their prayer) if the time exits while they are in prayer, because the permissibility of these is explicitly known for a new hadath, not for the previous hadath. And with the exit of time, the previous hadath appears, because it is known that wudu only removes what came before it of hadath, and does not remove what comes after it, and it is only nullified by the exit of time (only), not by its entry only like Dhuhr as Zufar said, nor by both, as Abu Yusuf said, because we say: The time stands in place of performance, and purification must precede performance, so it is valid to precede it also with what takes its place, so that one can occupy the entire time with performance, as is the determination. And the exit of time is evidence of the disappearance of need, while its entry is evidence of its existence. So considering the appearance of hadath at the exit time is more appropriate for attributing nullification to it than attributing nullification to the evidence of need (entry). So if he performs wudu for the Eid prayer, it is permissible for him to pray Dhuhr with it according to the correct view, just as if they performed wudu for the Duha prayer, because it is a neglected time. And if they perform wudu during the time of Dhuhr for Asr, they do not pray Asr with it according to the most correct view, because that purification occurred for Dhuhr, so that if their Dhuhr becomes invalid, they may pray it with it, so it does not remain after its exit. (And one does not become excused until the excuse encompasses a full time, in which there is no interruption) of his excuse (to the extent of wudu and prayer), because if there is, he is not excused.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings for those with chronic excuses (sahib al-‘udhr) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by restating the ruling on touching the Quran: it is prohibited without a cover, citing the verse "None touch it except the purified." Then it defines istihadah (non-menstrual vaginal bleeding) as blood from a burst vein, not from the uterus, having no smell. Its ruling is likened to a perpetual nosebleed: it does not prevent the obligation or validity of prayer, nor does it prevent fasting or intercourse, unlike menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The mustahadah is then defined: one whose bleeding is less than three days or exceeds ten (for menstruation), or who never reached nine years, or is menopausal, or exceeds forty days (postpartum), or exceeds her habit beyond the maximum limits. Those with other chronic excuses (urinary dribbling, stomach incontinence, flatus, wounds, continuous nosebleed) are included. The passage emphasizes that if the person can stop the flow with a bandage or plug, or by sitting, he must do so, and then is not considered excused. There is a difference of opinion on whether a mustahadah can cease to be excused by stopping the flow; the text mentions both views. It also addresses the case of one whose flow increases when leaning: he should pray sitting with gestures. It prohibits one with flatus from praying behind one with dribbling urine, due to the imam having two excuses (impurity and najasah) while the follower has one. The core ruling: the excused person performs wudu for the time of each fard prayer (not for every prayer), based on the hadith "The mustahadah performs wudu for the time of every prayer." The commentator (Marghinani?) explains that the word "prayer" in the hadith means its time, as shown by other textual evidence, thus wudu is valid for the entire time and remains until the time exits. With this wudu, they can pray any number of fard, nafl, and wajib prayers, and also touch the Quran and perform tawaf. The wudu is nullified only by the exit of the time (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), not by its entry (Zufar) nor by both (Abu Yusuf). The reasoning is that entry of time indicates need, but exit indicates the end of need, and the previous hadath becomes apparent at exit. Exceptions: wudu performed for Eid can be used for Dhuhr because Eid has no fixed time, but wudu performed during Dhuhr for Asr cannot be used for Asr because it is linked to Dhuhr. Finally, the condition for being considered excused: the excuse must encompass a complete time period without an interruption sufficient for wudu and prayer.

Key Terms
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Blood from a burst vein in the vagina, not from the uterus, having no smell, and not subject to the rulings of menstruation.
المعذور (al-ma‘dhur): One who has a chronic excuse (e.g., urine drip, gas, wound) that encompasses a full time without interruption sufficient for wudu and prayer.
سلس البول (sals al-bawl): Continuous or frequent dribbling of urine that cannot be controlled without extreme difficulty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the wudu of a person with a chronic excuse (ma‘dhur) become void?
A) With the entry of the time for the next prayer.
B) Only with the exit of the time for which wudu was performed.
C) With both entry and exit of time.
D) It never becomes void as long as the excuse persists.

Key Takeaway
A chronic excuse does not nullify worship; the excused person performs wudu per fard time, and it remains valid until that time exits.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Wudu of the Ma'dhur: Validity, Breakage, and Conditions
Ruling on validity of wudu for ma'dhur, breakage with time exit, conditions for becoming and ceasing to be ma'dhur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for becoming and ceasing to be a ma'dhur, explain when the wudu of a ma'dhur becomes invalid according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Zufar, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A ma'dhur can pray multiple prayers with one wudu, which only breaks when the prayer time exits — not on entry.

This lesson examines the special rulings for those with chronic excuses (ma'dhur) in the context of wudu and prayer. We will learn the precise conditions that define a ma'dhur, the validity and scope of their wudu, and the exact moment it becomes invalid. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether exit of time, entry, or both nullify the wudu, and understand the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the validity of wudu for a ma'dhur
The passage states that a ma'dhur can pray any number of obligatory and supererogatory prayers with a single wudu as long as no other nullifier occurs. Note the scope: it includes fard, nafl, witr, Eid, funeral prayer, touching the mushaf, and tawaf.
The wudu is valid for the entire time for which it was performed; it is not limited to one prayer.
2
Determine when the wudu breaks
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the wudu becomes invalid only with the exit of the time for which it was performed (e.g., sunrise for Fajr). The attribution of nullification to exit is metaphorical; actually the previous hadath appears at that moment.
The text says 'يَبْطُلُ وُضُوءُ المَعْذُورِينَ بخروج الوقت فقط'.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar held that wudu breaks with entry of the next prayer time; Abu Yusuf held that both entry and exit break it. Understand their proofs: Zufar argued that entry indicates a new obligation, Abu Yusuf saw both as indicators. The Hanafi response is that exit indicates removal of need, so it is more appropriate for nullification.
The text rejects Zufar and Abu Yusuf, affirming 'وهو قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد'.
4
Understand the conditions for becoming a ma'dhur
A person does not become a ma'dhur until the excuse covers them for a complete prayer time without an interruption sufficient to perform wudu and prayer. This is a condition for establishment (shart thubut).
The interruption must be longer than the time needed for wudu and prayer; a short break that does not prevent them is considered continuous.
5
Understand conditions for continuity and termination
For continuity of ma'dhur status, the excuse must appear in every subsequent time period, even once. For termination, a complete prayer time must be free from the excuse entirely. The condition for establishment is real or legal complete coverage.
Real coverage: continuous flow. Legal coverage: minor interruption that does not hinder purification and prayer.

(ولا يصير معذوراً حتى يستوعبه العذرُ وقتاً كاملاً، لَيْسَ فِيهِ انْقِطَاعٌ) لعذره (بقَدْرِ الوضوء والصلاة)، إذ لو وُجِدَ لا يكــــــون معذوراً،
وهذا شرط ثبوتِهِ، وشرط دوامِهِ: وجوده في كل وقت بعد ذلك، ولو مرة وشرط انقطاعِهِ، وخروج صاحبِهِ عَنْ كونِهِ مَعْدُورَاً: خُلُو وقتِ كاملٍ عَنْهُ.
(وَهَذَا الاستيعاب الحقيقي بالسيلان فيه جميعاً، أو الحكمي بالانقطاع القليل الذي لا يسي الطهارة والصلاة (شَرْطُ نُبُوتِهِ)، أي: العذر، (وَشَرْطُ دَوَامِهِ) أي: العذر، (وَجُودُهُ) أي: العذر (في كلّ وقت بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ) الاستيعاب، (وَلَو) وجده (مَرَّةً) ليعلم بها بقاؤه، (وشرط انقطاعه، وخُرُوحِ صاحبِهِ عَنْ كَونِهِ مَعْدُورَاً، خُلُو وقتٍ كامل عَنْهُ بانقطاعه حقيقة، وهو أن لا يراه فيه أصلاً.
And they may pray with it whatever they wish of obligatory and supererogatory prayers. And the wudu of those with chronic excuses becomes invalid only with the exit of the time. And a person does not become a ma'dhur until the excuse covers him for a complete time period, in which there is no interruption equal to the duration of wudu and prayer. ... From the first is His statement: 'Verily, prayer has a beginning and an end' i.e., for its time. And his statement: 'Any man whom prayer overtakes, let him pray.' From the second: I will come to you at the time of Zuhr prayer, i.e., for its time. So it became necessary to interpret the hadith 'Perform wudu for every prayer' as referring to the more definitive [usage], because the lām is for time as we mentioned. And the rest of those with excuses are in the ruling of the mustahadah, so the evidence includes them: their wudu becomes for the time of every fard, remaining with the remainder of the time as long as it is not nullified by a nullifier other than the excuse. So as long as the time remains, they may pray with it whatever they wish of obligatory prayers, as performance for those with specified times and as make-up for others, and whatever they wish of supererogatory prayers and obligatory acts like witr, the two Eids, and likewise the funeral prayer, touching the mushaf, and circumambulation of the Ka'bah. And the wudu of those with chronic excuses becomes invalid, if no nullifier other than the excuse occurs, with the exit of the time, such as sunrise for Fajr, and this is the saying of Abu Hanifah and Muhammad. And attributing nullification to exit is a metaphorical attribution, to make it easy for students, otherwise exit and entry have no real effect on nullification; rather, the previous hadath appears at that point, as we mentioned regarding the passing of the period of wiping. For this reason, it is not permissible for them to wipe over the khuffs after the time has exited, while they put them on or performed wudu during the excuse, and it is not permissible for them to build upon [the prayer] if the time exits while they are in prayer, because the permissibility of both is known textually regarding a fresh hadath, not a previous hadath. And with the exit of time, the previous hadath appears, because it is known that wudu only removes what preceded it of hadath, and does not remove what follows it; rather, it becomes invalid only with the exit of time, not with its entry alone like Zuhr as Zufar stated, nor with both, as Abu Yusuf stated. For we say: Time stands in place of performance, and purification must precede performance, so it is valid to precede it also to what substitutes for it, so that one may occupy the entire time with performance, as is the resolution. And the exit of time is evidence of the removal of need, and its entry is evidence of its existence, so considering the appearance of hadath at exit is more appropriate for attributing nullification to it than attributing nullification to the evidence of the establishment of need, which is entry. So if he performs wudu for the Eid prayer, it is permissible for him to pray Zuhr with it according to the correct view, just as if they performed wudu for the Duha prayer, because it is a time that is neglected. And if they perform wudu in the time of Zuhr for Asr, they may not pray Asr with it according to the most correct view, because that purification occurred for Zuhr, such that if their Zuhr turns out to be invalid, they may pray it with it, so it does not remain after its exit. (And a person does not become a ma'dhur until the excuse covers him for a complete time period, in which there is no interruption of his excuse equal to the duration of wudu and prayer, for if it occurs, he is not a ma'dhur. This is a condition for its establishment. And a condition for its continuity: its existence in every time after that, even once. And a condition for its termination and the exit of its owner from being a ma'dhur: a complete time being free from it. And this complete coverage, either real through continuous flow, or legal through a small interruption that does not prevent purification and prayer, is a condition for its establishment, i.e., of the excuse, and a condition for its continuity: its existence in every time after that, even once, so that its persistence is known. And a condition for its termination and the exit of its owner from being a ma'dhur: a complete time being free from it by its actual cessation, which is that he does not see it at all therein.)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, deals with the rulings of a ma'dhur (one with a chronic excuse such as continuous nosebleed or urinary incontinence). The passage first states that such a person may pray any number of obligatory (fard) and supererogatory (nafl) prayers with a single wudu, as long as no other nullifier occurs. Their wudu only breaks when the time for that prayer exits (e.g., sunrise for Fajr). The author explains that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Zufar held that wudu breaks with the entry of the time for the next prayer (e.g., entering Zuhr time breaks wudu made for Fajr), while Abu Yusuf held that both entry and exit break it. The author then provides the Hanafi proof: the hadith 'إن للصلاة أولاً وآخراً' (prayer has a beginning and an end) and the principle that time stands in place of performance, so purification must precede the time of performance. Since exit indicates removal of need, it is more appropriate to attribute nullification to exit than to entry. The text then discusses conditions for becoming a ma'dhur: the excuse must cover a complete prayer time without an interruption long enough to perform wudu and prayer. This is a condition for establishment. For continuity, the excuse must appear in every subsequent time period, even once. If a complete time passes without the excuse, the person ceases to be a ma'dhur. The passage also distinguishes between real coverage (continuous flow) and legal coverage (small interruption that does not impede purification and prayer).

Key Terms
مَعْذُور (ma'dhur): A person with a chronic excuse (e.g., incontinence, continuous nosebleed) that prevents complete purity.
وَقْت (waqt): The prescribed time period for a particular prayer (e.g., Zuhr from zawal to asr).
اسْتِيعَاب (isti'ab): Full coverage of a time period by the excuse without a break sufficient for wudu and prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), when does the wudu of a ma'dhur become invalid?
A) With the entry of the next prayer time
B) With the exit of the current prayer time
C) With both entry and exit
D) Only when a new nullifier occurs

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhur's wudu remains valid for multiple prayers within the same prayer time, breaking only when the time exits, not when a new time enters.

Conditions for the Excuse (Udh) in Continual Impurity
Explains the three conditions for the excuse (udhr)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for establishing, continuing, and terminating the excuse (udhr) in Hanafi fiqh and apply them to a scenario of continuous impurity.

Core Idea
An excuse is established when impurity covers most of one prayer time, continues if it occurs at least once in each subsequent time, and ends if a full time passes without any occurrence.

In Hanafi fiqh, a person suffering from a continuous impurity such as urinary incontinence is granted a legal excuse (udhr) that allows them to perform prayers with less rigorous purification. However, this status is strictly regulated. This lesson explains the precise conditions under which the excuse is established, what is required for it to persist, and what causes it to cease. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the ruling in daily worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status
The text defines the excuse (udhr) as a dispensation. Recognize that it is a rukhsah, not a wajib or fard, that allows worship despite impurity.
The excuse is a concession, not a requirement.
2
Condition for establishment (thubut)
The flow must cover most of one complete prayer time. Distinguish between actual coverage (isti'ab haqiqi) – continuous flow – and legal coverage (isti'ab hukmi) – small breaks that do not disrupt purification or prayer.
This is the threshold that starts the excuse.
3
Condition for continuation (dawam)
After establishment, the excuse continues if the flow occurs at least once in each subsequent prayer time. Even one occurrence suffices to maintain the status.
Continuation does not require coverage of most of each time.
4
Condition for termination (inqita')
The excuse ends when a complete prayer time passes without any flow. This means the person does not see any impurity from the start to the end of a time.
The entire time must be free; a break is not enough.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a specific case of continuous impurity, walk through the three conditions to determine whether the person is excused for a given prayer time.
Always check establishment first, then continuation, then possible termination.

And this is the condition for its establishment, and the condition for its continuation: its existence in every time after that, even once. And the condition for its termination and the exit of its possessor from being excused: a complete time free from it, with its cessation in reality, which is that he does not see it at all in that time.

Commentary
This passage, from the sharh of Nur al-Idah, explains the conditions for a person to be considered excused (ma'dhur) due to a continuous impurity like urinary incontinence or chronic flow. The excuse is established (thubut) when the flow covers most of a prayer time, either actually by continuous flow (isti'ab haqiqi) or legally by small breaks that do not disrupt purification and prayer (isti'ab hukmi). For the excuse to continue (dawam), it suffices that the flow occurs at least once in each subsequent time. The excuse ends and the person is no longer excused when a complete time slot passes without any occurrence of the flow, meaning the person does not see it at all in that time. This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh regarding the definition of an excuse in matters of purification.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A legal excuse that permits a person to perform acts of worship despite a continuous impurity, such as urinary incontinence or chronic nosebleed.
استيعاب (isti'ab): The condition that the flow covers most of a prayer time, either actually (continuous flow) or legally (small interruptions that do not affect prayer/purification).

Key Takeaway
The excuse (udhr) in Hanafi fiqh is a dispensation governed by three precise conditions: coverage of most of a time for establishment, at least one occurrence per subsequent time for continuation, and a complete time free from flow for termination.

Definition and Ruling on Impurities (Najasah)
Defines impurity and distinguishes real vs. legal, then introduces the obligation to remove it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real impurity (najis) and legal impurity (hadath) and state the obligation of removing the preventing impurity.

Core Idea
Removing impurity that prevents prayer is fard; impurities are classified into heavy and light.

In this lesson, we begin with the classification of impurities (najasah) in the Hanafi school. The text explains the linguistic distinction between 'najas' and 'najis', then establishes the fard ruling for removing impurities that invalidate prayer. This foundational lesson sets the stage for understanding what qualifies as excusable impurity and how to purify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of impurity
Distinguish between hadath (legal impurity, requiring wudu/ghusl) and khubth (real impurity, tangible filth). The passage uses 'khubth' for real and 'hadath' for legal.
This distinction is essential for understanding different purification rules.
2
Analyze the linguistic usage
Note that 'najas' (with fatha) is a noun for intrinsic impurity, while 'najis' (with kasra) is an adjective for both intrinsic and accidental impurity. The passage emphasizes that one does not say 'najas' for an accidentally impure garment.
This precise language helps avoid confusion in fiqh texts.
3
State the ruling on removal
The passage explicitly states: 'وإزالة النجاسة المانعة فرض' — removing the impurity that prevents prayer is fard (obligatory). It provides a hadith as evidence, emphasizing the imperative to avoid urine.
The ruling applies to body, clothing, and place of prayer.
4
Begin the classification
The passage starts dividing real impurity into heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah), but the page cuts off. The classification determines how much impurity is excused and how to purify.
This division is unique to the Hanafi school and impacts daily practice.

خبث (khubth): Real impurity (najis) that is tangible, as opposed to hadath (legal impurity).
حدث (hadath): Legal impurity that requires wudu or ghusl, as opposed to real impurity (najis).

Definition and Ruling on Impurity (Najis)
Defines impurity, distinguishes grammatical forms, states obligation of removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'najis' in Hanafi law, distinguish between 'najas' (essential) and 'najis' (acquired) in usage, and state the obligation of removing impurity from body, garment, and place for prayer.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'najas' denotes essential impurity; removal of all impurity from body/clothing/place is fard for prayer.

This lesson opens the chapter on real impurities (najāsāt). After covering legal impurity (hadath), the author now turns to physical, tangible impurities. We will learn the precise Arabic terminology—how 'najas' (with fatha) and 'najis' (with kasra) are used—and the fundamental obligation to remove impurity before prayer, with its concession when no remover is available.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish real vs. legal impurity
Identify the two categories: legal impurity (hadath) which requires wudu/ghusl, and real impurity (khabath/najāsa haqīqiyya) which is physical filth. The passage explains the author first discussed legal impurity, then turns to real impurity.
In Hanafi terminology, 'khabath' is reserved for real impurity, while 'hadath' is for legal.
2
Analyze the grammatical distinction
Note that 'najas' (fatha) is a noun used only for substances that are inherently impure (e.g., pig, blood), while 'najis' (kasra) is an adjective used for both essential and acquired impurity (e.g., a soiled garment). The passage gives explicit examples.
This distinction has legal implications: for acquired impurity, you may simply say 'najis', not 'najas'.
3
Identify the ruling on removal
The passage states that removing impurity that prevents prayer is fard (obligatory) from the body, garment, and place of prayer. However, this obligation falls away if one cannot find a remover (e.g., water) due to a valid excuse.
The ruling is fard, not sunnah, meaning prayer without removal is invalid if possible.
4

The passage begins to divide real impurity into heavy and light types, but only introduces the first (heavy). This will be detailed in subsequent lessons.
The criterion for division is the amount excused.

Chapter of Impurities and Purification from Them. When he finished explaining the legal impurity (hadath) and purification from it, he began to explain the real (physical) impurity, its remover, its division, the amount that is excused from it, and the method of purifying its location. He gave precedence to the first (legal impurity) because the restriction of the conditioned act (prayer) remains as long as some of the substance remains, even if small, without the remover reaching it, except in rare cases, and no ruling applies to it— such as a person with a severed limb and a wounded face— unlike the second (real impurity), for a small amount of it is excused, even a large amount due to necessity, as you will learn. 'Anjās' is the plural of 'najis' (with two fathas): a name for a substance considered filthy by law; originally a verbal noun, then used as a noun. Allah says: 'Indeed the polytheists are impure' (Quran 9:28). It applies to both real and legal impurity. 'Khabath' is specific to real impurity, and 'hadath' to legal impurity. 'Najas' (with fatha) is a noun and does not take the feminine suffix; 'najis' (with kasra) is an adjective and takes the feminine suffix. The first is used exclusively for essential impurity, not for something that acquires impurity, except hyperbolically. The second is used for both essential and accidental impurity, so it is more general. For example, feces is called 'najas' (with fatha) and 'najisa' (with kasra); the pig is called 'najis' (with fatha and kasra). One does not say of a garment that has been touched by impurity 'najas' (with fatha), but only 'najis' (with kasra). Removing impurity that prevents (prayer) is obligatory (fard) from the body, place, and garment, and it is excused if there is an excuse of not finding a remover. 'Tathīr' (purification) is either establishing purity or removing impurity. Each requires that impurity be established in the location, whether the impurity was legal or real, to avoid establishing what already exists or removing what has been removed. If 'tathīr' is interpreted as removal, that is good; if interpreted as establishing purity, the meaning is purifying the location from impurity. Purification is given precedence because it is among the most important matters. It has been reported that the first thing a servant is asked about in his grave is purification, and that most punishment of the grave is due to lack of care for it and guarding against it, especially urine. He began its division and said: 'Real impurity divides into two types: one type is heavy impurity' due to the small amount excused.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on physical impurities (najāsāt). The commentary explains that after discussing legal impurity (hadath), the author now turns to real, tangible impurities. The term 'najas' is defined linguistically and legally: it is a substance deemed filthy by the Sharia. The author distinguishes between the forms 'najas' (fatha) for essential impurity and 'najis' (kasra) for accidental or descriptive impurity. This distinction is unique to Arabic grammar but has legal implications: essential impurities (like blood) are always called 'najas', while acquired impurities (like a garment touched by urine) are described as 'najis'. The removal of impurity that prevents prayer is fard (obligatory) from body, clothing, and place of prayer, unless one cannot find water or another purifier (a concession for the excused). The passage then introduces the division of impurity into heavy and light, which the next lesson will detail.

Key Terms
نجاسة حقيقية (najāsa haqīqiyya): Physical impurity that can be sensed by the senses, as opposed to legal impurity (hadath).
نجاسة حكمية (najāsa hukmiyya): Legal impurity (hadath) that necessitates purification (wudu or ghusl).
خبث (khabath): Real impurity, used synonymously with najāsa haqīqiyya.
حدث (hadath): Legal impurity that invalidates wudu or necessitates ghusl.
نجس بالفتح (najas (with fatha)): A noun used for something inherently impure (e.g., pig, blood).
نجس بالكسر (najis (with kasra)): An adjective describing something that has become impure (e.g., a soiled garment).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which form of 'najis' is used for a garment that has been touched by impurity?
A) 'Najas' with fatha
B) 'Najis' with kasra
C) Either form is acceptable
D) Neither, a different term is used

Key Takeaway
The term 'najis' has two forms: 'najas' for essential impurity, 'najis' for acquired; its removal is obligatory for prayer.

Divisions of Impurity: Heavy and Light with Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Classifies real impurity into heavy and light, with examples and ikhtilaf on criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi criterion for classifying impurities as heavy or light and apply it to cases like animal dung, including the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An impurity's class depends on whether its proof is uncontradicted (heavy) or subject to textual conflict (light).

Impurities are not all the same in Islamic law. The Hanafi school distinguishes between heavy (najāsa ghalīẓa) and light (najāsa khafīfa) impurities, based on the quantity that is excused during acts of worship. This passage from Imdād al-Fattāḥ explains the classification and the internal debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples over the correct criterion for sorting impurities like animal dung.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories and their practical effect
The passage defines heavy impurity by a small excused amount and light impurity by a larger excused amount. Note: The difference is only in the amount forgiven, not in purification method.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's criterion
Imam Abu Hanifa bases the classification on the strength of textual evidence: if a text establishes impurity without any conflicting text, it is heavy; if there are conflicting texts (one for impurity, one for purity), it becomes light. The example of urine of edible animals illustrates this.
The hadith of 'Uranis is considered a conflicting text, but Abu Hanifa did not definitively abrogate it.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's criterion
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani instead rely on whether the topic allows ijtihad (scholarly reasoning). If scholars have differed on its purity, it is considered light, because ijtihad is a valid evidence.
This leads to different rulings on animal dung.
4
Apply to the case of dung
Trace how each view classifies dung: Abu Hanifa calls it heavy (no conflicting text), while the Sahibayn call it light (due to the ijtihad of Malik and general affliction).
The mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
The difference affects how much dung is excused in prayer. Under Abu Hanifa, a small amount (less than a dirham) is excused; under the Sahibayn, up to one-fourth of the garment is excused. Later Hanafi fatwa relies on the lighter view due to necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْقَسِمُ النَّجَاسَةُ الحقيقية ( إلى قسمين: أحدهما نجاسة (غليظة) باعتبار قلة المعفو عنه منها، لا في كيفية تطهيرها، إذ لا يختلف بالغلظ والخفة. (وَ) القسم الثاني: نجاسة (خفيفة) باعتبار كثرة المعفو عنه منها، بما ليس في المغلظة، لا في التطهير وإصابة المائعات والماء، إذ لا يختلف حكم تنجيسها بهما. واعلم أنهم اختلفوا فيما يثبت به الغلظ والخفة، فقال أبــو حنيفة رحمه الله : ما ثبتت نجاسته بنص لم يعارضه نص آخر يخالفه فهو غليظ، كالدم المسفوح ونحوه، وما تعارض فيه نصان، أحدهما في نجاسته، والآخر في طهارته، فهو خفيف، والأخذ بدليـل النجاسة فيه أولى، لوجود المرجح مثل بول مأكول اللحم، فإنَّ قولـه عـلـيـه الصـلاة والسلام: استنزهُوا مِنَ البَوْلِ » (۱) يدل على نجاسته وخبر العرنيين (۳) يدل على الطهارة، لأمرهم بشرب أبوال الإبل وألبانها، فخف حكمه للتعارض، فإن قيل : كيف يتحقق تعارض النصين وحديث العرنيين (۳) منسوخ عنده؟ قلنا: إنما قال ذلك اجتهاداً ورأياً ولم يقطع بـه، فتكون صورة التعــارض قائمة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : ما ساغ الاجتهاد في طهارته فهو مخفف، لأن الاجتهاد حجة في وجوب العمل به، وثمرة الاختلاف تظهر في حكم الروث والخشي، والبعر ) ونحوها. فعند أبـي حنيفة: مغلظة؛ لأن ما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام من أنه ألقى الروثة، وقال: « إِنَّهَا رِجْسٌ ) لم يعارضه نص آخر. ولا اعتبار عنده بالبلوى في موضع النص، كما في بول الآدمي، فإنَّ الـبـلـوى فـيـه أعم، وعندهما: مخففة؛ لاختلاف العلماء، فإن الإمام مالك: يرى طهارتها لعموم البلوى، لامتلاء الطرق بها، وإذا علمت ذلك، (فَالغَلِيظَةُ: كَالخَمْرِ)، وهي: التي من ماء العنب، إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد (والدَّمِ المَسْفُوحِ) لا الباقي في اللحم المهزول والسمين، ولا الباقي في عروق المذكى
Real impurity divides into two types: one is heavy impurity, due to the small amount excused from it, not in the manner of its purification, for that does not differ by heaviness or lightness. And the second type is light impurity, due to the large amount excused from it, beyond what is excused from heavy impurity, not in purification or contact with liquids and water, for the ruling of making them impure does not differ by these two. Know that they differed on what establishes heaviness and lightness. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whatever impurity is established by a text (nass) that is not contradicted by another text opposing it is heavy, like flowing blood and the like. Whatever is contradicted by two texts, one indicating its impurity and the other its purity, is light, and acting on the evidence of impurity is preferable due to a preponderant factor— like the urine of an animal whose meat is eaten, for the Prophet's saying 'Guard yourselves from urine' indicates its impurity, and the hadith of the 'Uranis indicates purity, as he ordered them to drink camel urine and milk. So its ruling became light due to contradiction. If it is said: How can there be contradiction between the two texts when the hadith of the 'Uranis is abrogated (mansukh) according to him? We say: He only said that by ijtihad and opinion, and did not state it definitively, so the appearance of contradiction remains. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Whatever allows ijtihad regarding its purity is light, because ijtihad is a valid proof that necessitates acting by it. The fruit of the difference appears in the ruling on dung (rawth, khathī, ba'r) and the like. According to Abu Hanifa: they are heavy, because the hadith that he (peace be upon him) threw a dung pellet and said 'It is filth' (rijs) was not contradicted by another text. And according to him (Abu Hanifa), the prevalence of affliction (balwā) is not considered when there is a text, as in the case of human urine, for the affliction is more general. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): they are light, due to the difference of scholars, for Imam Malik considers them pure due to general affliction, as roads are filled with them. When you know this, (the heavy is like wine) which is from grape juice when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam, and (flowing blood) not the blood remaining in lean or fat meat, nor the blood remaining in the veins of a properly slaughtered animal.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the division of real impurity into heavy and light categories, focusing on the amount excused. Heavy impurities have a smaller forgiven quantity (e.g., a dirham's size for solid impurities), while light ones have a larger forgiven quantity (e.g., one-fourth of the garment). The author then explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on the criterion for classification. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that heavy impurities are those proven by a clear text without any conflicting text indicating purity; if there are conflicting texts, the impurity becomes light. For example, the urine of an animal whose meat is eaten: the hadith 'Guard yourselves from urine' suggests impurity, but the hadith of the 'Uranis (who were ordered to drink camel urine for medicine) suggests purity. Since there is apparent contradiction, the impurity is light. A possible objection (that the 'Urani hadith is abrogated) is dismissed because Abu Hanifa did not definitively abrogate it; he only considered it by ijtihad. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) base the division on whether the impurity is subject to ijtihad among scholars; if there is ikhtilaf, it is light. The practical difference appears in the ruling on animal dung (like horse, donkey, camel droppings). Abu Hanifa considers them heavy because the hadith 'It is filth' is uncontradicted. The companions consider them light due to the ijtihad of Imam Malik and general necessity (since streets are full of them). The passage then lists examples of heavy impurities: wine (khamr) defined as fermented grape juice, and flowing blood (not residual blood in meat or veins).

Key Terms
نجاسة غليظة (najāsa ghalīẓa): Heavy impurity: small amount excused (less than a dirham). Examples: wine, flowing blood.
نجاسة خفيفة (najāsa khafīfa): Light impurity: larger amount excused (up to one-fourth of garment). Examples: urine of edible animals, dung.
دم مسفوح (dam masfūḥ): Flowing blood, i.e., blood that gushes out, not residual blood in meat or veins.
خمر (khamr): Intoxicating drink from grape juice that ferments and becomes strong.
روث (rawth): Dung of hoofed animals like horses, donkeys; included in the ikhtilaf between imams.
بلوى (balwā): General affliction or widespread necessity; used as evidence to classify dung as light.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the urine of an edible animal considered light impurity?
A) Because it is less dirty than other urines
B) Because there are conflicting texts, one showing impurity and another showing purity
C) Because scholars differed on it
D) Because of general affliction

Key Takeaway
Animal dung is considered a light impurity in the Hanafi school according to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, so up to one-fourth of a garment is excused.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Classification of Heavy and Light Impurities
Explaining the distinction between heavy and light impurities based on textual evidence and scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for heavy vs. light impurities, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Heavy impurities are established by an uncontested text; light impurities arise from conflicting texts or scholarly disagreement.

This lesson delves into a unique Hanafi distinction: impurities are not all equal. The school divides them into heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah), a classification that affects the amount excused in prayer. The passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the underlying criterion and highlights an internal Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying the rulings in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Purpose of Classification
Understand that the heavy/light distinction only affects the amount of impurity that is excused in prayer (ma'fu 'anhu). It does not change the method of purification or the ruling of contaminating other things.
Common mistake: thinking the classification affects purification rules.
2
Abu Hanifa's Textual Criterion
Learn Abu Hanifa's rule: if a single, uncontested text (nass) establishes the impurity, it is heavy (ghaliz). If two texts conflict—one indicating impurity, the other purity—it is light (khafif).
The conflict itself causes the light ruling, even if the impurity evidence is preferred.
3
Example: Urine of Meat-Eating Animals
Analyze the example of the urine of an animal whose meat is eaten. The hadith 'Guard yourselves from urine' suggests impurity, but the 'Urayniyyin hadith allows drinking camel urine, suggesting purity. Abu Hanifa says this conflict makes the urine light.
Although Abu Hanifa believes the 'Urayniyyin hadith is abrogated, he does not assert this definitively, so the conflict remains.
4
Sahibayn's Ijtihad Criterion
Understand the Sahibayn's alternative criterion: if ijtihad (scholarly reasoning) is possible regarding the purity of a substance, then it is light. They consider the existence of scholarly disagreement as a basis for leniency.
This leads to a different classification for some impurities like animal dung.
5
Practical Difference: Animal Dung
Apply the two criteria to animal dung. Abu Hanifa calls it heavy because a hadith says 'It is rijs' with no conflicting text. The Sahibayn call it light because scholars like Imam Malik deem it pure due to widespread difficulty (balwa).
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.
6
Memorize the Examples
Heavy: wine, poured-out blood, dead animal meat/hide, urine of non-edible animals, dog feces, predator droppings/saliva, poultry droppings, and anything that breaks wudu. Light: horse urine.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive.

Source Text

(وَ) القسم الثاني: نجاسة (خفيفة) باعتبار كثرة المعفو عنه منها، بما ليس في المغلظة، لا في التطهير وإصابة المائعات والماء، إذ لا يختلف حكم تنجيسها بهما. واعلم أنهم اختلفوا فيما يثبت به الغلظ والخفة، فقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : ما ثبتت نجاسته بنص لم يعارضه نص آخر يخالفه فهو غليظ، كالدم المسفوح ونحوه، وما تعارض فيه نصان، أحدهما في نجاسته، والآخر في طهارته، فهو خفيف، والأخذ بدليل النجاسة فيه أولى، لوجود المرجح مثل بول مأكول اللحم، فإنَّ قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: استنزهُوا مِنَ البَوْلِ يدل على نجاسته وخبر العرنيين يدل على الطهارة، لأمرهم بشرب أبوال الإبل وألبانها، فخف حكمه للتعارض، فإن قيل : كيف يتحقق تعارض النصين وحديث العرنيين منسوخ عنده؟ قلنا: إنما قال ذلك اجتهاداً ورأياً ولم يقطع به، فتكون صورة التعارض قائمة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : ما ساغ الاجتهاد في طهارته فهو مخفف، لأن الاجتهاد حجة في وجوب العمل به، وثمرة الاختلاف تظهر في حكم الروث والخشي، والبعر ونحوها. فعند أبي حنيفة: مغلظة؛ لأن ما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام من أنه ألقى الروثة، وقال: « إِنَّهَا رِجْسٌ ) لم يعارضه نص آخر. ولا اعتبار عنده بالبلوى في موضع النص، كما في بول الآدمي، فإنَّ البلوى فيه أعم، وعندهما: مخففة؛ لاختلاف العلماء، فإن الإمام مالك: يرى طهارتها لعموم البلوى، لامتلاء الطرق بها، وإذا علمت ذلك، (فَالغَلِيظَةُ: كَالخَمْرِ)، وهي: التي من ماء العنب، إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد (والدَّمِ المَسْفُوحِ) لا الباقي في اللحم المهزول والسمين، ولا الباقي في عروق المذكى، ولحم الميتة، وإهابها، وبول ما لا يؤكل لحمه، ونَجْوِ الكلب، ورجيع السباع، ولعابها، وخرء الدجاج، والبَط، والإوز، وما ينقض الوضوء بخروجه من بدن الإنسان. وأمّا الخفيفةُ: فكبولِ الفَرَس.
And light. So the heavy is like wine, and poured-out blood, what is excused from them is not in the manner of their purification, for it does not differ by heaviness and lightness. (And) the second category: a (light) impurity, by consideration of the abundance of what is excused from it, not by what is in the heavy, not in purification and not in striking liquids and water, for the ruling of its rendering impure does not differ by them. Know that they differed on what establishes heaviness and lightness. Abu Hanifa said: What establishes its impurity by a text that is not opposed by another text that contradicts it is heavy, like poured-out blood and the like. And what two texts conflict over, one regarding its impurity and the other regarding its purity, it is light, and acting on the evidence of impurity is preferable due to the existence of a preferred factor, like the urine of a meat-eating animal, for his statement (peace be upon him): 'Guard yourselves from urine' indicates its impurity, and the report of the 'Urayniyyin indicates purity, because he ordered them to drink camel urine and milk, so its ruling became light due to the contradiction. If it is said: How can the contradiction of the two texts be realized when the hadith of the 'Urayniyyin is abrogated according to him? We say: He only said that through ijtihad and opinion, and he did not definitively declare it, so the form of contradiction remains. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Whatever ijtihad is permissible regarding its purity is light, because ijtihad is an evidence mandating acting upon it. And the fruit of the difference appears in the ruling of dung, dried dung, and animal droppings and the like. According to Abu Hanifa: heavy, because what was reported from him (peace be upon him) that he threw away the dung and said: 'It is filth' was not opposed by another text. And he does not consider the common difficulty in the place of a text, as in human urine, for the common difficulty is greater. And according to the two: light, due to the difference of scholars, for Imam Malik considers it pure due to widespread difficulty, because the paths are full of it. And when you know that, (then the heavy: like wine), which is that which is from grape water when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam, and (poured-out blood) not the remaining in lean and fatty meat, nor the remaining in the veins of the slaughtered animal, and meat of a dead animal, and its hide, and urine of an animal whose meat is not eaten, and dog feces, and predator droppings, and their saliva, and chicken droppings, and duck, and goose, and whatever breaks wudu by exiting from the human body. And the light: like horse urine.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, discusses the Hanafi classification of impurities (najasah) into heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah). The author begins by noting that this distinction affects the amount that is excused (ma'fu 'anhu) in prayer, not the method of purification or the ruling of contamination. He then explains the disagreement among Hanafi scholars on what determines these categories. Abu Hanifa bases the classification on the textual evidence: if a single, uncontested text establishes the impurity, it is heavy; if two conflicting texts exist (one for impurity, one for purity), it is light. The example is urine of an animal whose meat is eaten: a hadith commands guarding against urine (implying impurity), while another hadith (the 'Urayniyyin incident) permits drinking camel urine, implying purity. Abu Hanifa resolves the conflict by preferring the impurity evidence, but the contradiction itself makes the urine light. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) use a different criterion: if ijtihad is possible regarding its purity (i.e., scholars disagree), it is light. They consider the difference of opinion as a basis for leniency. A practical difference is seen in the ruling of animal dung: Abu Hanifa deems it heavy because a hadith calls it 'rijs' without contradiction, while the Sahibayn consider it light because scholars like Malik deem it pure due to general difficulty. The passage then lists the heavy impurities: wine, poured-out blood, meat of dead animals, hides of dead animals, urine of animals not eaten, dog feces, predator droppings and saliva, droppings of poultry like chickens, ducks, geese, and anything that breaks wudu when exiting the body (like flowing blood, semen, etc.). Light impurities are exemplified by horse urine, because there are conflicting texts on eating horse meat, leading to a lighter ruling.

Key Terms
غليظ (ghaliz): Heavy impurity: established by an uncontested text according to Abu Hanifa; its najasah is unambiguous.
خفيف (khafif): Light impurity: established by conflicting texts or where ijtihad is permissible; greater amount is excused in prayer.
نجاسة (najasah): Impurity, ritual uncleanliness; categorized into heavy and light in this passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what makes an impurity light (khafif)?
A) When the impurity is small in size
B) When two conflicting texts exist about its impurity and purity
C) When scholars have ijma' on its impurity
D) When the impurity is from an animal whose meat is eaten

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi heavy/light classification hinges on textual conflict or scholarly disagreement, affecting the excused amount in prayer, not the purification method.
