Heavy and Light Impurities in Taharah
Base text listing categories of heavy and light najasah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah) impurities as classified in the Hanafi school and identify the specific items listed in the matn.

Core Idea
Heavy impurities require complete washing; light impurities have flexible purification rules.

This passage from Nur al-Idah provides a concise yet comprehensive list of najasat categories. Knowing these distinctions is foundational for taharah, as the ruling on how to purify an object depends on whether the impurity is heavy or light. The matn serves as a memorization anchor for the student.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognise that the matn first lists heavy impurities (al-ghalizah) and then mentions light impurities (al-khafifah) after the phrase 'As for the light [impurity]'.
Heavy impurities are explicitly named; light impurities are illustrated by one example.
2
List each heavy impurity item
Extract each item: carrion meat, carrion skin (before tanning), urine of animals whose meat is not eaten, dog dung, feces of predators, saliva of predators, droppings of chicken, duck, goose, and any substance that breaks wudu when exiting the human body.
Some items have specific terminology: 'najw' for dog dung, 'raji'' for predator feces.
3
Note the light impurity example
The only example given for light impurity is horse urine. Recognise that this is not an exhaustive list; other light impurities are covered elsewhere.
Light impurities are less severe; they can often be purified by simply pouring water over the affected area without scrubbing.
4
Understand the practical implication
Heavy impurities require the removal of the substance and thorough washing of the affected area (e.g., if on clothing, the garment must be washed until the impurity is gone). Light impurities are more lenient – for example, horse urine can be rinsed briefly.
The distinction affects rulings for salat and handling of objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولحم المَيْتَةِ، وإهابها، وبول ما لا يُؤكل لحمه، ونَجْوِ الكلب، ورجيع السباع، ولعابها، وخرء الدجاج، والبَط، والإوز، وما ينقض الوضوء بخروجه من بدنِ الإنسان. وأمّا الخفيفةُ: فكبولِ الفَرَسِ .
And the meat of carrion, and its skin, and the urine of an animal whose meat is not eaten, and the dung of the dog, and the feces of predators, and their saliva, and the droppings of chicken, duck, goose, and that which breaks wudu when it exits from the human body. As for the light [impurity]: such as the urine of a horse.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage from the matn of Nur al-Idah enumerates the categories of heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah) impurities in the Hanafi school. The heavy impurities include carrion meat and skin before tanning, urine of impermissible animals, dog dung, predator feces and saliva, droppings of fowl (chicken, duck, goose), and bodily substances that require wudu renewal. The light impurity mentioned here is horse urine. This serves as a memorization anchor; the commentary will expand each item.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): The skin of a dead animal before tanning.
نَجْو (najw): Dung, specifically of a dog.
رجيع (raji'): Feces of predators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school divides najasah into heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah); the matn provides an essential list that includes carrion meat, skin, urine of non-edible animals, dog dung, predator feces/saliva, and certain bird droppings as heavy, and horse urine as light.

Heavy and Light Impurities: Definitions, Proofs, and Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Detailed expansion of each impurity category with proofs and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi classification of heavy and light impurities, identify the Quranic proof for heavy impurities, and explain the disputes over horse urine, edible animal dung, and inedible bird droppings.

Core Idea
Impurity rulings are based on textual proof and necessity: heavy impurities have clear texts, light impurities involve conflicts or widespread difficulty.

Understanding what is considered impure (najis) and to what degree is essential for valid worship, especially prayer. The Hanafi school distinguishes between heavy impurities (najasah ghalizah) and light impurities (najasah khafifah), each with different implications for purification and the validity of salah. This lesson unpacks the classifications, proofs, and key disagreements within the school, grounding you in both the rulings and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Categories
The passage divides impurities into heavy and light. Understand the general criteria: heavy impurities have clear textual evidence (Quran or consensus), while light impurities involve conflicting evidence or hardship (umum al-balwa).
Heavy: blood, carrion, dog/pig feces, etc. Light: horse urine, edible animal dung (disputed), etc.
2
Analyze the Proof for Heavy Impurities
The Quranic verse 7:157 ("And He prohibits for them the impure things") is cited as the primary proof. The word 'al-khaba'ith' is general, and prohibition implies impurity. Also, sound nature repels them. Note that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree on heavy impurities because no ijtihad is possible in their purity.
The verse covers all heavy impurities listed, including blood, carrion, urine of non-edible animals, etc.
3
Examine the Light Impurity of Horse Urine
Horse urine is the quintessential light impurity. Conflicting hadiths on horse meat create a middle ground: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule it light impurity; Muhammad rules it pure. The fatwa follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (light). Note the reasoning: the conflict in texts prevents a ruling of heaviness or purity.
Thamarah al-khilaf: prayer with horse urine is valid but makruh if significant according to the fatwa view.
4
Follow the Dispute on Edible Animal Dung/Urine
For camels, cows, sheep, etc., Abu Hanifa holds their dung/urine as heavy impurity because no conflicting texts exist. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule it light, influenced by Imam Malik's view of purity and the widespread necessity (umum al-balwa) as roads and inns fill with dung. Muhammad later declared it pure. The fatwa is on lightness.
The principle of umum al-balwa is key: widespread hardship lightens the ruling.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi Positions for All Cases
Consolidate: horse urine = light (fatwa on Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Edible animal dung/urine = light (fatwa on Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Inedible bird droppings = light in the most correct from Abu Hanifa (due to necessity and prevalence). Fish blood = pure (Zahir al-Riwayah, as it is not real blood).
These mufta bihi rulings are practical for daily fiqh and fatwa.

(۲) بفتح أوله وتشديد ثانيه، مدينة مشهورة من أمهات البلاد وأعلام المدن وهي محط الحاج على طريق السابلة، وبينها وبين نيسابور مائة وستون فرسخاً. ا.هـ معجم البلدان / ري /.
Heavy impurity: (and not blood of liver and spleen) and heart, and blood that has not flowed from its place in a human, and blood of bedbugs, fleas, and lice even if plentiful, and blood of fish, and blood of the martyr in his own right but not for others, such that if a person carries him and prays, and does not have on him more than the size of a dirham of his blood, his prayer is valid. (And meat of carrion) that has blood; so fish, locusts, and what has no flowing blood are excluded. (And its skin) meaning the skin of the dead animal before tanning, and urine of what is not eaten (like donkey, hyena, human, even a nursing infant), and it includes the urine of a bat because it is not eaten, but it does not spoil water due to inability to avoid it; and urine of cat and mouse and their droppings are impure in the most apparent narrations, spoiling water, but if mouse droppings are ground into wheat and not apparent, it does not harm due to necessity. If a mouse urinates on clothes, it is said: it becomes impure by analogy to water; and it is said: it does not become impure because clothes cannot be guarded from it as it may urinate from above, unlike a utensil because it is covered. And in Sharh al-Mukhtar: similarly urine of mouse and its droppings are from the absolute nature of his statement: 'Avoid urine,' and avoiding it is possible in water but not in food and clothes, so it is excused in them. (And the dog's najw) with jim: its feces, and pig, (and feces of predators) of beasts like leopard, lion, wolf, (and their saliva) meaning of predatory beasts, because it originates from impure meat, (and droppings of chicken) with three possible vowels for dal, and duck, and goose) because of its transformation into putridity and corruption, (and that which breaks wudu upon exiting from the human body) like flowing blood, semen, prostatic fluid, pre-ejaculate, menstrual blood, istihadah, and vomiting a mouthful; what is less than that and what does not flow of blood is pure in the correct view; and it is said: they render liquids impure but not solids. These things are heavy impurity unanimously. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa) because the text came regarding their impurity without opposition, which is the verse: 'And He prohibits for them the impure things' [7:157], and sound natures consider them repulsive, and prohibition indicates their impurity. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because there is no room for ijtihad in their purity. (As for) the second category: light impurity: such as horse urine, because two texts conflict: one indicating prohibition of eating horse meat (implying impurity), and one indicating permission (implying purity). This leads to a difference at his (Abu Hanifa's) end, and similarly at Abu Yusuf's because it is edible, and the fatwa is on their statement as in al-Fatawa al-Kubra, and according to Muhammad it is pure because the urine of what is eaten is pure according to him, and similarly the urine of what is eaten (meaning an animal) like camels, cows, sheep, deer, and rabbits, due to what preceded. The dispute with the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is... Note: The cud of a camel is like its dung, as in al-Dhahiriyyah. And al-Jirrah is what rises from its stomach to its mouth. And we restricted to urine of what is eaten because the impurity of camel and sheep dung, horse, mule, and donkey excrement, and cow and buffalo dung is heavy according to Imam Abu Hanifa due to the absence of conflicting texts as we mentioned. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: light, due to the occurrence among scholars, for Imam Malik considers them pure because they are used as firewood by the people of the Two Sanctuaries. This establishes the apparent lightening due to general necessity because roads are full of them, and Muhammad later declared them pure and said: Dung does not prevent prayer even if abundant, because he saw the affliction of people with roads and inns full of it when he entered al-Rayy with the caliph, and the scholars analogized based on this statement to the mud of Bukhara, because the walking of people and animals is one in them. Another note: Fish blood and the saliva of mule and donkey are pure in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), which is correct. From Abu Yusuf, that large fish, if something excessive flows from it, is heavy impurity, but there is a problem because he does not say it is heavy where there is difference, and in al-Mabsut it is stated that what is narrated from Abu Yusuf is weak. End. The correct is the apparent narration because it is not blood in reality, for blooded creatures do not live in water, and for this Muhammad sufficed in his reasoning by saying: because it lives in water, and the evidence that it is not blood is that it whitens in the sun while blood blackens, so it is not blood. (And the droppings of a bird not eaten) like falcon, hawk, eagle, kite, crow, and its impurity is light in the most correct from Abu Hanifa, because necessity does not have more effect than that; it rarely occurs except when excessive, so the ruling of lightness suffices due to general prevalence and necessity. In one narration from him: it is pure, as there is no difference between edible and inedible birds in droppings, for the droppings of edible birds are pure, so too the droppings of inedible birds. Al-Sarakhsi corrected this. Muhammad said: its impurity is heavy because nature changes it to putridity, and it is not widespread due to lack of mixing, so no necessity, no lightness, contrary to... (Footnote 1: i.e., they use it as firewood. As in al-Bada'i' (1/197). (2) With fatha on the first letter and shaddah on the second: a famous city, one of the mother cities and prominent towns, a stop for pilgrims on the way to al-Sabilah, and between it and Nishapur is one hundred and sixty parasangs. End. Mu'jam al-Buldan / Rayy /.)

Commentary
This passage is the detailed commentary (sharh) on the matn of Nur al-Idah regarding heavy and light impurities. The commentator expands each item from the matn, providing definitions, exceptions, proofs, and intra-Hanafi disputes.

For heavy impurities, the commentary clarifies that blood from liver/spleen/heart is not impure (citing a hadith). Flowing blood from a wound is heavy, but non-flowing blood or small amounts are pure. Martyr's blood is treated as pure due to necessity. The meat and skin of carrion are heavy impurities before tanning; fish and locusts are excluded. Urine of non-edible animals (including human, even infant) is heavy, but some exceptions are made due to difficulty (e.g., bat urine does not spoil water, mouse urine on clothes may be excused). Dog feces, pig feces, predator feces and saliva are heavy. Droppings of chicken, duck, goose are heavy because they change to putridity. Substances that break wudu (e.g., flowing blood, semen, urine, vomit) are heavy impurities unanimously, with proof from the Quran (7:157) 'prohibits for them the impure things' and rational argument that these are repulsive.

For light impurities, the commentary focuses on horse urine. Due to conflicting hadiths on eating horse meat, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider horse urine light (because the meat is permissible in both their views, though Abu Hanifa considers it permissible while Abu Yusuf considers it makruh tanzihi? Actually the text says 'tanzihiyyah' at his end). Muhammad considers horse urine pure. The fatwa is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

The commentary then extends the category of light impurities to urine of edible animals (camels, cows, etc.)—here there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa considers their dung/urine heavy, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it light (due to widespread difficulty and Malik's opinion of purity). Muhammad later changed his view and declared it pure based on the practical situation in al-Rayy. The relied-upon view is lightness.

Finally, fish blood is ruled pure (contrary to a weak narration from Abu Yusuf), as it is not true blood (it whitens in sun). Droppings of inedible birds: most correct from Abu Hanifa is light due to necessity and prevalence; Muhammad says heavy; one narration says pure. The commentator does not explicitly state the fatwa here.

Key Terms
دم الكبد والطحال (dam al-kabid wa al-tihal): Blood of the liver and spleen; these are not considered impure blood by consensus, based on a hadith.
بول الفرس (bawl al-faras): Urine of a horse; classified as light impurity (najasah khafifah) according to the relied-upon view.
استحالة (istihalah): Transformation; used to explain why chicken/droppings are heavy: they change to putridity.
عموم البلوى (umum al-balwa): Widespread affliction or general necessity; a basis for lightening impurity rulings in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this commentary, what is the ruling on horse urine?
A) Pure (tahir)
B) Light impurity (najasah khafifah)
C) Heavy impurity (najasah ghalizah)
D) Not impure at all (mubah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies impurities based on textual proof and necessity; heavy impurities are established by clear text, light impurities by conflicting evidence or widespread need.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Urine of Edible Animals: Light Impurity
Intra-Hanafi dispute with evidence and fatwa determination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the urine of edible animals and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Urine of edible animals is light impurity; the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the impurity of urine from animals whose meat is eaten. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf classify it as light impurity, while Muhammad considers it pure. The relied-upon position for fatwa is the light impurity view, as recorded in al-Fatawa al-Kubra.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: light impurity. Muhammad: pure. Note the basis: analogy to human urine (respect) and edibility for the first two; analogy to milk (pure) for Muhammad.
The division is consistent with the Hanafi tendency to consider the edibility of the animal.
2
Analyze the evidence
Abu Hanifa argues that urine of edible animals is analogous to human urine (due to respect for the animal) and thus light. Abu Yusuf adds edibility as a factor. Muhammad argues that since the urine is from a pure source (edible meat), it remains pure.
The fatwa picks the view of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) as it is more cautious.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is the light impurity view, as stated in al-Fatawa al-Kubra. This means that purification from such urine requires washing, but sprinkling suffices and small amounts are excused.
This ruling eases the burden on the common person.

وكذا بَوْلُ مَا يُؤْكَلُ لحمه، وخُرْءُ طير لا يُؤكلُ.. تنزيهية عنده، وعلى تقدير كونها تحريمية لحمه طاهر، لأن حرمته لكرامتـه كـالآدمي، فصـار بـولـه مخففاً عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا عند أبي يوسف لأنه مأكول، والفتوى على قولهمـا كمـا في « الفتـاوى الكبرى»، وعند محمد طاهر، لأن بول ما يؤكل لحمه طاهر عنده، وكذا بـول مــا ) بمعنى حيوان ( يُؤْكَلُ لحمه )، كالإبل والبقر والغنم والغزلان والأرانب، لما تقدم. الخلاف عندهما، وهو = = تنبيه: جرة البعير كسرقينه كذا في « الظهيرية». والجِرَّة: ما يصعد مـن جـوفـه إلـى فـيـه. وقيدنــا ببول ما يؤكل لأن نجاسة بعر الإبل والغنم، وروث الخيل والبغال والحمير ، وخثي البقر والجاموس نجاسته غليظة عند الإمام، لعدم تعارض النصين كما ذكرنا. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد: خفيفة، لوقوع بین العلماء، فإن الإمام مالك يرى طهارتها لأنها وقود (۱) أهل الحرمين. وبه يثبت التخفيف الأظهر لعموم البلوى بامتلاء الطرق بها، وطهرها محمد آخراً وقال: لا يمنع الروث وإن فحش، لما رأى من بلوى الناس من امتلاء الطرق والخانات بها، لما دخل الري) مع الخليفة، وقاس المشايخ على قوله هذا طين بخارى، لأن ممشى الناس والدواب واحد فيهما. تنبيه آخر دم السمك ولعاب البغل والحمار طاهر في ظاهر الرواية»، وهو الصحيح، وعـن أبي يوسف، أن السمك الكبير، إذا سال منه شيء فاحش يكون نجساً مغلظـاً، وفيه إشكال لأنه لا يقول بالتغليظ مع وجود الاختلاف فيه ونص في ( المبسوط». على أن المروي عن أبي يوسف ضعيف. انتهى. والصحيح ظاهر الرواية لأنه ليس بدم على التحقيق، لأن الدموي لا يسكن الماء، ولهذا اكتفى محمد في تعليل المسألة بقوله: لأن هذا مما يعيش في الماء، والدليـل عـلـى أنـه ليس بدم أنه يبيض بالشمس، والدم يسود بها، فلا يكون دماً. (وخره طير لا يُؤكل) كالصقر والبازي والعقاب والحدأة والغراب، ونجاسته مخففة في الأصح عن أبي حنيفة، بناء على أن الضرورة فيه لا تؤثر أكثر من ذلك، فإنه قَلَّ ما يصل إلا أن يفحش، فيكفي الحكم بتخفيفـه لـعـمـوم البلوى والضرورة. وفي رواية عنه طاهر، إذ لا فرق بين مأكول اللحم وغيره في الخـــرء، لأن خرء مـا ؤكل لحمه من الطيور طاهر، فكذا خرء ما لا يؤكل. وصححه السرخسي ) وقال محمد: نجاسته فليظة، لأن الطبيعة غيرته إلى نتن، ولا تعم به البلوى لعدم المخالطة، فلا ضرورة، فلا خفة، بخلاف الحمام والعصفور لوجود المخالطة فيهما. وروي عن أبي يوسف مثل ما روي عنهما.
And likewise the urine of what is eaten of meat, and the droppings of a bird not eaten... It is a light impurity according to him, and assuming it is prohibited, its meat is pure because its prohibition is due to its dignity like a human, so its urine becomes light according to Abu Hanifa. And likewise according to Abu Yusuf because it is edible, and the fatwa is on their statement as in al-Fatawa al-Kubra. According to Muhammad it is pure, because the urine of what is eaten of meat is pure according to him. And likewise the urine of what (meaning an animal) is eaten of meat, like camels, cows, sheep, deer, and rabbits, due to what preceded. The ikhtilaf according to them both. Reminder: The cud of a camel is like its dung, as in al-Zahiriyyah. And al-jirrah is what rises from its stomach to its mouth. And we restricted [the ruling] to urine of what is eaten because the impurity of camel and sheep dung, and the manure of horses, mules, donkeys, and the excrement of cows and buffaloes is heavy according to the Imam due to the absence of conflict between two texts as we mentioned. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: light, due to the occurrence among scholars, for Imam Malik considers them pure because they are fuel of the people of the two Harams. By this is established the more apparent lightening due to the generality of affliction with the filling of roads with them. And Muhammad later considered them pure and said: dung does not prevent even if it is excessive, due to what he saw of people's affliction with roads and inns filled with them when he entered ar-Rayy with the caliph. And the shaykhs analogized this to the mud of Bukhara because the walking of people and animals is one in both. Another reminder: The blood of fish and the saliva of mule and donkey are pure in the apparent narration, and that is correct. From Abu Yusuf that large fish, if something excessive flows from it, it becomes heavy impurity, but there is a difficulty because he does not say with heavy impurity while there is ikhtilaf in it. And in al-Mabsut it is stated that what is reported from Abu Yusuf is weak. End. And the correct is the apparent narration because it is not blood in reality, for blood does not dwell in water, and for this Muhammad sufficed in his explanation of the issue by saying: because this lives in water. The evidence that it is not blood is that it turns white in the sun, while blood turns black in it, so it is not blood. And the droppings of a bird not eaten, like falcon, hawk, eagle, kite, and crow. Its impurity is light in the most authentic from Abu Hanifa, based on the fact that necessity in it does not affect more than that, for rarely does it reach except if it is excessive, so the ruling of lightening suffices due to generality of affliction and necessity. In a narration from him it is pure, as there is no difference between edible meat and other in droppings, because droppings of what is eaten of meat from birds are pure, so likewise droppings of what is not eaten. Al-Sarakhsi authenticated this. Muhammad said: its impurity is heavy, because nature has changed it to a stench, and affliction is not general due to lack of mixing, so no necessity, so no lightening, unlike the pigeon and sparrow due to mixing with them. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf similar to what is narrated from them.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute covering the impurity classification of various animal substances. It begins with the urine of animals whose meat is eaten: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it light impurity, while Muhammad considers it pure. The fatwa follows the first two. A reminder is given that camel cud has the same ruling as its dung. Then the dung of various domestic animals is discussed: Abu Hanifa classifies it as heavy impurity, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is light, supported by Malik's view of purity and the widespread affliction of roads being filled with it. Muhammad later adopted a view of purity due to his observation of common practice. Another reminder addresses fish blood and saliva of mule/donkey: the correct view is that they are pure, as fish blood is not true blood (it whitens in the sun). Finally, the droppings of inedible birds: the most authentic view of Abu Hanifa is light impurity, but there is a narration of purity, and Muhammad says heavy impurity, except for pigeons and sparrows due to their constant mixing with people.

Key Terms
بول (baul): Urine; here specifically of animals whose meat is eaten.
نجاسة مخففة (najasah mukhaffafah): Light impurity; less severe, excused in larger amounts and easier to purify.
عيم البلوى (umum al-balwa): General affliction or widespread common occurrence; used as a basis for lightening the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the urine of a sheep (animal whose meat is eaten)?
A) Pure (tahir).
B) Heavy impurity (najis ghaliz).
C) Light impurity (najis khafif).
D) Pure but disliked.

Key Takeaway
Urine of edible animals is light impurity; sprinkling water suffices for purification.

Droppings of Inedible Birds: Light Impurity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on impurity of inedible bird droppings with tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the most authentic Hanafi view on droppings of inedible birds and justify it using umum al-balwa.

Core Idea
Droppings of inedible birds are light impurity due to widespread affliction and necessity.

This lesson covers the impurity of droppings from birds that are not eaten (e.g., hawks, crows). The most authentic view from Abu Hanifa is that it is light impurity, based on the principle of umum al-balwa (widespread affliction). Other narrations and Imam Muhammad's view are also discussed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Most authentic Abu Hanifa: light impurity. Another narration from Abu Hanifa: pure. Muhammad: heavy impurity. Abu Yusuf: similar to the other two.
The dispute hinges on whether the droppings are unavoidable and whether they change to a stench.
2
Analyze the rationale for light impurity
Abu Hanifa's light impurity view is based on the fact that these birds are not kept in close proximity, so their droppings are rarely encountered unless excessive, and general affliction justifies a lenient ruling.
The ruling is lightened because of the difficulty in avoiding contact.
3
Identify the most authentic view
Al-Sarakhsi authenticated the pure narration, but the majority and the text favor the light impurity view as most authentic (al-ashahh).
The mufta bihi is the light impurity view.

Dung of Domestic Animals: From Heavy to Light
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dung of domestic animals with evolution of the ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the evolution of the Hanafi ruling on dung of domestic animals and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Dung of domestic animals is light impurity; Muhammad even later considered it pure due to widespread need.

This lesson examines the ruling on dung of camels, cows, horses, etc. Abu Hanifa initially considered it heavy impurity, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad held it to be light. Later, Muhammad went further and declared it pure after observing the common practice in cities where roads and inns were filled with dung. The light impurity view is the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's heavy impurity view
Abu Hanifa considered dung of domestic animals as heavy impurity because no contradictory texts exist to lighten it, unlike urine of edible animals.
Heavy impurity requires thorough washing and is not excused in small amounts.
2
Explain the light impurity view of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it light impurity due to umum al-balwa and the fact that Imam Malik considered it pure. The widespread filling of roads with dung makes avoidance difficult.
This view is more practical for the common person.
3
Trace Muhammad's later view of purity
Muhammad, upon seeing the condition of roads and inns in Rayy, opined that dung does not prevent prayer even if excessive, analogizing it to the mud of Bukhara where people and animals walk alike.
This is a nawadir view but shows the impact of 'urf (custom).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is the light impurity view, as it is more evident due to general need (al-Fatawa al-Kubra). Muhammad's later purity view is not the operative one for fatwa.
The heavy view is abandoned.

Source Text

وكذا بَوْلُ مَا يُؤْكَلُ لحمه، وخُرْءُ طير لا يُؤكلُ.. تنزيهية عنده، وعلى تقدير كونها تحريمية لحمه طاهر، لأن حرمته لكرامتـه كـالآدمي، فصـار بـولـه مخففاً عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا عند أبي يوسف لأنه مأكول، والفتوى على قولهمـا كمـا في « الفتـاوى الكبرى»، وعند محمد طاهر، لأن بول ما يؤكل لحمه طاهر عنده، وكذا بـول مــا ) بمعنى حيوان ( يُؤْكَلُ لحمه )، كالإبل والبقر والغنم والغزلان والأرانب، لما تقدم. الخلاف عندهما، وهو = = تنبيه: جرة البعير كسرقينه كذا في « الظهيرية». والجِرَّة: ما يصعد مـن جـوفـه إلـى فـيـه. وقيدنــا ببول ما يؤكل لأن نجاسة بعر الإبل والغنم، وروث الخيل والبغال والحمير ، وخثي البقر والجاموس نجاسته غليظة عند الإمام، لعدم تعارض النصين كما ذكرنا. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد: خفيفة، لوقوع بین العلماء، فإن الإمام مالك يرى طهارتها لأنها وقود (۱) أهل الحرمين. وبه يثبت التخفيف الأظهر لعموم البلوى بامتلاء الطرق بها، وطهرها محمد آخراً وقال: لا يمنع الروث وإن فحش، لما رأى من بلوى الناس من امتلاء الطرق والخانات بها، لما دخل الري) مع الخليفة، وقاس المشايخ على قوله هذا طين بخارى، لأن ممشى الناس والدواب واحد فيهما. تنبيه آخر دم السمك ولعاب البغل والحمار طاهر في ظاهر الرواية»، وهو الصحيح، وعـن أبي يوسف، أن السمك الكبير، إذا سال منه شيء فاحش يكون نجساً مغلظـاً، وفيه إشكال لأنه لا يقول بالتغليظ مع وجود الاختلاف فيه ونص في ( المبسوط». على أن المروي عن أبي يوسف ضعيف. انتهى. والصحيح ظاهر الرواية لأنه ليس بدم على التحقيق، لأن الدموي لا يسكن الماء، ولهذا اكتفى محمد في تعليل المسألة بقوله: لأن هذا مما يعيش في الماء، والدليـل عـلـى أنـه ليس بدم أنه يبيض بالشمس، والدم يسود بها، فلا يكون دماً. (وخره طير لا يُؤكل) كالصقر والبازي والعقاب والحدأة والغراب، ونجاسته مخففة في الأصح عن أبي حنيفة، بناء على أن الضرورة فيه لا تؤثر أكثر من ذلك، فإنه قَلَّ ما يصل إلا أن يفحش، فيكفي الحكم بتخفيفـه لـعـمـوم البلوى والضرورة. وفي رواية عنه طاهر، إذ لا فرق بين مأكول اللحم وغيره في الخـــرء، لأن خرء مـا ؤكل لحمه من الطيور طاهر، فكذا خرء ما لا يؤكل. وصححه السرخسي ) وقال محمد: نجاسته فليظة، لأن الطبيعة غيرته إلى نتن، ولا تعم به البلوى لعدم المخالطة، فلا ضرورة، فلا خفة، بخلاف الحمام والعصفور لوجود المخالطة فيهما. وروي عن أبي يوسف مثل ما روي عنهما.
And likewise the urine of what is eaten of meat, and the droppings of a bird not eaten... It is a light impurity according to him, and assuming it is prohibited, its meat is pure because its prohibition is due to its dignity like a human, so its urine becomes light according to Abu Hanifa. And likewise according to Abu Yusuf because it is edible, and the fatwa is on their statement as in al-Fatawa al-Kubra. According to Muhammad it is pure, because the urine of what is eaten of meat is pure according to him. And likewise the urine of what (meaning an animal) is eaten of meat, like camels, cows, sheep, deer, and rabbits, due to what preceded. The ikhtilaf according to them both. Reminder: The cud of a camel is like its dung, as in al-Zahiriyyah. And al-jirrah is what rises from its stomach to its mouth. And we restricted [the ruling] to urine of what is eaten because the impurity of camel and sheep dung, and the manure of horses, mules, donkeys, and the excrement of cows and buffaloes is heavy according to the Imam due to the absence of conflict between two texts as we mentioned. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: light, due to the occurrence among scholars, for Imam Malik considers them pure because they are fuel of the people of the two Harams. By this is established the more apparent lightening due to the generality of affliction with the filling of roads with them. And Muhammad later considered them pure and said: dung does not prevent even if it is excessive, due to what he saw of people's affliction with roads and inns filled with them when he entered ar-Rayy with the caliph. And the shaykhs analogized this to the mud of Bukhara because the walking of people and animals is one in both. Another reminder: The blood of fish and the saliva of mule and donkey are pure in the apparent narration, and that is correct. From Abu Yusuf that large fish, if something excessive flows from it, it becomes heavy impurity, but there is a difficulty because he does not say with heavy impurity while there is ikhtilaf in it. And in al-Mabsut it is stated that what is reported from Abu Yusuf is weak. End. And the correct is the apparent narration because it is not blood in reality, for blood does not dwell in water, and for this Muhammad sufficed in his explanation of the issue by saying: because this lives in water. The evidence that it is not blood is that it turns white in the sun, while blood turns black in it, so it is not blood. And the droppings of a bird not eaten, like falcon, hawk, eagle, kite, and crow. Its impurity is light in the most authentic from Abu Hanifa, based on the fact that necessity in it does not affect more than that, for rarely does it reach except if it is excessive, so the ruling of lightening suffices due to generality of affliction and necessity. In a narration from him it is pure, as there is no difference between edible meat and other in droppings, because droppings of what is eaten of meat from birds are pure, so likewise droppings of what is not eaten. Al-Sarakhsi authenticated this. Muhammad said: its impurity is heavy, because nature has changed it to a stench, and affliction is not general due to lack of mixing, so no necessity, so no lightening, unlike the pigeon and sparrow due to mixing with them. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf similar to what is narrated from them.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute covering the impurity classification of various animal substances. It begins with the urine of animals whose meat is eaten: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it light impurity, while Muhammad considers it pure. The fatwa follows the first two. A reminder is given that camel cud has the same ruling as its dung. Then the dung of various domestic animals is discussed: Abu Hanifa classifies it as heavy impurity, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is light, supported by Malik's view of purity and the widespread affliction of roads being filled with it. Muhammad later adopted a view of purity due to his observation of common practice. Another reminder addresses fish blood and saliva of mule/donkey: the correct view is that they are pure, as fish blood is not true blood (it whitens in the sun). Finally, the droppings of inedible birds: the most authentic view of Abu Hanifa is light impurity, but there is a narration of purity, and Muhammad says heavy impurity, except for pigeons and sparrows due to their constant mixing with people.

Key Terms
بعر (ba'r): Dung of camels and sheep.
روث (rath): Manure of horses, mules, donkeys.
خثي (khiy): Excrement of cows and buffaloes.
عيم البلوى (umum al-balwa): General affliction or widespread common occurrence; used as a basis for lightening the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on horse manure?
A) Heavy impurity (najis ghaliz).
B) Light impurity (najis khafif).
C) Pure (tahir).
D) Pure but disliked.

Key Takeaway
Dung of domestic animals is light impurity; the heavy view is abandoned due to umum al-balwa.

Taharah — Purification

Fish Blood and Saliva of Mule/Donkey: Pure
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fish blood and saliva with tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on fish blood and saliva of mule/donkey and explain the evidence for their purity.

Core Idea
Fish blood and saliva of mule/donkey are pure; fish blood is not true blood.

This lesson covers two specific substances: fish blood and the saliva of mules and donkeys. The apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) declares them pure, and this is the correct view. Fish blood is not considered true blood because it whitens in the sun and the fish lives in water. The saliva of mules and donkeys is also pure despite the animal being prohibited.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on fish blood
Fish blood is pure in the apparent narration. Abu Yusuf's narration that large fish blood is heavy impurity is weak.
The proof: fish blood changes color in the sun (white), unlike true blood which darkens.
2
State the ruling on saliva of mule/donkey
Saliva of mule and donkey is pure (zahir al-riwayah).
This is analogous to the human saliva rule; the prohibition of meat does not make the saliva impure.
3
Analyze the evidence
Fish blood is not blood in the legal sense because it does not share the properties of blood (it whitens in sun, it is not 'dammi' as it lives in water). Saliva of mule/donkey: the animal's prohibition is due to dignity (like human), so its saliva is like human saliva, but human saliva is pure; however, mule/donkey are not as dignified, so saliva is pure by default.
The weak narration from Abu Yusuf is disregarded.

ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): Apparent narration; the most authentic transmission in the Hanafi school.

Excused Amount for Heavy Impurity
Ruling on excused heavy impurity amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the excused amount of heavy impurity and distinguish between solid and liquid measures.

Core Idea
Heavy impurity up to a dirham is excused; weight for solids, area for liquids.

In this lesson we examine the excused limit for heavy impurity, a key concession that allows continued prayer without removal when the impurity is minor. You'll learn the exact measure and the condition of ability to remove.

Guided Path
1
Identify heavy impurity and its excused limit
First, note that heavy impurity (najasah mughallazah) has an excused amount of one dirham. This is the default rule.
The dirham measure is based on the silver dirham weight.
2
Distinguish solid vs. liquid impurity
For solid impurities, the measure is by weight (20 qirats). For liquids, it is by area (the hollow of the palm). This distinction is important because one cannot measure weight for liquids.
3
State the condition of ability to remove
If the impurity exceeds one dirham and the person is able to remove it, removal is obligatory. If unable (e.g., no water or cloth), the excess may be excused under necessity.
The text says 'with ability to remove' – as long as one can remove, the excess must be removed.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a bloodstain on a garment, measure: if it is smaller than a dirham in area, prayer is valid without washing. If larger and removable, must wash.
This ruling is specific to prayer; other acts like tawaf may have stricter rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من بيان قسمي النجاسة شرع في بيان القدر المعفو عنه منهما، فقال: (وعُفِيَ عن قدر الدرهم) وزناً في المتجسدة، وهو عشرون قيراطاً، ومساحة في المائعة، وهـو قـدر مقعـر الكـف داخل مفـاصـل الأصابع، كما وفق به « الهندواني»، وهو الصحيح (مِنَ) النجاسة (المُغَلَّظَةِ)، فلا يعفى عنها إذا زادت على الدرهم، مع القدرة على الإزالة، (وَ) عفي قدر ما دُونَ رُبْعِ النَّوبِ) الكامل (أو البدنِ) على الصحيح، (من الخفيفة من قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ لقيام الربع مقام الكل في وجوب الصلاة في ثوب ربعه طاهر، وفي وجوب مسح ربع الرأس، ولزوم الجزاء بحلقه وهو محرم ، قال شمس الأئمة: هو الصحيح، وعن الإمام ربع أدنى ثوب تجوز فيه الصلاة، كالمئزر. قال الإمام البغدادي المشهور « بالأقطع » : وهذا هو (۳) أصح ما روي فيه، لكنه قاصر على الثوب، وقيل: ربـع المـوضـع الذي أصابه، كالذيل، والكم، والدخريض ) ، قال في «التحفة » : وهو الأصح، وفي « الحقائق » : وعليه الفتوى، وصححه صاحب ( المحيط ) وغيره، وعن أبي يوسف شبر في شبر، وهي رواية الحســن عـــن الإمام. وروى هشام عنه أكثر من شبر في شبر، لأن الشبر حد ،الفصل، فالزيادة عليه لا تعفى كالزيادة على قدر الدرهم في الغليظة. وذكر الطحاوي عنه: ذراع في ذراع، وروى المعلّى، وعلي بـــن الجعد، عن أبي حنيفة: أنه كره أن يحد الكثير الفاحش بمقدار، لتفاوت الاستفحاش بتفاوت الناس، فقد يستفحش إنسان ما لا يستفحشه غيره، فيكون موكولاً إلى العادات، (وعفي عن رَشَاش بــول) ولو مغلظاً، أو بول نفسه، وذلك (كرؤوس الإبر)، ولـو محـل إدخال الخيط للضرورة، وإن امتلأ الثوب أو البدن، لأنه لا يستطاع الامتناع عنه، ويجف بمجرد وصوله، فسقط اعتباره، ولا يخفى أن العفو يقتضي التنجس، لكن سقط حكمه، ولو أصابـه مـاء فكثر لا يجب غسله أيضاً، وعـن أبـي يوسف وجوب غسله لأنه نجس حقيقة. ولو ألقى عذرة أو بولاً في ماء فانتضح عليه مـاء مـن وقـعـهـا لا ينجس، ما لم يظهر لون النجاسة، أو يعلم أنه البول، ويعفى من غسالة الميت ما لا يمكن
When he finished explaining the two divisions of impurity, he began explaining the amount excused from them. He said: Pardon is given for the measure of a dirham in weight for solid impurities, and that is twenty qirats, and in area for liquid, and that is the measure of the hollow of the palm within the finger joints, as reconciled by al-Hindwani, and that is correct. From the heavy impurity, so it is not excused if it exceeds a dirham while having ability to remove. And pardon is given for what is less than a quarter of the complete garment or the body, correctly, from light impurity, according to the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; because a quarter stands in place of the whole in the obligation of prayer in a garment a quarter of which is pure, and in the obligation of wiping a quarter of the head, and the obligation of a penalty for shaving it while in ihram. Shams al-A'imma said: it is correct. From the Imam [Abu Hanifa there is] a quarter of the minimum garment in which prayer is permissible, like an izaar. Imam al-Baghdadi known as al-Aqta' said: this is the most authentic of what is narrated in it, but it is restricted to the garment. And it is said: a quarter of the place that is afflicted, like the hem, sleeve, and slitted opening. Al-Tuhfah said it is the most authentic, and al-Haqa'iq said the fatwa is upon it, and the author of al-Muhit and others authenticated it. From Abu Yusuf: a hand span by a hand span, and this is a narration of al-Hasan from the Imam. Hisham narrated from him: more than a hand span by a hand span, because the hand span is the limit of separation, so exceeding it is not excused like exceeding the dirham in heavy. Al-Tahawi mentioned from him: a cubit by a cubit. And Mu'alla and Ali ibn al-Ja'd narrated from Abu Hanifa: he disliked to limit excessively foul with a specific measure, because the degree of foulness varies with people, so what one considers foul another may not, so it is left to customs. And pardon is given for splashes of urine, even if heavy, or one's own urine, that are like heads of needles, even if on the place of inserting the thread for necessity, even if it fills the garment or body, because it is impossible to avoid, and it dries immediately upon reaching, so its consideration falls. And it is not hidden that pardon implies actual impurity, but its ruling has dropped. And if water that exceeds [in quantity] does not require washing it even if it increases. From Abu Yusuf: its washing is obligatory because it is truly impure. And if one throws feces or urine into water and water splashes onto him from its fall, it does not become impure unless the color of impurity appears or he knows it is urine. And the washing water of the deceased is excused as much as is possible.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh detailing the amounts of impurity that are excused in prayer. It begins by stating that for heavy impurity, the excused amount is the weight of a dirham (20 qirats) for solids, and the area of the hollow of the palm for liquids. If it exceeds that, it must be removed if possible. For light impurity, the excused amount is less than a quarter of the garment or body, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. This is supported by the principle that a quarter stands in for the whole in certain obligations (e.g., prayer in a garment a quarter of which is pure, wiping a quarter of the head). The passage then discusses various opinions: some say a quarter of the minimum garment, others say a quarter of the affected spot, and still others from Abu Yusuf say a hand span by a hand span. The relied-upon view is a quarter of the garment. The passage also covers splashes of urine: even heavy urine, if it is like needle heads, is excused because it is impossible to avoid and dries quickly. If water splashes from a fallen impurity, it does not become impure unless color or knowledge indicates otherwise. Finally, the washing water of the deceased is excused as much as possible.

Key Terms
قدر الدرهم (qadr al-dirham): The measure of a dirham in weight (20 qirats) for solids or area (hollow of the palm) for liquids; the excused limit for heavy impurity.
نجاسة مغلظة (najasah mughallazah): Heavy impurity, such as blood, urine, dog saliva, etc., which requires more stringent purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum amount of heavy impurity that is excused in prayer?
A) The size of a dirham.
B) The size of a quarter of the garment.
C) The size of a hand span.
D) Any amount less than a needle's head.

Key Takeaway
Heavy impurity up to a dirham is excused; beyond requires removal if possible.

Excused Amount for Light Impurity and Urine Splashes
Rulings on light impurity excused amount and urine splashes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the excused amount of light impurity, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the ruling on urine splashes.

Core Idea
Light impurity less than a quarter of the garment is excused; needle-head urine splashes are always excused.

Light impurity (najasah khafifah) has a different excused limit than heavy impurity. This lesson covers the quarter-of-garment measure and the significant intra-Hanafi disagreement on its exact definition. We also look at urine splashes, which are excused virtually regardless of quantity due to unavoidable nature.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for light impurity
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, light impurity is excused if it is less than a quarter of the garment (or body).
This is based on qiyas where a quarter stands for the whole in certain obligations.
2
Understand the reasoning (qiyas)
The quarter measure is derived from the principle that a quarter of a garment being pure suffices for prayer, similar to wiping a quarter of the head. Thus, impurity less than a quarter is excused.
This is a qiyas on the basis of 'qiyam al-rub' maqam al-kull'.
3
Examine intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held a hand span by hand span. Other narrations suggest a quarter of the affected spot, or even leaving it to custom. The relied-upon position is a quarter of the whole garment.
Authors like al-Halwani, al-Aqta', and others have weighed in.
4
Apply the ruling on urine splashes
Urine splashes (rashaash al-bawl) that are like heads of needles are excused entirely, even if they cover the whole garment, because avoiding them is impossible.
This applies even to one's own urine, and even if the splashes are from heavy urine.
5
Combine both rulings in a scenario
If a garment has both light impurity and urine splashes, treat each separately: light impurity less than quarter is excused; splashes excused regardless.
But note: if light impurity exceeds quarter, it invalidates prayer regardless of splashes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من بيان قسمي النجاسة شرع في بيان القدر المعفو عنه منهما، فقال: (وعُفِيَ عن قدر الدرهم) وزناً في المتجسدة، وهو عشرون قيراطاً، ومساحة في المائعة، وهـو قـدر مقعـر الكـف داخل مفـاصـل الأصابع، كما وفق به « الهندواني»، وهو الصحيح (مِنَ) النجاسة (المُغَلَّظَةِ)، فلا يعفى عنها إذا زادت على الدرهم، مع القدرة على الإزالة، (وَ) عفي قدر ما دُونَ رُبْعِ النَّوبِ) الكامل (أو البدنِ) على الصحيح، (من الخفيفة من قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ لقيام الربع مقام الكل في وجوب الصلاة في ثوب ربعه طاهر، وفي وجوب مسح ربع الرأس، ولزوم الجزاء بحلقه وهو محرم ، قال شمس الأئمة: هو الصحيح، وعن الإمام ربع أدنى ثوب تجوز فيه الصلاة، كالمئزر. قال الإمام البغدادي المشهور « بالأقطع » : وهذا هو (۳) أصح ما روي فيه، لكنه قاصر على الثوب، وقيل: ربـع المـوضـع الذي أصابه، كالذيل، والكم، والدخريض ) ، قال في «التحفة » : وهو الأصح، وفي « الحقائق » : وعليه الفتوى، وصححه صاحب ( المحيط ) وغيره، وعن أبي يوسف شبر في شبر، وهي رواية الحســن عـــن الإمام. وروى هشام عنه أكثر من شبر في شبر، لأن الشبر حد ،الفصل، فالزيادة عليه لا تعفى كالزيادة على قدر الدرهم في الغليظة. وذكر الطحاوي عنه: ذراع في ذراع، وروى المعلّى، وعلي بـــن الجعد، عن أبي حنيفة: أنه كره أن يحد الكثير الفاحش بمقدار، لتفاوت الاستفحاش بتفاوت الناس، فقد يستفحش إنسان ما لا يستفحشه غيره، فيكون موكولاً إلى العادات، (وعفي عن رَشَاش بــول) ولو مغلظاً، أو بول نفسه، وذلك (كرؤوس الإبر)، ولـو محـل إدخال الخيط للضرورة، وإن امتلأ الثوب أو البدن، لأنه لا يستطاع الامتناع عنه، ويجف بمجرد وصوله، فسقط اعتباره، ولا يخفى أن العفو يقتضي التنجس، لكن سقط حكمه، ولو أصابـه مـاء فكثر لا يجب غسله أيضاً، وعـن أبـي يوسف وجوب غسله لأنه نجس حقيقة. ولو ألقى عذرة أو بولاً في ماء فانتضح عليه مـاء مـن وقـعـهـا لا ينجس، ما لم يظهر لون النجاسة، أو يعلم أنه البول، ويعفى من غسالة الميت ما لا يمكن

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh detailing the amounts of impurity that are excused in prayer. It begins by stating that for heavy impurity, the excused amount is the weight of a dirham (20 qirats) for solids, and the area of the hollow of the palm for liquids. If it exceeds that, it must be removed if possible. For light impurity, the excused amount is less than a quarter of the garment or body, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. This is supported by the principle that a quarter stands in for the whole in certain obligations (e.g., prayer in a garment a quarter of which is pure, wiping a quarter of the head). The passage then discusses various opinions: some say a quarter of the minimum garment, others say a quarter of the affected spot, and still others from Abu Yusuf say a hand span by a hand span. The relied-upon view is a quarter of the garment. The passage also covers splashes of urine: even heavy urine, if it is like needle heads, is excused because it is impossible to avoid and dries quickly. If water splashes from a fallen impurity, it does not become impure unless color or knowledge indicates otherwise. Finally, the washing water of the deceased is excused as much as possible.

Key Terms
ربع الثوب (rub' al-thawb): One quarter of a garment; the excused limit for light impurity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
رشاش البول (rashaash al-bawl): Splashes of urine; very small amounts like heads of needles are excused.
نجاسة خفيفة (najasah khafifah): Light impurity, such as bird droppings or alcohol according to some; less restrictive than heavy impurity.

Excused Amounts of Heavy and Light Impurity
Ruling on excused amounts of impurity in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the excused amounts for heavy and light impurity and derive the ruling using Hanafi qiyas.

Core Idea
A dirham for heavy impurity, less than a quarter for light — the quarter analogy shapes the rule.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all impurity on one's garment or body invalidates prayer. The law excuses small amounts to avoid hardship. This lesson explains the specific measures for heavy and light impurity and the qiyas proof that establishes them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of impurity
Distinguish mughallazah (heavy) and khafifah (light). Heavy impurities include dog saliva and pig; light include infant urine and animal blood.
2
State the excused amount for each
For heavy: weight of a dirham (20 qirats) or area of a cupped palm. For light: less than a quarter of the garment or body.
3
Understand the qiyas proof for light impurity
The quarter is significant in prayer: prayer in a garment whose quarter is pure is valid, wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory, and shaving a quarter of the head in ihram incurs a penalty. Thus impurity less than a quarter of the garment/body is excused.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi differences
Abu Yusuf reported different measures (handspan, cubit). The quarter view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is relied upon (mufta bihi).

Source Text

(Y) الحمام والعصفور لوجود المخالطة فيهما. وروي عن أبي يوسف مثل ما روي عنهما. ولما فرغ من بيان قسمي النجاسة شرع في بيان القدر المعفو عنه منهما، فقال: (وعُفِيَ عن قدر الدرهم)) وزناً في المتجسدة، وهو عشرون قيراطاً، ومساحة في المائعة، وهـو قـدر مقعـر الكـف داخل مفـاصـل الأصابع، كما وفق به « الهندواني»، وهو الصحيح (مِنَ) النجاسة (المُغَلَّظَةِ)، فلا يعفى عنها إذا زادت على الدرهم، مع القدرة على الإزالة، (وَ) عفي قدر ما دُونَ رُبْعِ النَّوبِ) الكامل (أو البدنِ) على الصحيح، (من الخفيفة من قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ لقيام الربع مقام الكل في وجوب الصلاة في ثوب ربعه طاهر، وفي وجوب مسح ربع الرأس، ولزوم الجزاء بحلقه وهو محرم ، قال شمس الأئمة: هو الصحيح، وعن الإمام ربع أدنى ثوب تجوز فيه الصلاة، كالمئزر. قال الإمام البغدادي المشهور « بالأقطع » : وهذا هو (۳) أصح ما روي فيه، لكنه قاصر على الثوب، وقيل: ربـع المـوضـع الذي أصابه، كالذيل، والكم، والدخريض ) ، قال في «التحفة » : وهو الأصح، وفي « الحقائق » : وعليه الفتوى، وصححه صاحب ( المحيط ) وغيره، وعن أبي يوسف شبر في شبر، وهي رواية الحســن عـــن الإمام. وروى هشام عنه أكثر من شبر في شبر، لأن الشبر حد ،الفصل، فالزيادة عليه لا تعفى كالزيادة على قدر الدرهم في الغليظة. وذكر الطحاوي عنه: ذراع في ذراع، وروى المعلّى، وعلي بـــن الجعد، عن أبي حنيفة: أنه كره أن يحد الكثير الفاحش بمقدار، لتفاوت الاستفحاش بتفاوت الناس، فقد يستفحش إنسان ما لا يستفحشه غيره، فيكون موكولاً إلى العادات، (وعفي عن رَشَـاش بــول) ولو مغلظاً، أو بول نفسه، وذلك (كرؤوس الإبر)، ولـو محـل إدخال الخيط للضرورة، وإن امتلأ الثوب أو البدن، لأنه لا يستطاع الامتناع عنه، ويجف بمجرد وصوله، فسقط اعتباره، ولا يخفى أن العفو يقتضي التنجس، لكن سقط حكمه، ولو أصابـه مـاء فكثر لا يجب غسله أيضاً، وعــن أبـي يوسف وجوب غسله لأنه نجس حقيقة. ولو ألقى عذرة أو بولاً في ماء فانتضح عليه ماء مـن وقـعـهـا لا ينجس، ما لم يظهر لون النجاسة، أو يعلم أنه البول، ويعفى من غسالة الميت ما لا يمكن الامتناع عنه، ما دام في علاجه لعموم البلوى، بخلاف الغسالات الثلاث إذا اجتمعت في موضع فأصابت شيئاً نجسته. ( ولا يَنْجُسُ ثوب رطب بنشره على أرض نَجَسَةٍ يَابِسَةٍ) بإصابة بول أو سرقين)، (فَتَنْدَّتْ) الأرض (مِنْهُ)، أي من الثوب الرطب
And he excused the measure of a dirham from the heavy (impurity), and less than a quarter of the garment or the body from the light. And he excused the splash of urine like the heads of needles. (Y) The pigeon and sparrow due to the mixing in them. And it was narrated from Abu Yusuf like what was narrated from them. When he finished explaining the two types of impurity, he began explaining the excused amount from them, and said: (And he excused the measure of a dirham) in weight for solid impurity, which is twenty qirats, and in area for liquid, which is the measure of the cupped palm inside the finger joints, as reconciled by al-Hindwani, and it is the correct view; from the heavy impurity, so it is not excused if it exceeds a dirham while able to remove it. (And) he excused the measure less than a quarter of the complete garment or the body, according to the correct view, from the light impurity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; because the quarter stands in place of the whole in the obligation of prayer in a garment whose quarter is pure, and in the obligation of wiping a quarter of the head, and the obligation of a penalty for shaving it while in ihram. Shams al-A'imma said: it is the correct view. And from the Imam (Abu Hanifa): a quarter of the minimum garment in which prayer is permitted, like an izaar. Imam al-Baghdadi known as al-Aqta said: this is the most authentic narrated about it, but it is limited to the garment. And it was said: a quarter of the part that is hit, like the tail, sleeve, and slit. He said in al-Tuhfa: it is the most correct, and in al-Haqa'iq: upon it is the fatwa, and the author of al-Muhit and others affirmed it. And from Abu Yusuf: a handspan by a handspan, which is a narration of al-Hasan from the Imam. And Hisham narrated from him: more than a handspan by a handspan, because the handspan is the limit of separation; exceeding it is not excused like exceeding the dirham amount in heavy impurity. Al-Tahawi mentioned from him: a cubit by a cubit. And al-Mualla and Ali ibn al-Jaad narrated from Abu Hanifa that he disliked defining excessive foulness by a measure because of the variation of disgust among people, so it is left to customs. (And he excused the splash of urine) even if heavy, or his own urine, like the heads of needles, even the place of inserting the thread due to necessity, even if it fills the garment or body, because it cannot be avoided, and it dries upon contact, so its consideration falls. And it is not hidden that the pardon implies impurity, but its ruling is waived. And if water touches it and becomes abundant, it is not necessary to wash it also. And from Abu Yusuf: it is obligatory to wash it because it is truly impure. And if he throws feces or urine into water and water splashes onto him from its fall, it does not become impure unless the color of the impurity appears or he knows it is urine. And he excused from the washing water of the deceased what cannot be avoided, as long as it is in its treatment due to general affliction, unlike the three wash waters if they gather in a place and hit something, they make it impure. (Note: If impure oil of the excused amount hits him, then it spreads and increases, it does not prevent the validity of prayer according to the choice of al-Marghinani and a group, and others chose prohibition, and most took the prohibition over the choice of al-Marghinani, as in the commentary of al-Majma from al-Tabyin, and it indicates that it prevents in the view of others. And he said in al-Bahr and in al-Qunyah: urine that hits like the heads of needles, if it connects and spreads and exceeds the dirham amount, should be like impure oil when it spreads. End. And before that he said in al-Bahr: the consideration is at the time of hitting; so if it was impure oil of a dirham amount and then it spread and became more than that, it does not prevent in the choice of al-Marghinani and a group, and the choice of others is prohibition. So if he prayed before its expansion it is valid, and after it is not, and most took that, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. End. And if he walked in the market and his feet became wet from what was splashed in the market, his prayer is not valid because impurity is prevalent in our markets, and there is no hardship, and it was said: it suffices him. And the mud of the road and the mire that contains impurity is excused unless he sees the actual impurity, due to necessity from abundant rains, and what is generally afflicted, its case falls. (And if a bed or pure dust becomes wet) and their wetness is from the sweat of a sleeper on them, or from wetness of a foot, and the trace of impurity appears (taste, color, or smell) on the body and foot, they become impure due to the presence of impurity by the appearance of its trace in the place. And if not, they do not become impure, just as a dry pure garment wrapped in a wet impure garment that does not drip if squeezed does not become impure due to no detachment of any particle of impurity to it at that time. And the scholars differed regarding a dry pure garment that if squeezed does not drip; al-Halwani mentioned that it does not become impure in the most correct view, but it is not certain that what separates to the dry is mere moisture from the impure unless the impure does not drip when squeezed, because the dry garment receives a large amount of impurity and does not emit anything by squeezing, as observed at the beginning of washing when more than two ratls of water are poured on it, it absorbs it and does not drip when squeezed, so it is certain that a fatwa contrary to what al-Halwani affirmed should be given. (And a wet garment does not become impure by spreading it on a dry impure ground) affected by urine or dung, (so the ground becomes damp from it), i.e., from the wet garment.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Nur al-Idah addresses the amounts of impurity that are excused (mu'afu anhu) in prayer. The author first states the general ruling: for heavy impurity (najisah mughallazah), up to the weight of a dirham (twenty qirats) or, for liquid, the area of the cupped palm is excused. For light impurity (najisah khafifah), an amount less than a quarter of the garment or body is excused. The text then delves into the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the exact measure. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the criterion is a quarter of the garment or body, drawing analogy (qiyas) to the role of a quarter in prayer validity and wiping the head. Abu Yusuf is reported to have different measures: a handspan, more than a handspan, or a cubit. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is the quarter of the garment, though some restrict it to the garment itself. The discussion highlights the Hanafi method of determining excusable impurity through textual analogy and consideration of custom. The passage also covers rulings on urine splash (rashash) which is excused as long as it is like needle heads, even if it fills the garment, because it is unavoidable. There is a difference with Abu Yusuf who requires washing. Subsequent sections address splash from throwing impurity into water, water from washing a deceased person (excused while being treated due to general affliction), and the effect of impure oil expanding beyond the excused amount. The later part discusses transfer of impurity through wetness: if a wet bed or dust becomes wet from sweat or wet feet and shows trace of impurity, it becomes impure; otherwise not. Similarly, a dry garment wrapped in a wet impure garment that does not drip does not become impure, and a wet garment spread on a dry impure ground does not become impure unless the ground shows trace of impurity. These rulings are based on the principle that impurity transfers only when there is actual detachment of impurity particles.

Key Terms
النجاسة المغَلَّظة (an-najāsah al-mughallaẓah): Heavy or severe impurity, such as dog saliva, pig, or human urine and feces according to some.
النجاسة الخفيفة (an-najāsah al-khafīfah): Light impurity, such as the urine of a male infant who only drinks milk, or blood of animals.
العَفْو (al-ʿafw): Excusable impurity; the legal ruling of impurity is waived despite actual impurity.
الدرهم (ad-dirham): A silver coin; used as a measure of weight (approx. 3 grams) and area (size of a cupped palm).
الربع (ar-rub'): One quarter of the garment or body; the threshold for light impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the excused amount for heavy impurity (mughallazah) according to the passage?
A) The weight of a dirham or the area of a cupped palm
B) Less than a quarter of the garment
C) A handspan by a handspan
D) Any amount less than a cubit

Key Takeaway
Heavy impurity up to a dirham and light impurity less than a quarter of the garment or body are excused in prayer, based on qiyas of the quarter's legal significance.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Measure for Light Impurity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the measure for light impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf regarding the excused amount for light impurity, and explain which view is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the quarter rule; Abu Yusuf reports handspan and cubit measures.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a well-known disagreement on the exact excused amount for light impurity. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that it is less than a quarter of the garment or body, while Abu Yusuf reports different measures: a handspan, more than a handspan, or a cubit. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the tarjih (preferred view) that settles the issue.

Guided Path
1
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They rule that light impurity is excused if it is less than a quarter of the complete garment or the body.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's reports
Multiple reports: (1) a handspan by a handspan, (2) more than a handspan, (3) a cubit by a cubit. These are weaker views.
3
Understand the tarjih mechanism
Later authorities like Shams al-A'imma and al-Marghinani prefer the quarter view. It is considered mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).
4
Note the practical consequence
The quarter rule is lenient compared to a handspan or cubit, allowing more impurity to be excused under the light category.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Nur al-Idah addresses the amounts of impurity that are excused (mu'afu anhu) in prayer. The author first states the general ruling: for heavy impurity (najisah mughallazah), up to the weight of a dirham (twenty qirats) or, for liquid, the area of the cupped palm is excused. For light impurity (najisah khafifah), an amount less than a quarter of the garment or body is excused. The text then delves into the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the exact measure. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the criterion is a quarter of the garment or body, drawing analogy (qiyas) to the role of a quarter in prayer validity and wiping the head. Abu Yusuf is reported to have different measures: a handspan, more than a handspan, or a cubit. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is the quarter of the garment, though some restrict it to the garment itself. The discussion highlights the Hanafi method of determining excusable impurity through textual analogy and consideration of custom. The passage also covers rulings on urine splash (rashash) which is excused as long as it is like needle heads, even if it fills the garment, because it is unavoidable. There is a difference with Abu Yusuf who requires washing. Subsequent sections address splash from throwing impurity into water, water from washing a deceased person (excused while being treated due to general affliction), and the effect of impure oil expanding beyond the excused amount. The later part discusses transfer of impurity through wetness: if a wet bed or dust becomes wet from sweat or wet feet and shows trace of impurity, it becomes impure; otherwise not. Similarly, a dry garment wrapped in a wet impure garment that does not drip does not become impure, and a wet garment spread on a dry impure ground does not become impure unless the ground shows trace of impurity. These rulings are based on the principle that impurity transfers only when there is actual detachment of impurity particles.

Key Terms
شبر (shibr): A handspan; the distance between the tip of the thumb and the little finger when stretched.
ذراع (dhirā'): A cubit; approximate length from elbow to fingertip.
المرجوح (al-marjūḥ): The weaker view in a scholarly dispute, not relied upon for fatwa.
المفتى به (al-muftā bihī): The operative view that is given as a fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The mufta bihi view for light impurity is less than a quarter of the garment or body, despite Abu Yusuf's alternative reports.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Impurity Transfer Through Wetness: The Trace Condition
Ruling on impurity transfer based on trace appearance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a wet impure surface transfers impurity to the body only when a visible trace (athar) appears.

Core Idea
No trace, no impurity—even if the surface is wet and impure.

This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies a key principle in Hanafi taharah: mere contact with an impure wet surface does not automatically make the person impure. Instead, the ruling depends on a visible sign—the trace of impurity (taste, color, or smell) on the body or foot. This lesson unpacks the condition, its rationale, and its practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two impure surfaces (bed, ground) become wet from a person's sweat or footstep. The ruling hinges on whether a trace (athar) appears on the person's body or foot.
The surfaces are already impure; the wetness is the medium.
2
State the conditional ruling
If a trace of impurity (taste, color, or smell) appears on the body/foot, the person becomes impure (najis). If no trace appears, the person remains pure (tahir).
The trace is the evidence of actual transfer.
3
Understand the rationale (talil)
The presence of trace confirms that impure particles have moved to the person. Without it, the wetness may be mere moisture that did not carry any impurity. This reasoning is based on practical analogy (istihsan).
This avoids unnecessary rulings of impurity based on speculation.
4
Apply to similar cases
Use the same principle in any situation where a wet impure surface contacts a clean body part: check for visible trace. If absent, purity is presumed.
Example: stepping on wet impure floor – check foot for stain or smell.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ابتل فراش، أو تراب نَجِسانِ، وكان ابتلالهما من عرق نائم عليهما، أو من بلل قدم، وظهر أثر النجاسة في البدن والقدم تنجسا، وإلا فلا.
And if a bed or two impure pieces of earth become wet, and their wetness is from the sweat of a sleeping person upon them, or from the wetness of a foot, and the trace of impurity appears on the body and foot, they become impure; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on how impurity transfers through moisture. If a bed or ground (which is already impure) becomes wet from a source like sweat or a wet foot, the body or foot becomes impure only if a trace of the impurity (taste, color, or smell) appears on them. Otherwise, they remain pure. The reasoning is that the presence of the trace confirms actual transfer of impure particles.

Key Terms
أثر النجاسة (athar al-najāsah): Trace of impurity meaning taste, color, or smell on the body or clothing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, if a wet bed made of impure material gets wet from the sweat of a sleeping person, when does the person's body become impure?
A) Always, because the bed is impure
B) Only if a trace of impurity (taste, color, or smell) appears on the body
C) Only if the person was sweating heavily
D) Never, because sweat is pure

Key Takeaway
Impurity transfer through wetness requires visible evidence; without it, purity is presumed.

Dry Garment Wrapped in Wet Impure Garment: No Impurity without Moisture Separation
Ruling on impurity transfer from wet impure garment to dry pure garment when squeezing yields no moisture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for impurity transfer between garments when one is wet and the other dry, and identify the condition that prevents impurity transfer.

Core Idea
No impurity transfers without actual moisture separation — a Hanafi test based on physical particle transfer.

In the Hanafi school, the transmission of najasah (impurity) from one object to another is not automatic upon contact. A physical transfer of impurity particles must occur. This passage from Nur al-Idah explains a key scenario: a dry pure garment wrapped in a wet impure garment. The ruling hinges on whether the wet garment yields moisture when squeezed. We will unpack this rule and the reasoning (ta'lil) behind it, along with a scholarly difference within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the general rule
State the case: a dry pure garment is wrapped inside a wet impure garment. The rule: if the wet garment does not yield moisture when squeezed, the dry garment remains pure.
The key phrase is 'la yan'asir' (does not yield on squeezing).
2
Examine the underlying reasoning (ta'lil)
The rule is based on the absence of separation of impurity particles. If no moisture comes out, no impurity particles can transfer to the dry garment.
The ta'lil is explicitly given: 'li'idami infisal shay' min jurm al-najasah ilayhi'.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Scholars differed concerning a case where the dry garment itself would not drip if squeezed. Al-Halwani maintained that it still does not become impure, and this is the more correct view (al-ashah).
The text indicates that al-ashah is the mufta bihi.
4
Apply the principle to determine the mufta bihi
In practice, the relied-upon position is that impurity transfers only when the wet garment can be squeezed to release moisture. The condition of the dry garment does not affect the outcome in the preferred view.
This resolves the ikhtilaf in favor of al-Halwani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما لا ينجس ثوب جاف طاهر، لف في ثوب نجس رطب، لا ينعصر الرطب لو عصر؛ لعدم انفصال شيء من جرم النجاسة إليه حينئذ، واختلف المشايخ فيما لو كان الثوب الجاف الطاهر بحيث لو عصر لا يقطر، فذكر الحلواني أنه لا ينجس في الأصح.
Similarly, a dry pure garment wrapped in a wet impure garment, when the wet one does not yield moisture if squeezed, does not become impure due to no separation of any particle of impurity to it at that time. And the scholars differed regarding if the dry pure garment were such that if squeezed it would not drip; al-Halwani stated that it does not become impure in the more correct view.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage gives a rule for impurity transfer through fabrics. If a pure dry cloth is wrapped inside an impure wet cloth, and the wet cloth is so damp that no water comes out when squeezed, then no impurity transfers to the dry cloth. The reason is that no actual particles of impurity separate and reach the dry cloth. There is a scholarly difference: some say even if the dry cloth would not drip, it might still absorb the moisture; but al-Halwani maintains it remains pure in the soundest opinion.

Key Terms
انعصر (in'aṣara): To be squeezed out; if the wet garment does not yield moisture when squeezed, it is considered non-transferable.
انفصال (infiṣāl): Separation; the physical detachment of impurity particles from one surface to another.
جرم النجاسة (jurm al-najāsah): The substance or particles of impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when does a dry pure garment become impure if wrapped inside a wet impure garment?
A) Always, because of direct contact.
B) Only if the wet garment is loose enough to drip (i.e., yields moisture when squeezed).
C) Only if the dry garment becomes visibly stained.
D) Never, because dry fabric repels impurity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, impurity transfers only when moisture (and thus impurity particles) physically separates from the source to the object.

When Does Impurity Transfer? The Trace Principle in Hanafi Fiqh
Two purity rulings with an exception clause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on impurity transfer via a wet garment on dry impure ground or wind-blown impurity, and identify the condition (appearance of a trace) that triggers impurity.

Core Idea
No impurity transfer without a visible trace—contact alone is not enough.

This lesson examines two common scenarios that illustrate a core Hanafi principle: a wet garment placed on dry impure ground, and wind carrying impurity onto a garment. According to the Hanafi school, the garment remains pure unless a trace (taste, color, or smell) of the impurity becomes visible. This rule is based on the legal maxim that certainty (of purity) is not overridden by mere possibility of transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage covers two distinct situations: (1) a wet garment spread on dry impure ground, and (2) wind blowing over impurity and hitting a garment. Identify the common element: both involve potential impurity transfer without direct liquid contact.
2
State the ruling for each case
For both cases, the ruling is the same: the garment does NOT become impure (la yanjus). The text uses the negation 'ولا ينجس' to emphasize that impurity is not automatically transferred.
3
Identify the condition that changes the ruling
The exception is 'إلا أن يظهر أثرها' (unless its trace appears). The appearance of a visible sign of impurity—taste, color, or smell—triggers impurity. Without such a trace, the garment remains pure.
The key term is 'أثر' (athar), meaning a perceptible remnant of the impurity.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling is based on the principle that certainty (yaqin) is not removed by doubt (shakk). The original state of the garment is purity; mere contact with a dry impure surface or airborne particles does not create certainty of transfer. Only visible evidence establishes impurity.
This principle is known as 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' (certainty is not removed by doubt).
5
Note the intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dimension
All three major Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on this ruling. However, other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that wind automatically transfers impurity even without a visible trace, making this a point of legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينجس ثوب رطب بنشره على أرض نجسة يابسة بإصابة بول أو سرقين، فتندت الأرض منه، أي من الثوب الرطب، ولم يظهر أثرها فيه، ولا بريح هبت على نجاسة فأصابت الثوب، إلا أن يظهر أثرها فيه.
And a wet garment does not become impure by spreading it on dry impure soil (with urine or dung), so that the soil becomes moist from it (the wet garment), and no trace of it appears on it; nor by wind that blew on impurity and hit the garment, unless its trace appears on it.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
Here are two cases: First, if you spread a wet garment on dry impure ground (like soil contaminated with urine), and the garment's moisture makes the soil damp but no trace of impurity appears on the garment, the garment stays pure. Second, if wind blows over impurity and then carries particles to a garment, the garment remains pure unless a trace (taste, color, or smell) of the impurity appears on it. The common principle is that mere contact with a dry impure surface or airborne particles does not transfer impurity; only visible evidence matters.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): Visible sign of impurity (taste, color, smell)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a wet garment is spread on dry impure ground and no trace of impurity appears on the garment, what is the ruling?
A) The garment becomes impure because the ground is impure
B) The garment remains pure
C) The garment becomes impure only if the ground was wet
D) The garment must be washed once to be sure

Key Takeaway
A garment remains pure after contact with a dry impure surface or airborne impurity unless a visible trace of the impurity appears.
