Purification of Visible Impurity: Removal of Substance
Ruling on purifying items from visible impurity by removing its substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for purifying visible impurity and explain why a single wash suffices, citing the underlying rationale (zawal al-'ayn).

Core Idea
A visible impurity is purified by removing its substance; traces that are hard to remove do not invalidate purity.

When a garment or surface contacts a visible impurity like blood, the key question is: what makes it pure? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: purification is achieved by removing the substance of the impurity itself. This lesson unpacks that ruling, explains why the number of washes does not matter, and addresses special cases like impure dye, oil, honey, and old pottery. You will learn both the mainstream position and a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage rules on purifying an item contaminated with visible impurity. The act is washing; the result is purification (taharah). Determine the legal status of a single wash.
The text says 'wa law bi-marrah 'ala al-sahih' – even one wash is sufficient in the correct view.
2
Locate the Dalil (Qiyas)
The proof is qiyas (analogy): the impurity is considered to be in its substance ('ayn); removing the substance removes the cause of impurity. No explicit Quran or hadith is cited; the reasoning is talil (causal explanation).
The dalil is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala, but it is overridden by istihsan in some cases like oil.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The link: visible impurity is defined by its substance. Once the substance is gone, there is no longer any impurity to rule. The number of washes is irrelevant because the impurity is not in the water but in the substance.
This contrasts with non-visible impurity where washing once may not remove the subtle impurity.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Two differing opinions exist: Abu Ja'far required two washes after removal; Fakhr al-Islam required three. The text favors the single-wash view (al-sahih).
The differences arise from analogy to non-visible impurity (Abu Ja'far) or precaution (Fakhr al-Islam).
5
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preferred View)
The author sides with one wash, and this is the mufta bihi (operative ruling). The basis is that the impurity's cause is the substance; once it is gone, purification is complete.
This view is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطهر متنجس بنجاسة مرئية بزوال عينها، ولو بمرة على الصحيح، ولا يعتبر العدد؛ لأن النجاسة فيه باعتبار عينها فتزول بزوالها. وعن الفقيه أبي جعفر أنه يغسل مرتين بعد زوال العين، إلحاقاً لها بغير مرئية غسلت مرة. وعن فخر الإسلام أنه يغسل محلها ثلاثاً بعد زوال عينها. ولا يضر بقاء أثر، كلون وريح، شق زواله بالاحتياج إلى شيء آخر غير الماء. وعلى هذا قالوا: لو صبغ ثوبه أو يده بصبغ أو حناء نجسين، فغسل إلى أن صار الماء صافياً، يطهر مع بقاء اللون. وإذا غسل يده من دهن نجس، طهرت، ولا يضر أثر الدهن على الأصح. و إذا تنجس العسل، يلقى في قدر، ويصب عليه الماء ويغلى بالنار، حتى يعود إلى حاله الأول، ثم يفعل ذلك ثانياً وثالثاً، وكذا الدبس. والخزف والآجر والخشب، إن كان مستعملاً قديماً يكفي غسله ثلاث مرات متواليات.
And what is contaminated with visible impurity is purified by removal of its substance, even with one wash in the correct view, and multiplicity is not considered because the impurity here is by its substance and it is removed by its removal. From al-Faqih Abu Ja'far that he washes twice after removal, as analogy to non-visible that was washed once. From Fakhr al-Islam that he washes the spot three times after removal. And the remaining effect, like color and smell, the removal of which is difficult by needing something other than water, does not harm. And based on this they said: if one dyes his garment or hand with impure dye or henna, and washes until the water becomes clear, it becomes pure with the color remaining. And if he washes his hand from impure oil, it becomes pure, and the trace of oil does not harm in the soundest view. And if honey becomes impure, it is put in a pot, water is poured on it and boiled until it returns to its first state, then do that a second and third time; similarly dibs (molasses). And for pottery, bricks, and wood, if they are used old, washing them three times consecutively suffices.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage covers the purification of items contaminated with visible impurity (like blood). The key ruling: purification is achieved by removing the substance itself, even with a single wash in the correct Hanafi view. The number of washes is not required for visible impurity because the impurity is its substance; once the substance is gone, it is pure. However, there are differing opinions within the school: Abu Ja'far said two washes after removal, and Fakhr al-Islam said three. The text supports the single wash view as sahih. It also clarifies that remaining traces (like color or smell) that are hard to remove with water alone do not prevent purification. Special cases include: impure dye/henna on cloth or skin – once the water runs clear, the item is pure even if color remains; impure oil on the hand – the oil trace is not harmful because oil impurity is by contact, not inherent; honey or molasses – boil with water three times; pottery, bricks, wood – three consecutive washes suffice if they are old and used.

Key Terms
زوال العين (zawāl al-'ayn): Removal of the substance of the impurity itself (not just its effect)
شق الزوال (shuqq al-zawāl): Difficulty of removal requiring something beyond water (like soap)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the correct Hanafi view, how many times must a garment with visible blood be washed to become pure?
A) Once, after removing the blood
B) Three times
C) Seven times, once with soil
D) Until no trace remains

Key Takeaway
For visible impurity, removal of the substance (zawal al-'ayn) is the rukn; number of washes is not required, and harmless traces are excused.

Wind Carrying Impurity: The Default Ruling
States the ruling on wind carrying impurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether wind passing over impurity renders a garment impure, and identify the exceptional condition that triggers impurity.

Core Idea
Wind itself is pure; impurity transmits only if its effect appears on the garment.

A common concern is whether wind that has passed over an impurity can then contaminate clothing. The Hanafi school clarifies that wind does not carry impurity in itself; rather, it is the direct transfer of the substance that matters. This lesson examines the ruling and a point of disagreement among Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wind blowing over an impurity and then contacting a garment. The default ruling is that the garment does not become impure unless an effect of the impurity appears on it.
2
Note the exceptional condition
If the effect of the impurity (e.g., colour, smell, wetness) appears on the garment, then it becomes impure. This is the only condition that triggers impurity.
Some scholars say that if the garment is wet, impurity is transmitted because the wind carries moisture. But the correct view is that wetness does not automatically transmit impurity.
3
Understand Shams al-A'imma's position
Shams al-A'imma ruled that if a person passes wind while his seat is wet, the exiting wind (which is pure) can carry moisture and make clothes impure. However, the correct view is that the wind itself is pure and does not transmit impurity.
The basis: the substance of impurity must directly contact the garment.
4
Conclude with the correct view
The correct view is that wind is pure, and impurity does not transfer unless its effect appears. Therefore, a wet garment does not automatically become impure from wind that passed over impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولم يظهر أثرها فيه، (وَلا) ينجس الثوب (بريح هبت على نجاسة فأصابتِ) الريح (التوب)، (إلاّ أن يظهر أثرها ) أي : النجاسة (فيهِ)، أي: الثوب، وقيل: ينجس، إن كان مبلولاً، لاتصالهـا بـه، ولـو (1) خرج منه ريح ومقعدته مبلولة، حكم شمس الأئمة بتنجسه، وغيره بعدمه، وتقدم أن الصحيح طهارة الريح الخارجة، فلا تنجس الثياب المبتلة. ويَطهُرُ متنجس) سواء كان بدناً أو ثوباً، أو آنـيـة ( بِنَجَاسَةٍ)، ولو غليظة (مرئيّةٍ)، كدم، (بزوالِ عَيْنِها ، ولَوْ ) كان (بَمْرةٍ)، أي: غسلة واحدة، (عَلَى الصحيح)، ولا يعتبر العدد؛ لأن النجاسة فيه باعتبار عينها فتزول بزوالها، وعــن الفقـيـه « أبـي جعفر » أنه يغسل مرتين بعد زوال العين، إلحاقاً لها بغير مرئية غسلت مرة، وعن فخر الإسلام أنــه يغسل محلها ثلاثاً بعد زوال عينها، إلحاقاً لها بنجاسة غير مرئية، لم تغسل بعد، وإذا مسح موضع المحجمة ) بثلاث خرق رطبات نظاف، أجزأه عن الغسل، لأنه يعمل عمله. أثر الدهن على الأصح لأن نجاسة الدهن بالمجاورة، وقد زالت المجاورة، فيبقى الدهن على يده طاهراً، بخلاف شحم الميتة، فإنه لابد من زواله بالمرة، لأنه عين النجاسة. تنبيه: إذا تنجس العسل، يلقى في قدر، ويصب (۳) تطهر الأعيان النجسة كالميتة إذا صارت ملحاً، والعذرة تراباً أو رماداً. والبلة النجسة في التنور بالإحراق، ورأس الشاة إذا زال عنها الدم بالإحراق، والخمر إذا خللت كما لو تخللت، والزيت النجس إذا جعل صابوناً يطهر.
And nor [does it become impure] by wind that blew over impurity and then struck the garment, unless its effect appears in it. And it becomes pure from visible impurity by the removal of its substance, even if by one [wash] according to the correct view, and the remaining trace whose removal is difficult does not harm. And if its effect does not appear in it, (and) the garment does not become impure (by wind that blew over impurity and then struck) the wind (the garment) (unless its effect appears) i.e., of the impurity (in it), i.e., the garment. And it was said: it becomes impure if it is wet, due to its contact with it... and if he passed wind and his seat was wet, Shams al-A'imma ruled it becomes impure, others not, and it has preceded that the correct view is the purity of exiting wind, so it does not make wet clothes impure. And a mutanajjis (something that has become impure) becomes pure, whether it be a body or garment or vessels, (from an impurity) even if thick (visible), like blood, (by removal of its substance, even if) it is (by one [wash]), i.e., one wash, (according to the correct view), and the number is not considered, because impurity in it is by virtue of its substance, so it goes away by its removal. And from the jurist Abu Ja'far that he washes twice after removal of the substance, analogizing it to a non-visible [impurity] washed once, and from Fakhr al-Islam that he washes its place three times after removal of its substance, analogizing it to a non-visible impurity that has not been washed yet. And if he wipes the place of cupping with three clean wet cloths, it suffices him from washing, because it does its work. (And the remaining trace) like color and smell in its place, (whose removal is difficult) by needing something other than water, or other than liquid, to remove it, like potash and soap, because the tool prepared for removing impurity is water, so if it needs something else, that is difficult. And upon this they said: if he dyed his garment or hand with a dye or henna [both impure], and washed until the water became clear, it becomes pure with the [remaining] color, and it was said: he washes afterwards three times. And if he washed his hand from impure oil, it becomes pure, and the trace of oil does not harm according to the most correct view, because the impurity of the oil is by adjacency, and the adjacency has gone, so the oil remaining on his hand is pure, unlike the fat of a carcass, for it must be removed completely, because it is the substance of impurity. Note: If honey becomes impure, it is thrown into a pot, and water is poured over it and boiled with fire until it returns to its original state, then this is done a second and third time, and similarly date syrup, as narrated from Abu Yusuf regarding oil if impurity befell it: it is placed in a vessel, then water is poured over it, so the oil rises above the water, then it is removed with something, and so on three times, then it becomes pure on the third time. And earthenware, fired brick, and wood: if old and used, washing it three times consecutively suffices; like the body if it becomes impure with a non-visible [impurity] and water is poured over it consecutively, it takes the place of three washes, and the criterion is the preponderance of the presumption of purity with the removal of the trace. And if the washer is a child or insane, then the criterion is the presumption of the user, because he is the one who needs it. And if it is new, like pottery, it is washed three times and dried each time by the cessation of dripping, and this is the interpretation of drying in such cases, like leather tanned with something impure, and dryness is not required. And in al-Hawi: [he said] the new of earthenware and vessels is burned, and the old is washed, and vessels of glass and polished lead are wiped, and rough [vessels] are washed, and new wood is scraped and old is washed, and meat cooked with impurity while boiling is not eaten, because it absorbs the impurity, and if not while boiling, then it is washed three times and becomes pure, and the broth has no good in it. And wheat if cooked with wine: Abu Yusuf said: it is cooked three times with water and dried each time, and the fatwa is that it never becomes pure, and this is the statement of Abu Hanifa. And wheat moistened with impurity until it swells becomes pure by cooking three times and cooling each time, and when it dries and is ground, its eating becomes permissible if no trace of impurity is seen in it. And the chicken that was boiled before the removal of its intestines: if it remained until it reached cookedness, it does not become pure at all, and similarly the stomach before its purification due to the absorption of impurity. But if it was thrown [into water] to the extent of the opening of pores for plucking feathers, then it becomes pure by washing three times. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned that a knife that has been treated with impure water is treated with pure water three times. End. And if it were said: one treatment suffices, it would be sound, because fire removes the parts of impurity entirely, and repetition removes doubt. End. And the treatment purifies its inside according to Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the fatwa. And if it is washed before treatment with water three times, its outside becomes pure, so that if one cuts watermelon with it, it does not become impure. And if a person carries it before treating it three times with pure [water], his prayer is not valid, by agreement. And transformation (istihalah) purifies impure entities, like a carcass when it becomes salt, and feces [becomes] soil or ash, and the wet impurity in the oven by burning, and the head of a sheep when the blood is removed by burning, and wine when it becomes vinegar, just as if it becomes vinegar, and impure oil when made into soap becomes pure.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary expanding on the matn of Nur al-Idah. It covers three main areas: (1) wind carrying impurity and its effect on cloth, (2) purification from visible impurity by removal of its substance, and (3) the ruling on residual traces that are difficult to remove.

First, the author states that wind which has passed over an impurity and then strikes a garment does not make the garment impure unless the effect of the impurity appears on the garment. There is an alternative view that if the garment is wet, it becomes impure due to direct contact. Shams al-A'imma ruled that if one passes wind while his seat is wet, the exiting wind (which is pure) can carry moisture and make the clothes impure, but the correct view is that the wind itself is pure, so it does not cause impurity. This is based on the principle that the substance of impurity must directly transfer.

Second, the ruling on visible impurity (like blood) is that the item becomes pure by removal of the substance itself, even with a single wash, because the impurity is considered to reside in the substance. Once the substance is gone, the item is pure. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Ja'far said two washes after removal, and Fakhr al-Islam said three washes, analogizing to non-visible impurity. But the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is one wash, as stated 'ala al-sahih. The author also mentions that wiping the site of cupping with three clean wet cloths suffices instead of washing, because it accomplishes the same purpose.

Third, regarding residual traces like color or smell that are difficult to remove with water alone, such as dye or henna, if the item is washed until the water runs clear, it is pure even if the color remains. This is because the water is the tool for removing impurity, and if an additional substance (like soap) is needed, that is considered a difficulty. The principle is that what is hard to remove is excused. However, if the remaining trace is from the substance of impurity itself (like fat from a carcass), it must be completely removed because it is the very impurity. The text gives examples: if one dyes a garment with an impure dye, washing until the water is clear renders it pure despite the color. Similarly, if one washes a hand from impure oil, the trace of oil is not harmful because the oil itself is not the impurity; it only became impure by contact, and that contact no longer exists.

The passage then provides specific applications: honey, date syrup, oil, earthenware, wood, meat, wheat, chicken, and knives. For honey and date syrup, they are purified by boiling with water three times. For oil, it is purified by floating it on water three times. For old earthenware, three consecutive washes suffice; for new earthenware, it must be burned. Meat cooked with impurity while boiling is not purified; if not boiling, washing three times works. Wheat cooked with wine: Abu Hanifa says it never becomes pure; Abu Yusuf said cooking three times with drying suffices, but the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa. Chicken boiled without removing intestines does not become pure if it reaches full cooking; otherwise, washing three times works. A knife treated with impure water is treated with pure water three times (Abu Yusuf's view, which is the fatwa). Finally, the principle of istihalah (transformation) purifies impure substances: e.g., a carcass turning into salt, feces becoming ash, wine becoming vinegar, impure oil made into soap. This is because the substance itself has changed into something else.

Key Terms
نجاسة مرئية (najāsa mar'iyya): Visible impurity, such as blood, which has a substance that can be seen and removed.
شق زواله (shaqqu zawālihi): A trace (e.g., color, smell) whose removal requires something beyond water and is therefore excused.
الاستحالة (al-istiḥāla): Transformation of a substance into a different substance, which purifies the original impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, what is the condition for a garment to become impure when wind blows over impurity and strikes it?
A) The wind must be strong.
B) The garment must be wet.
C) The effect of the impurity must appear on the garment.
D) The impurity must be thick.

Key Takeaway
Wind is pure; impurity transfers only if its effect is visible on the garment.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
One Wash Suffices for Visible Impurity
States the ruling on purification from visible impurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why one wash suffices for visible impurity according to the Hanafi school, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the number of washes.

Core Idea
Visible impurity is removed by its substance; one wash suffices – no fixed number.

When a garment or body is contaminated with a visible impurity like blood, the key question is how many washes are required. The Hanafi school maintains that once the substance is removed, the item is pure, even with a single wash. This lesson explores the proof and addresses the differences among Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Define visible impurity
Visible impurity (najāsa mar'iyya) is a substance that has body, like blood, that can be seen and physically removed.
Contrast with non-visible impurity (e.g., urine that dries without trace) which requires washing.
2
State the ruling
The item becomes pure upon removal of the substance of the impurity, even if only one wash is performed. The number of washes is not required because impurity is by substance.
The text says 'على الصحيح' (according to the correct view).
3
Analyse the proof
Proof: impurity in a visible object is by virtue of its substance (عين), so when the substance is gone, the impurity is gone. This is a rational proof based on the nature of impurity.
No explicit Quran or hadith is cited; it's a talil (rational argument).
4
Compare with other Hanafi views
Abu Ja'far required two washes after removal, and Fakhr al-Islam required three. They analogized to non-visible impurity. The correct view is that visible impurity is different because its source is directly removed.
The cupping example: wiping with three wet cloths suffices; this supports that one effective removal is enough.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that one wash suffices. This is the fatwa and the view of the founding imams.
The text says 'على الصحيح' indicating tarjih.

ولم يظهر أثرها فيه، (وَلا) ينجس الثوب (بريح هبت على نجاسة فأصابتِ) الريح (التوب)، (إلاّ أن يظهر أثرها ) أي : النجاسة (فيهِ)، أي: الثوب، وقيل: ينجس، إن كان مبلولاً، لاتصالهـا بـه، ولـو (1) خرج منه ريح ومقعدته مبلولة، حكم شمس الأئمة بتنجسه، وغيره بعدمه، وتقدم أن الصحيح طهارة الريح الخارجة، فلا تنجس الثياب المبتلة. ويَطهُرُ متنجس) سواء كان بدناً أو ثوباً، أو آنـيـة ( بِنَجَاسَةٍ)، ولو غليظة (مرئيّةٍ)، كدم، (بزوالِ عَيْنِها ، ولَوْ ) كان (بَمْرةٍ)، أي: غسلة واحدة، (عَلَى الصحيح)، ولا يعتبر العدد؛ لأن النجاسة فيه باعتبار عينها فتزول بزوالها، وعــن الفقـيـه « أبـي جعفر » أنه يغسل مرتين بعد زوال العين، إلحاقاً لها بغير مرئية غسلت مرة، وعن فخر الإسلام أنــه يغسل محلها ثلاثاً بعد زوال عينها، إلحاقاً لها بنجاسة غير مرئية، لم تغسل بعد، وإذا مسح موضع المحجمة ) بثلاث خرق رطبات نظاف، أجزأه عن الغسل، لأنه يعمل عمله. (ولا يضر بقاء أثـر ) كلون وريح في محلها، (شَقَ زَوَالُهُ) بالاحتياج إلى شيء آخر غير الماء، أو غـيـر الـمـائـع، لقلعـهـا، كحرض ) وصابون، لأن الآلة المعدة لقلع النجاسة هي الماء، فإذا احتاج إلى شيء آخــر شـق عـلـيـه ذلك، وعلى هذا قالوا: لو صبغ ثوبه أو يده بصبغ أو حناء [ نجسين ]، فغسل إلى أن صار الماء صافياً، يطهر مع [ قيام ] اللون، وقيل: يغسل بعد ذلك ثلاثاً، وإذا غسـل يـده مـن دهـن نجس، طهرت، ولا يضر أثر الدهن على الأصح لأن نجاسة الدهن بالمجاورة، وقد زالت المجاورة، فيبقى الدهن على يده طاهراً، بخلاف شحم الميتة، فإنه لابد من زواله بالمرة، لأنه عين النجاسة. تنبيه: إذا تنجس العسل، يلقى في قدر، ويصب عليه الماء ويغلى بالنار، حتى يعود إلى حاله الأول، ثم يفعل ذلك ثانياً وثالثاً، وكذا الدبس، كما روي عن أبي يوسف في الدهن إذا أصابته النجاسة، يجعل في إناء، ثم يصب عليه الماء، فيعلو الدهن الماء، فيرفع بشيء، وهكذا ثلاثاً، فيطهر في المرة الثالثة، والخزف والآجر والخشب، إن قديماً مستعملاً يكفي غسله ثلاث مرات متواليات؛ كالجسد إذا تنجس بغير مرئية وصب الماء عليه متوالياً، يقوم مقام التثليث، والعبرة لـغـلبـة ظـن الطهارة مع زوال الأثر، وإذا كان الغاسل صغيراً أو مجنوناً، فالعبرة لظن المستعمل، لأنـه هـو المحتاج إليه، وإن كان حديثاً كالفخار، يغسل ثلاث مرات ويجففه كل مرة بانقطاع التقاطر، وهذا تفسير الجفاف في مثله، كالجلد المدبوغ بنجس، ولا يشترط اليبس. وقال في « الحاوي » : بحرق الجديد من الخزف والأواني، وبغسل العتيق، وأواني الزجاج والرصاص الصقيل يمسح، والخشن يغسل، والخشب الجديد ينحت والقديم يغسل، واللحم المطبوخ بالنجس حالة الغليان لا يؤكل، لأنه يتشرب النجاسة، وإن لم يكن حالة الغليان، فإنه يغسل ثلاثاً، فيطهر، والمرقة لا خير فيها، والحنطة إذا طبخت بخـمـر قـال أبو يوسف: تطبخ ثلاثاً بالماء، وتجفف كل مرة، والفتوى على أنها لا تطهر أبداً، وهو قول أبي حنيفة، والحنطة المبلولة بالنجس حتى انتفخت تطهر بالطبخ ثلاثاً وتبرد في كل مرة، فإذا جفت وطحنـت حــل أكلها، إن لم ير فيها أثر النجاسة، والدجاجة التي أُغليت قبل إخراج أمعائها، إن بقيت إلى أن وصلت إلى الاستواء، لا تطهر أصلاً، وكذا الكرش قبل تطهيره لتشرب النجاسة، وأما إذا أُلقيت، بقدر انحلال المسام لنتف الريش، فتطهر بالغسل ثلاثاً. ذكره الزيلعي وغيره، أن السكين المموهة بالماء النجس، تمـوه السكين بالماء الطاهر ثلاث مرات. انتهى. ولو قيل: يكفي التمويه مرة، لكان وجيهاً، لأن النار تزيل أجزاء النجاسة بالكلية، والتكرار يزيل الشبهة. انتهى. والتمويه يطهر باطنها عند أبي يوسف، وعـلـيـه الفتوى، وإذا غسلت قبل التمويه بالماء ثلاثاً، طهر ظاهرها، حتى لو قطع بـهـا بطيخ لا يتنجس، ولـو حملها إنسان قبل تمويهها ثلاثاً بالطاهر، لا تجوز صلاته، اتفاقاً، والاستحالة (۳) تطهر الأعيان النجسة كالميتة إذا صارت ملحاً، والعذرة تراباً أو رماداً. والبلة النجسة في التنور بالإحراق، ورأس الشاة إذا زال عنها الدم بالإحراق، والخمر إذا خللت كما لو تخللت، والزيت النجس إذا جعل صابوناً يطهر.

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary expanding on the matn of Nur al-Idah. It covers three main areas: (1) wind carrying impurity and its effect on cloth, (2) purification from visible impurity by removal of its substance, and (3) the ruling on residual traces that are difficult to remove.

First, the author states that wind which has passed over an impurity and then strikes a garment does not make the garment impure unless the effect of the impurity appears on the garment. There is an alternative view that if the garment is wet, it becomes impure due to direct contact. Shams al-A'imma ruled that if one passes wind while his seat is wet, the exiting wind (which is pure) can carry moisture and make the clothes impure, but the correct view is that the wind itself is pure, so it does not cause impurity. This is based on the principle that the substance of impurity must directly transfer.

Second, the ruling on visible impurity (like blood) is that the item becomes pure by removal of the substance itself, even with a single wash, because the impurity is considered to reside in the substance. Once the substance is gone, the item is pure. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Ja'far said two washes after removal, and Fakhr al-Islam said three washes, analogizing to non-visible impurity. But the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is one wash, as stated 'ala al-sahih. The author also mentions that wiping the site of cupping with three clean wet cloths suffices instead of washing, because it accomplishes the same purpose.

Third, regarding residual traces like color or smell that are difficult to remove with water alone, such as dye or henna, if the item is washed until the water runs clear, it is pure even if the color remains. This is because the water is the tool for removing impurity, and if an additional substance (like soap) is needed, that is considered a difficulty. The principle is that what is hard to remove is excused. However, if the remaining trace is from the substance of impurity itself (like fat from a carcass), it must be completely removed because it is the very impurity. The text gives examples: if one dyes a garment with an impure dye, washing until the water is clear renders it pure despite the color. Similarly, if one washes a hand from impure oil, the trace of oil is not harmful because the oil itself is not the impurity; it only became impure by contact, and that contact no longer exists.

The passage then provides specific applications: honey, date syrup, oil, earthenware, wood, meat, wheat, chicken, and knives. For honey and date syrup, they are purified by boiling with water three times. For oil, it is purified by floating it on water three times. For old earthenware, three consecutive washes suffice; for new earthenware, it must be burned. Meat cooked with impurity while boiling is not purified; if not boiling, washing three times works. Wheat cooked with wine: Abu Hanifa says it never becomes pure; Abu Yusuf said cooking three times with drying suffices, but the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa. Chicken boiled without removing intestines does not become pure if it reaches full cooking; otherwise, washing three times works. A knife treated with impure water is treated with pure water three times (Abu Yusuf's view, which is the fatwa). Finally, the principle of istihalah (transformation) purifies impure substances: e.g., a carcass turning into salt, feces becoming ash, wine becoming vinegar, impure oil made into soap. This is because the substance itself has changed into something else.

Key Terms
نجاسة مرئية (najāsa mar'iyya): Visible impurity, such as blood, which has a substance that can be seen and removed.
شق زواله (shaqqu zawālihi): A trace (e.g., color, smell) whose removal requires something beyond water and is therefore excused.

D) Because visible impurity is less severe.

Key Takeaway
Visible impurity is removed by the removal of its substance; one wash suffices.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Residual Traces: When Colour or Smell Does Not Prevent Purity
States the ruling on residual traces after washing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a remaining trace (colour/smell) is excused after washing, and apply the principle to examples like dye and henna.

Core Idea
If removing a trace requires something beyond water, it is excused; water is the tool for purification.

After washing a garment that had impurity, sometimes colour or smell remains. Is the garment still impure? The Hanafi school rules that if the trace is difficult to remove with water alone, it does not affect purity. This lesson clarifies the principle and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trace
The trace is either colour (لون) or smell (ريح) that remains after washing.
Not the substance of impurity itself.
2
Determine if removal is difficult
If the trace's removal requires something beyond water (e.g., soap, potash), then it is considered 'difficult to remove' (شق زواله).
The difficulty is not just personal effort, but the need for a substance other than water.
3
Apply the ruling
If the trace is difficult to remove, it does not prevent purity. The item is pure once the substance of impurity is gone and the water runs clear.
Example: dyeing cloth with impure dye – after washing until water is clear, the colour remains but the cloth is pure.
4
Distinguish from substance of impurity
If the trace is the actual substance of impurity (e.g., fat of a carcass), it must be removed completely because it is itself the impurity.
Impurity by adjacency: oil that becomes impure by contact does not leave a trace that is itself impure; but fat of a carcass is intrinsically impure.
5
Recognize the principle
The tool for purification is water. If an additional substance is needed, that is a difficulty that is overlooked.
This principle is derived from the idea that the Sharī’ah does not impose unbearable hardship.

And nor [does it become impure] by wind that blew over impurity and then struck the garment, unless its effect appears in it. And it becomes pure from visible impurity by the removal of its substance, even if by one [wash] according to the correct view, and the remaining trace whose removal is difficult does not harm. And if its effect does not appear in it, (and) the garment does not become impure (by wind that blew over impurity and then struck) the wind (the garment) (unless its effect appears) i.e., of the impurity (in it), i.e., the garment. And it was said: it becomes impure if it is wet, due to its contact with it... and if he passed wind and his seat was wet, Shams al-A'imma ruled it becomes impure, others not, and it has preceded that the correct view is the purity of exiting wind, so it does not make wet clothes impure. And a mutanajjis (something that has become impure) becomes pure, whether it be a body or garment or vessels, (from an impurity) even if thick (visible), like blood, (by removal of its substance, even if) it is (by one [wash]), i.e., one wash, (according to the correct view), and the number is not considered, because impurity in it is by virtue of its substance, so it goes away by its removal. And from the jurist Abu Ja'far that he washes twice after removal of the substance, analogizing it to a non-visible [impurity] washed once, and from Fakhr al-Islam that he washes its place three times after removal of its substance, analogizing it to a non-visible impurity that has not been washed yet. And if he wipes the place of cupping with three clean wet cloths, it suffices him from washing, because it does its work. (And the remaining trace) like color and smell in its place, (whose removal is difficult) by needing something other than water, or other than liquid, to remove it, like potash and soap, because the tool prepared for removing impurity is water, so if it needs something else, that is difficult. And upon this they said: if he dyed his garment or hand with a dye or henna [both impure], and washed until the water became clear, it becomes pure with the [remaining] color, and it was said: he washes afterwards three times. And if he washed his hand from impure oil, it becomes pure, and the trace of oil does not harm according to the most correct view, because the impurity of the oil is by adjacency, and the adjacency has gone, so the oil remaining on his hand is pure, unlike the fat of a carcass, for it must be removed completely, because it is the substance of impurity. Note: If honey becomes impure, it is thrown into a pot, and water is poured over it and boiled with fire until it returns to its original state, then this is done a second and third time, and similarly date syrup, as narrated from Abu Yusuf regarding oil if impurity befell it: it is placed in a vessel, then water is poured over it, so the oil rises above the water, then it is removed with something, and so on three times, then it becomes pure on the third time. And earthenware, fired brick, and wood: if old and used, washing it three times consecutively suffices; like the body if it becomes impure with a non-visible [impurity] and water is poured over it consecutively, it takes the place of three washes, and the criterion is the preponderance of the presumption of purity with the removal of the trace. And if the washer is a child or insane, then the criterion is the presumption of the user, because he is the one who needs it. And if it is new, like pottery, it is washed three times and dried each time by the cessation of dripping, and this is the interpretation of drying in such cases, like leather tanned with something impure, and dryness is not required. And in al-Hawi: [he said] the new of earthenware and vessels is burned, and the old is washed, and vessels of glass and polished lead are wiped, and rough [vessels] are washed, and new wood is scraped and old is washed, and meat cooked with impurity while boiling is not eaten, because it absorbs the impurity, and if not while boiling, then it is washed three times and becomes pure, and the broth has no good in it. And wheat if cooked with wine: Abu Yusuf said: it is cooked three times with water and dried each time, and the fatwa is that it never becomes pure, and this is the statement of Abu Hanifa. And wheat moistened with impurity until it swells becomes pure by cooking three times and cooling each time, and when it dries and is ground, its eating becomes permissible if no trace of impurity is seen in it. And the chicken that was boiled before the removal of its intestines: if it remained until it reached cookedness, it does not become pure at all, and similarly the stomach before its purification due to the absorption of impurity. But if it was thrown [into water] to the extent of the opening of pores for plucking feathers, then it becomes pure by washing three times. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned that a knife that has been treated with impure water is treated with pure water three times. End. And if it were said: one treatment suffices, it would be sound, because fire removes the parts of impurity entirely, and repetition removes doubt. End. And the treatment purifies its inside according to Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the fatwa. And if it is washed before treatment with water three times, its outside becomes pure, so that if one cuts watermelon with it, it does not become impure. And if a person carries it before treating it three times with pure [water], his prayer is not valid, by agreement. And transformation (istihalah) purifies impure entities, like a carcass when it becomes salt, and feces [becomes] soil or ash, and the wet impurity in the oven by burning, and the head of a sheep when the blood is removed by burning, and wine when it becomes vinegar, just as if it becomes vinegar, and impure oil when made into soap becomes pure.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary expanding on the matn of Nur al-Idah. It covers three main areas: (1) wind carrying impurity and its effect on cloth, (2) purification from visible impurity by removal of its substance, and (3) the ruling on residual traces that are difficult to remove.

First, the author states that wind which has passed over an impurity and then strikes a garment does not make the garment impure unless the effect of the impurity appears on the garment. There is an alternative view that if the garment is wet, it becomes impure due to direct contact. Shams al-A'imma ruled that if one passes wind while his seat is wet, the exiting wind (which is pure) can carry moisture and make the clothes impure, but the correct view is that the wind itself is pure, so it does not cause impurity. This is based on the principle that the substance of impurity must directly transfer.

Second, the ruling on visible impurity (like blood) is that the item becomes pure by removal of the substance itself, even with a single wash, because the impurity is considered to reside in the substance. Once the substance is gone, the item is pure. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Ja'far said two washes after removal, and Fakhr al-Islam said three washes, analogizing to non-visible impurity. But the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is one wash, as stated 'ala al-sahih. The author also mentions that wiping the site of cupping with three clean wet cloths suffices instead of washing, because it accomplishes the same purpose.

Third, regarding residual traces like color or smell that are difficult to remove with water alone, such as dye or henna, if the item is washed until the water runs clear, it is pure even if the color remains. This is because the water is the tool for removing impurity, and if an additional substance (like soap) is needed, that is considered a difficulty. The principle is that what is hard to remove is excused. However, if the remaining trace is from the substance of impurity itself (like fat from a carcass), it must be completely removed because it is the very impurity. The text gives examples: if one dyes a garment with an impure dye, washing until the water is clear renders it pure despite the color. Similarly, if one washes a hand from impure oil, the trace of oil is not harmful because the oil itself is not the impurity; it only became impure by contact, and that contact no longer exists.

The passage then provides specific applications: honey, date syrup, oil, earthenware, wood, meat, wheat, chicken, and knives. For honey and date syrup, they are purified by boiling with water three times. For oil, it is purified by floating it on water three times. For old earthenware, three consecutive washes suffice; for new earthenware, it must be burned. Meat cooked with impurity while boiling is not purified; if not boiling, washing three times works. Wheat cooked with wine: Abu Hanifa says it never becomes pure; Abu Yusuf said cooking three times with drying suffices, but the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa. Chicken boiled without removing intestines does not become pure if it reaches full cooking; otherwise, washing three times works. A knife treated with impure water is treated with pure water three times (Abu Yusuf's view, which is the fatwa). Finally, the principle of istihalah (transformation) purifies impure substances: e.g., a carcass turning into salt, feces becoming ash, wine becoming vinegar, impure oil made into soap. This is because the substance itself has changed into something else.

Key Terms
شق زواله (shaqqu zawālihi): A trace (e.g., color, smell) whose removal requires something beyond water and is therefore excused.
نجاسة بالمجاورة (najāsa bil-mujāwara): Impurity that affects something by contact, not intrinsically. Once the contact is removed, the thing becomes pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a remaining trace (colour/smell) not prevent purity?
A) When the trace is faint.
B) When the trace is difficult to remove with water alone.
C) When the trace is from an impure substance.
D) When the trace is on a garment.

Key Takeaway
Residual colour or smell that is difficult to remove with water does not affect purity.

Taharah — Purification

Purification of Non-Visible Najasah by Three Washes
Provides the ruling and proof for washing non-visible impurity three times with wringing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for purifying non-visible impurity and apply the hadith proof to determine the required number of washes.

Core Idea
Non-visible impurity requires three washes with wringing; the hadith of the awakened person establishes this numerical standard.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling for purifying non-visible impurity (najasa ghayr mar'iyya), such as dried urine on a garment. The text from Imdad al-Fattah explains that three washes, each accompanied by wringing, are obligatory. The reasoning is rooted in the need to achieve a preponderant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) of purity, and the hadith of the awakened person serves as the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being purified (non-visible impurity) and the hukm level: wajib (obligatory) to wash three times with wringing.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the hadith of the awakened person: 'When one of you wakes from sleep, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times...'
This is an ahad hadith, zanni al-thubut, but sufficient for wajib rulings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'yaghsilha thalathan' indicates obligation (wujub) per Hanafi usul. The reason for washing (uncertainty of impurity) is analogized to any non-visible impurity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf stated that a seventh wash would also purify, but the relied-upon view is three washes.
Intra-Hanafi difference, but mufta bihi remains three.
5
Apply to dog impurity
For dog impurity, seven washes with dusting is recommended (not obligatory) to avoid Shafi'i disagreement.
The Hanafi original position is one wash, but practice follows the recommendation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(و)[ يطهر ] محل النجاسة (غير المرئية بغسلها ثلاثاً) وجوباً، وسبعاً مع التتريب ندباً في نجاسة الكلب خروجاً من الخلاف. (والعَصْر كلّ مرة) تقديراً لغلبة الظن فإنه المدار فأقيم السبب الظاهـر مـقـام غـلبـة الظن تيسيراً، ولأن التكرار لا بد منه للاستخراج. وفي ( المبسوط » : لا يحكم بزوالها قبل الثلاث، لحديث المستيقظ (). وعن أبي يوسف: إذا غسله سابعة طهر. و العَصْر كلّ مرّة..
And non-visible [impurity is purified] by washing it three times, and wringing each time, as an estimation for the preponderance of presumption, for it is the basis; so the apparent cause is made to stand in place of the preponderance of presumption for facilitation, and because repetition is necessary for extraction. And in al-Mabsut: it is not judged to have gone before three [washes], due to the hadith of the one who wakes up. And from Abu Yusuf: if he washes it a seventh time, it becomes pure. And wringing each time... And seven [washes] with dusting is recommend in the impurity of the dog to exit from disagreement. (And the place of impurity becomes pure) (non-visible by washing it three times) obligatorily, and seven with dusting is recommend in the impurity of the dog to exit from disagreement. (And wringing each time) as an estimation for the preponderance of presumption, for it is the basis; so the apparent cause is made to stand in place of the preponderance of presumption for facilitation, and because repetition is necessary for extraction. And in al-Mabsut: it is not judged to have gone before three [washes], due to the hadith of the one who wakes up. And from Abu Yusuf: if he washes it a seventh time, it becomes pure.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah focuses on the purification of non-visible impurity (najasa ghayr mar'iyya), such as urine that has dried. The author explains that the required method is to wash the affected area three times, and each time must include wringing (in the case of a garment or similar) or squeezing to expel the absorbed impurity. The reason given is that the repetition and wringing serve to create a preponderant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the impurity has been removed. The apparent cause (sabab zahir) of three washes is established as a substitute for this presumption for ease of practice, since it is impossible to know for certain when the impurity is gone.

The proof for three washes is the hadith of the awakened person (hadith al-mustayqiz): The Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'When one of you wakes from sleep, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times, for he does not know where his hand spent the night.' This hadith is used to establish that three washes are sufficient for removing uncertainty about impurity.

The author then notes that according to Abu Yusuf, if one washes a seventh time, the item becomes pure (meaning that the seventh wash is sufficient, but not required). The text also mentions that for the impurity of a dog, it is recommended (not obligatory) to wash seven times, the first with dust (turbah), to avoid disagreement with the Shafi'i school. This is a concession to the dominant practice while adhering to the Hanafi view that one wash suffices for dog saliva.

The obligatory (wajib) requirement for non-visible impurity is three washes with wringing; the wringing is considered an integral part of the purification process for porous items like cloth. The hadith from al-Mabsut clarifies that before three washes, the impurity is not legally considered removed.

Overall, the passage emphasizes the Hanafi principle that for non-visible impurities, the number of washes (three) is a legislative determination based on the need to achieve certainty of purity, and the wringing ensures extraction of the absorbed impurity.

Key Terms
غير المرئية (ghayr al-mar'iyya): Non-visible impurity, such as dried urine or alcohol, which has no visible substance.
العَصْر (al-'asr): Wringing or squeezing the washed item to extract water containing impurity; required each time for non-visible impurity.
التتريب (al-tatrīb): Dusting with soil as part of purification for dog impurity; recommended (not obligatory) in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many washes with wringing are required for purifying a garment from non-visible impurity?
A) One wash with wringing
B) Three washes with wringing
C) Seven washes without wringing
D) Three washes without wringing

Key Takeaway
Three washes with wringing are obligatory (wajib) for purifying non-visible impurity, as established by the hadith of the awakened person.

Taharah — Purification

Washing Non-Visible Najasah: Three Washes with Wringing
Commentary on the ruling for washing non-visible najaasah three times with wringing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for purifying non-visible najasah from solid items, identify the evidence from the hadith of the waking person, and explain the requirement of wringing and its intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Three washes with wringing each time is wajib for non-visible najasah—the hadith establishes the principle.

Purifying objects from unseen impurity is a common need. The Hanafi school derives a clear method from a prophetic hadith, but the requirement of wringing and the number of washes involve internal scholarly discussion. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the operative position for practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is purifying a solid item from non-visible najasah. The ruling is that it must be washed three times, with wringing after each wash. This is wajib (obligatory), not fard, because the evidence is a hadith ahad.
The text says 'بغسلها ثلاثاً وجوباً' (by washing it three times obligatorily).
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence cited is the hadith of the waking person: 'When one of you wakes from sleep, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times.' This hadith is ahad but considered reliable.
The passage mentions 'لحديث المستيقظ' in reference to al-Mabsut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to wash three times after sleep, when the hand may have touched impurity during sleep, is used by analogy to general non-visible najasah. The repetition of three washes is taken as the required standard for removing such impurity.
The Hanafi school uses this as a general principle, not limited to the hand.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is disagreement about the number of washes: Abu Yusuf holds that a seventh wash makes it pure. Additionally, some non-zahir al-riwayah positions consider one wringing sufficient. The zahir al-riwayah insists on wringing each time.
The text says 'وعن أبي يوسف: إذا غسله سابعة طهر.'
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is three washes with wringing each time, as this is the zahir al-riwayah. However, the alternative of one wringing is considered 'awfaq' (more suitable) in some contexts.
The author states 'ظاهر الرواية' for the wringing condition, establishing its authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغير المرئية بغسلها ثلاثاً، والعَصْر كلّ مرّة..
الطهارة مع زوال الأثر، وإذا كان الغاسل صغيراً أو مجنوناً، فالعبرة لظن المستعمل، لأنـه هـو المحتاج إليه، وإن كان حديثاً كالفخار، يغسل ثلاث مرات ويجففه كل مرة بانقطاع التقاطر، وهذا تفسير الجفاف
في مثله، كالجلد المدبوغ بنجس، ولا يشترط اليبس. وقال في « الحاوي » : بحرق الجديد من الخزف
والأواني، وبغسل العتيق، وأواني الزجاج والرصاص الصقيل يمسح، والخشن يغسل، والخشب الجديد ينحت والقديم يغسل، واللحم المطبوخ بالنجس حالة الغليان لا يؤكل، لأنه يتشرب النجاسة، وإن لم يكن حالة الغليان، فإنه يغسل ثلاثاً، فيطهر، والمرقة لا خير فيها، والحنطة إذا طبخت بخـمـر قـال أبو يوسف: تطبخ ثلاثاً بالماء، وتجفف كل مرة، والفتوى على أنها لا تطهر أبداً، وهو قول أبي حنيفة، والحنطة المبلولة بالنجس حتى انتفخت تطهر بالطبخ ثلاثاً وتبرد في كل مرة، فإذا جفت وطحنـت حــل أكلها، إن لم ير فيها أثر النجاسة، والدجاجة التي أُغليت قبل إخراج أمعائها، إن بقيت إلى أن وصلت إلى الاستواء، لا تطهر أصلاً، وكذا الكرش قبل تطهيره لتشرب النجاسة، وأما إذا أُلقيت، بقدر انحلال المسام لنتف الريش، فتطهر بالغسل ثلاثاً. ذكره الزيلعي وغيره، أن السكين المموهة بالماء النجس، تمـوه السكين بالماء الطاهر ثلاث مرات. انتهى. ولو قيل: يكفي التمويه مرة، لكان وجيهاً، لأن النار تزيل أجزاء النجاسة بالكلية، والتكرار يزيل الشبهة. انتهى. والتمويه يطهر باطنها عند أبي يوسف، وعـلـيـه الفتوى، وإذا غسلت قبل التمويه بالماء ثلاثاً، طهر ظاهرها، حتى لو قطع بـهـا بطيخ لا يتنجس، ولـو حملها إنسان قبل تمويهها ثلاثاً بالطاهر، لا تجوز صلاته، اتفاقاً، (و)[ يطهر ] محل النجاسة (غير المرئية بغسلها ثلاثاً) وجوباً، وسبعاً مع التتريب ندباً في نجاسة الكلب خروجاً من الخلاف. (والعَصْر كلّ مرة) تقديراً لغلبة الظن فإنه المدار فأقيم السبب الظاهـر مـقـام غـلبـة الظن تيسيراً، ولأن التكرار لا بد منه للاستخراج. وفي « المبسوط » : لا يحكم بزوالها قبل الثلاث، لحديث المستيقظ (). وعن أبي يوسف: إذا غسله سابعة طهر.
And non-visible [najaasah is purified] by washing it three times, and wringing each time. Purification with the disappearance of the trace. If the washer is a child or insane, then consideration is given to the assumption of the user, because he is the one who needs it. If it is new like pottery, it is washed three times and dried each time by stopping the dripping, and this is the interpretation of drying in its likes, like tanned leather tanned with najaasah, and dryness is not required. Al-Hawi said: new pottery is burned and vessels [are burned], and old is washed, glass and polished lead vessels are wiped, rough ones are washed, new wood is scraped and old is washed, meat cooked with najaasah while boiling is not eaten because it absorbs the najaasah, and if not while boiling, then it is washed three times and becomes pure, and the broth has no good in it. Wheat cooked with wine: Abu Yusuf said: cook it three times with water and dry each time, but the fatwa is that it never becomes pure, and that is the saying of Abu Hanifa. Wheat moistened with najaasah until it swells becomes pure by cooking three times and cooling each time, and when it dries and is ground, eating it is permissible if no trace of najaasah is seen. A chicken that was boiled before removing its intestines: if it remains until it reaches doneness, it does not become pure at all, likewise the stomach before its purification due to absorption of najaasah. But if it is thrown [in water] to the extent of dissolving pores for plucking feathers, it becomes pure by washing three times. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned that the knife coated with najas water - coat the knife with pure water three times. End. If it is said that coating once is sufficient, it would be reasonable, because fire removes the parts of najaasah entirely, and repetition removes doubt. End. Coating purifies its inside according to Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is upon it. If it is washed before coating with water three times, its outside becomes pure, so that if one cuts a watermelon with it, it does not become najis. If a person carries it before coating it three times with pure [water], his prayer is not valid, by agreement. (And) it purifies the place of najaasah (non-visible) by washing it three times wajiban, and seven times with dusting recommendably in dog najaasah to exit from difference. (And wringing each time) by estimation for preponderance of assumption, for it is the standard, so the apparent cause was established in place of preponderance of assumption for facilitation, and because repetition is necessary for extraction. In al-Mabsut: It is not judged to have been removed before three, due to the hadith of the one who wakes up. From Abu Yusuf: If he washes it a seventh time, it becomes pure.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for purifying non-visible najaasah from solid objects like utensils, pottery, leather, etc. The author explains that washing three times with wringing each time is wajib, based on the hadith of the person who wakes up (who must wash his hand three times before dipping into the vessel). The wringing is meant to ensure removal of the impurity, and it is from zahir al-riwayah. However, an alternative view (from non-zahir al-riwayah) says one wringing suffices. The passage also discusses various cases: new pottery must be burned (according to al-Hawi), old pottery washed, glass wiped, etc. There is a dispute about wheat cooked with wine: Abu Yusuf says it can be purified by boiling and drying three times, but Abu Hanifa holds it never becomes pure, and the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. The principle is that the removal of najasah relies on the preponderance of assumption (ghalabat al-zann), so the three washes are a substitute. Abu Yusuf's view that a seventh wash purifies is also mentioned.

Key Terms
غير المرئية (ghayr al-mar'iyyah): Najasah that is not visible to the eye, either due to its nature or because it has been removed.
العصر (al-‘asr): Wringing or squeezing the washed item to extract water after each wash.
التمويه (al-tamwīh): Coating a blade or metal item with water to purify it by passing it through flame.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum number of washes required for a utensil with non-visible najaasah, and what must be done after each wash?
A) Three washes with wringing after each.
B) One wash without wringing.
C) Seven washes with wringing after each.
D) Three washes without wringing.

Key Takeaway
Three washes with wringing each time is the wajib standard for purifying solid items from non-visible najasah in the Hanafi school, based on hadith and established in zahir al-riwayah.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Purification by Istihalah (Transformation)
Definition and examples of istihalah as a method of purification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istihalah and list at least three examples from the Hanafi school where it renders a substance pure.

Core Idea
When a substance completely changes its essence, it becomes pure.

You know that najis items must be purified before use in worship. But what if the item itself changes into something entirely different? The Hanafi school recognizes this as purification through istihalah (transformation). This lesson examines the principle and its classical examples, helping you identify when a substance is legally pure due to essential change, not just washing.

Guided Path
1
Define istihalah
Understand the literal and legal meaning: it is the transformation of a substance into another with a different essence and properties.
2
Identify the condition for purification
The transformation must be complete, changing the substance's essence (dhāt) and not just its appearance or location.
3
List the examples given in the passage
Read each example: dead body to salt, feces to earth/ash, najis moisture burned off, sheep's head burned to remove blood, wine to vinegar, najis oil to soap. Note that in each case the original impurity no longer exists.
These are established cases in the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
If you encounter a new case, ask: has the substance's essence changed completely? If yes, it is pure through istihalah. If not, normal washing or disposal is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستحالة (۳) تطهر الأعيان النجسة كالميتة إذا صارت ملحاً، والعذرة تراباً أو رماداً. والبلة النجسة في التنور بالإحراق، ورأس الشاة إذا زال عنها الدم بالإحراق، والخمر إذا خللت كما لو تخللت، والزيت النجس إذا جعل صابوناً يطهر.
And istihalah (transformation) purifies najis substances, like a dead body when it becomes salt, and feces when it becomes earth or ash. And the najis moisture in the oven through burning, and the sheep's head when blood is removed from it through burning, and wine when it becomes vinegar, just as if it became vinegar, and najis oil when it is made into soap becomes pure.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage introduces the principle of istihalah (transformation) as a method of purification. When a najis substance undergoes a complete change in its essence and becomes a different substance, it becomes pure. The author gives several examples: a dead animal that turns into salt, feces that turn into earth or ash, najis moisture in an oven that is burned off, a sheep's head that is burned to remove blood, wine that turns into vinegar, and najis oil that is made into soap. These are all cases where the impurity is completely transformed and no longer exists. Note that this principle is accepted by the Hanafi school and is used for purification of certain items.

Key Terms
الاستحالة (al-istiḥālah): The transformation of a substance into another substance, changing its essence and properties.
العذرة (al-ʿadharah): Feces, specifically human or animal excrement.
التخليل (al-takhallul): The process of turning wine into vinegar, either naturally or through an additive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an example of istihalah?
A) A dead body turning into salt.
B) Feces turning into earth or ash.
C) Washing a najis garment with water.
D) Najis oil being made into soap.

Key Takeaway
A najis substance becomes pure only when its essence completely transforms into a different substance through istihalah, not through mere washing or dilution.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Purifying Najasah: Water and Pure Liquids
Ruling and proof for purifying najasah from cloth and body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying najasah from cloth and body, identify the conditions for valid purification (including wringing), and cite the Quranic and hadith proofs.

Core Idea
Najasah is removed by water or any pure liquid that can extract it; wringing is required unless running water.

This lesson explains the core Hanafi ruling that najasah (ritual impurity) on cloth and body is purified by water or any pure liquid that removes the impurity. The passage also addresses practical scenarios, such as forgetting the exact location of the impurity, the role of wringing, and the use of running water. We will examine the textual proofs from the Quran and Sunnah that establish the obligation of purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being purified (najasah on cloth/body) and the ruling: wajib (obligatory) to purify. Note that the purification is achieved by water or any pure liquid that removes the impurity.
The obligation is derived from the Quranic command in Surah al-Muddaththir and the hadith.
2
Understand the conditions and exceptions
Examine the conditions: wringing is required each time when washing in a vessel (zahir al-riwayah), but not in running water or for unwringable items. Also, if one forgets the exact spot, washing without taharri (seeking) is sufficient, but prayer must be repeated if the impurity is later found elsewhere.
The non-zahir riwayah holds that one wringing suffices, which is considered more suitable.
3
Analyze the proofs (dalil)
Identify the primary proofs: Quran 25:48 (water is purifying), Quran 74:4 (purify garments), and the hadith about washing menstrual blood. Understand the wajh al-dalalah (how each proof indicates the ruling).
The obligation for the garment is explicit from the text; for the body and place, it is by implication.
4
Recognize intra-Hanafi differences
Note the difference in riwayat regarding wringing: the zahir al-riwayah requires wringing each time, while another riwayah allows one wringing. Also, note the narration from Abu Yusuf about using oil to wash blood.
The chosen (mufta bihi) view on the number of wrings is the more convenient one.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Apply the ruling to common situations: washing clothes under a tap (no wringing needed), using antibacterial hand sanitizer (is it a pure liquid that removes impurity?), etc.
Remember that the liquid must be pure (tahir) and capable of removing the substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَطْهُرُ النّجاسَةُ عَنِ الثَّوبِ والبدنِ بالماء، وبكلّ مائع مزيل: . تنبيه: إذا نسي محل النجاسة، فغسل طرفاً من الثوب من غير تحر، حكم بطهارة الثوب عـلـى المختار، ولكن إذا صلى فيه، ثم ظهرت في محل آخر غير الذي غسله، يجب عليه إعادة الصلاة، كما في « الخلاصة»، واشتراط العصر في كل مرة، هو «ظاهر الرواية»، لأنه المستخرج للنجاسة، وفي غير رواية الأصول: يكتفي بالعصر مرة ، وهو أوفق، وهذا إذا غسله في إناء، أما إذا غمسه في الماء الجاري، فإنه يطهر، وكذا ما لا ينعصر ، ولا يشترط العصر، ولا التجفيف ولا تكرار الغمس، وكذا الإناء إذا جعل في النهر وملأه، وخرج منه طهر، والمياه الثلاثة متفاوتة في النجاسة، فالأولى يطهر ما أصابته بالغسل ثلاثاً، والثانية بثنتين، والثالثة بواحدة، وكذا الأواني الثلاثة التي غسل فيها واحدة بعد واحدة. وقيل: يطهر الإناء الثالث بمجرد الإراقة. والثاني بواحدة، والأول بثنتين، وتطهر النجاسة الحقيقية مرئية أو غير مرئية عن الثوب والبدن بالماء المطلق اتفاقاً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَنزَلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ ماء طهورا [الفرقان : ٤٨] وبالمستعمل على الصحيح. اعلم أن وجوب الطهارة بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَثِيَابَكَ فطر [المدير : ٤] أي: فطهرها من النجاسات، وما نقل مما هو خلاف ذلك في تفسيرها لا يوافق ظاهر اللغة، وبقوله : ( حنّيهِ ثُمَّ اقْرُصِيهِ، ثُمَّ اغْسِلِيْهِ بِالْمَاءِ » )، ( ونهى عليه الصلاة والســلام عـــن الصَّلاةِ في المَجْزَرَةِ والمَرْبَلَةِ » (۳) فقد ثبت وجوب تطهير الثوب بعبارة النص، والبدن والمكان بدلالة النص، وهذا لأن تطهير الثوب إما وجب لأن الصلاة مناجاة مع الله تعالى، فيجب أن يكون المصلي على أحسن الأحوال، وذلك في طهارته وطهارة ما يتصل به، وقد وجب عليه تطهير الثوب مع قصور اتصاله، لقيام الثوب به وقصور الصلاة بدونه في الجملة، فلا يجب عليه تطهير المكــان مــع كمال اتصاله، لقيامه به وعدم تصور الصلاة بدونه أولى. وتَطْهُرُ النّجاسة)، أي: محلها (عَنِ الثَّوبِ والبَدَنِ بالماء) على الصحيح (وَبِكُلِّ مَائِع) طاهر على الأصح. وقيل: إذا غسـل المـغـلـظ بمخفف صـار مخففاً، والصحيح بقاؤه على التغليظ، فلا يعفى عنه إلا قدر الدرهم، فلابد من طاهر (مزيل) احترز به عن الأدهان والعصير، فإنها لا تزيل لعدم خروجها بالعصر، وكذا اللبن، ولو مخيضـا عـلـى الصحيح، وروي عن أبي يوسف: لو غسل الدم من الثوب بدهن، أو سمن، أو زيت حتى ذهب أثره
And najaasah is purified from the garment and the body with water, and with every liquid that removes. Note: If one forgets the location of the najaasah, and washes a part of the garment without seeking [the exact spot], the garment is ruled pure according to the chosen view. But if he prays in it, then it appears in a place other than the one he washed, he must repeat the prayer, as in al-Khulasa. And the condition of wringing each time is from zahir al-riwayah, because it extracts the najaasah. In a non-principle riwayah, one wringing suffices, and it is more suitable. This is if he washes it in a vessel. But if he immerses it in running water, it becomes pure, likewise that which cannot be wrung; wringing, drying, and repetition of immersion are not required. Likewise, a vessel placed in a river, filled, and water exits, it becomes pure. The three waters differ in najaasah: the first purifies what it touches by three washes, the second by two, the third by one. Likewise, the three vessels in which [the item] is washed one after another. It is said that the third vessel is purified merely by pouring out [the water], the second by one [wash], the first by two. And real najaasah, visible or non-visible, is purified from the garment and body with absolute water by agreement due to His saying: 'And We sent down from the sky pure water' (al-Furqan 48). And with used [water] according to the correct view. Know that the obligation of purification is [proven] by His saying: 'And your garments purify' (al-Muddaththir 4), meaning purify them from najaasah, and what is reported contrary to that in its interpretation does not agree with the apparent language. And by His saying: 'Then wring it, then wash it with water' [hadith], and the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade praying in the slaughterhouse and garbage dump. So the obligation of purifying the garment is established by the text's wording, and the body and place by implication of the text. And this is because purification of the garment is obligatory because prayer is a private conversation with Allah, so the one praying must be in the best condition, and that is in his purity and the purity of what is connected to him. The purification of the garment is obligatory despite its limited connection, because the garment stands with him and prayer is generally not possible without it. So the purification of the place is even more obligatory due to its complete connection and the impossibility of prayer without it. And najaasah (i.e., its place) is purified from the garment and body with water according to the correct view, and with every liquid that is tahir according to the soundest view. It is said: if a heavy [najaasah] is washed with a light [liquid], it becomes light, but the correct view is that it remains heavy, so it is not excused except the amount of a dirham. So a purifying remover is necessary, which excludes oils and juice, because they do not remove due to their inability to be extracted by wringing, and likewise milk, even if churned, according to the correct view. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf: if blood is washed from a garment with oil, ghee, or olive oil until its trace disappears, [the garment becomes pure?]
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage begins with the matn statement that najaasah is purified from cloth and body by water and any tahir liquid that removes the impurity. The commentary then discusses several sub-points: (1) If one forgets the exact location of najaasah and washes part of the garment without aiming for the spot, the garment is ruled pure based on the chosen view, but if after prayer the najaasah is found elsewhere, prayer must be repeated. (2) The condition of wringing each time is from zahir al-riwayah; in another riwayah, one wringing suffices. (3) For running water or unwringable items, wringing is not required. (4) The three waters and three vessels in serial washing have different purifying capacities. (5) The dalil for purification is Quranic and Sunnah: verse 48 of al-Furqan for water, verse 4 of al-Muddaththir for garments, and the hadith about washing menstrual blood. (6) The obligation of purifying cloth is established directly, and by implication the body and place. (7) Purification with water is agreed upon; with other liquids, it is the correct view that they must be tahir and remove the impurity. Oils and milk do not qualify. (8) A narration from Abu Yusuf suggests that washing blood with oil may remove impurity, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
الماء المطلق (al-mā' al-muṭlaq): Absolute water that is naturally pure and purifying, not mixed with anything that alters its name.
المائع المزيل (al-mā'i' al-muzīl): Any liquid that is tahir (pure) and has the property to remove impurity, such as water.
التحري (al-taḥarrī): To seek and make an effort to determine the exact location of najaasah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is used as proof that water purifies najaasah from cloth and body?
A) Surah al-Muddaththir, verse 4
B) Surah al-Furqan, verse 48
C) Surah al-Ma'idah, verse 6
D) Surah al-Nisa', verse 43

Key Takeaway
Purification of najasah from cloth and body is wajib, established by Quran and Sunnah, and can be done with any pure liquid that removes the impurity, with wringing required in some cases.
