Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Purification of Najasah: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Details
States the general ruling on purification and intra-madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that najasah on garments and body is purified by water or any pure liquid that removes it, and identify the relied-upon position on wringing.

Core Idea
Any pure liquid that physically removes impurity purifies, with wringing once being sufficient.

This passage from Sharh Nur al-Idah lays out the general ruling on removing impurity (najasah) from garments and body. It clarifies that not only water but any pure liquid that effectively removes the impurity is valid, and discusses practical details like wringing and what to do if you forget the location of the impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'And impurity is purified from the garment and body by water, and by every liquid that is a remover.' This is the core ruling.
The phrase 'by every liquid that is a remover' is a key expansion beyond water.
2
Understand the exception for forgetting the spot
Note the clarification: if one forgets the location of impurity and washes without specification, the garment is considered pure on the chosen view, but if later found elsewhere, prayers repeated.
This shows the school's concern for practical certainty.
3
Analyze the wringing requirement
The text states that requiring wringing in every wash is the transmitted opinion (zahir al-riwayah), but a non-zahir narration allows one wringing, which is 'more appropriate'.
The tarjih (preference) is for one wringing, which is the mufta bihi.
4
Examine the scope of liquids
The text explicitly excludes oils, juice, and milk because they do not come out by wringing, but includes vinegar and rose water as examples of pure liquids that remove impurity.
The key is that the liquid must be a 'muzil' (remover).
5
Summarize the Hanafi position
Compile the ruling: purification with water is agreed; with other pure liquids it is valid on the relied-upon view; wringing once suffices; and the liquid must physically remove the impurity.
This position is supported by qiyas and the hadith of Aisha with saliva.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَطْهُرُ النّجاسَةُ عَنِ الثَّوبِ والبدنِ بالماء، وبكلّ مائع مزيل: . تنبيه: إذا نسي محل النجاسة، فغسل طرفاً من الثوب من غير تحر، حكم بطهارة الثوب عـلـى المختار، ولكن إذا صلى فيه، ثم ظهرت في محل آخر غير الذي غسله، يجب عليه إعادة الصلاة، كما في « الخلاصة»، واشتراط العصر في كل مرة، هو «ظاهر الرواية»، لأنه المستخرج للنجاسة، وفي غير رواية الأصول: يكتفي بالعصر مرة ، وهو أوفق، وهذا إذا غسله في إناء، أما إذا غمسه في الماء الجاري، فإنه يطهر، وكذا ما لا ينعصر ، ولا يشترط العصر، ولا التجفيف ولا تكرار الغمس، وكذا الإناء إذا جعل في النهر وملأه، وخرج منه طهر، والمياه الثلاثة متفاوتة في النجاسة، فالأولى يطهر ما أصابته بالغسل ثلاثاً، والثانية بثنتين، والثالثة بواحدة، وكذا الأواني الثلاثة التي غسل فيها واحدة بعد واحدة. وقيل: يطهر الإناء الثالث بمجرد الإراقة. والثاني بواحدة، والأول بثنتين، وتطهر النجاسة الحقيقية مرئية أو غير مرئية عن الثوب والبدن بالماء المطلق اتفاقاً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَنزَلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ ماء طهورا [الفرقان : ٤٨] وبالمستعمل على الصحيح. اعلم أن وجوب الطهارة بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَثِيَابَكَ فطِّرْ [المدثر : ٤] أي: فطهرها من النجاسات، وما نقل مما هو خلاف ذلك في تفسيرها لا يوافق ظاهر اللغة، وبقوله : « حنِّيهِ ثُمَّ اقْرُصِيهِ، ثُمَّ اغْسِلِيْهِ بِالْمَاءِ »، ونهى عليه الصلاة والســلام عـــن الصَّلاةِ في المَجْزَرَةِ والمَرْبَلَةِ فقد ثبت وجوب تطهير الثوب بعبارة النص، والبدن والمكان بدلالة النص، وهذا لأن تطهير الثوب إما وجب لأن الصلاة مناجاة مع الله تعالى، فيجب أن يكون المصلي على أحسن الأحوال، وذلك في طهارته وطهارة ما يتصل به، وقد وجب عليه تطهير الثوب مع قصور اتصاله، لقيام الثوب به وقصور الصلاة بدونه في الجملة، فلا يجب عليه تطهير المكــان مــع كمال اتصاله، لقيامه به وعدم تصور الصلاة بدونه أولى. وتَطْهُرُ النّجاسة)، أي: محلها (عَنِ الثَّوبِ والبَدَنِ بالماء) على الصحيح (وَبِكُلِّ مَائِع) طاهر على الأصح. وقيل: إذا غسـل المـغـلـظ بمخفف صـار مخففاً، والصحيح بقاؤه على التغليظ، فلا يعفى عنه إلا قدر الدرهم، فلابد من طاهر (مزيل) احترز به عن الأدهان والعصير، فإنها لا تزيل لعدم خروجها بالعصر، وكذا اللبن، ولو مخيضـا عـلـى الصحيح، وروي عن أبي يوسف: لو غسل الدم من الثوب بدهن، أو سمن، أو زيت حتى ذهب أثره، كالخل، وماء الورد. ويَطْهُرُ الخُفُّ، ونحوه، بالدَّلْكِ من نجاسة لها حِرْم، ولو كانت رَطْبةً.. جاز، فلا بد من مزيل، (كَالخَلّ ، وَمَاءِ الوَرْدِ) والمستخرج من البقول؛ لقول عائشة : « مـا كـانَ لإحدانا إلا ثوب واحـد تـحـيـض فيه، إذا أصابَهَا شيء من دم الحيض، بلتهُ بريقها، ثمَّ قَعَتْهُ بظفرهَا » أي: حكته، ولأن المائع مزيل بطبعه، فوجب أن يفيد الطهارة كالماء، لأنا نشاهد بالضرورة إزالته النجاسة شيئاً فشيئاً كل مرة، ويتغير لون المائع بالنجس، والنجاسة متناهية لتركبها من جواهر متناهية، فإذا انتهت أجزاؤها بقي المحل طاهراً، لعدم المجاورة فثبت أن زوالها معقول، والمائع مثل الماء في الإزالة وقلع النجاسة، فيعدى الحكم إليه، بخلاف الحدث، لأنه ليس في المحل نجاسة تزول بالمائع، لكن نجاسته حكمية خصت إزالتها بالماء بالنص، فلا يتعدى إلى غيره، لأنه أهون موجود لا يلحق حرج في إفساده باستعماله.
And impurity is purified from the garment and body by water, and by every liquid that is a remover. Note: If one forgets the location of impurity, and washes a part of the garment without specifying (taharri), the garment is ruled pure according to the chosen view. But if he prays in it and then the impurity appears in a different place than what he washed, he must repeat the prayer, as in al-Khulasa. And the requirement of wringing in every wash is the 'zahir al-riwayah' because it extracts the impurity. In a non-principle narration: it suffices to wring once, and that is more appropriate. This is if he washes it in a vessel. As for if he immerses it in running water, it becomes pure. Likewise, what does not allow wringing; neither wringing, nor drying, nor repeated immersion is required. Similarly, a vessel when placed in the river, filled, and its water comes out, it becomes pure. The three waters differ in impurity: the first purifies what it touches by washing three times, the second by two times, the third by one time. And likewise the three vessels washed one after another. It is said: the third vessel is purified by merely pouring out (the water), the second by one wash, and the first by two washes. And the real impurity (najasa haqiqiyya), whether visible or invisible, is purified from the garment and body by absolute water (ma' mutlaq) by agreement, due to His statement: 'And We sent down from the sky pure water' [Al-Furqan 25:48], and by used water (al-musta'mal) according to the correct view. Know that the obligation of purification (tahara) is by His statement: 'And your garments purify' [Al-Muddaththir 74:4], meaning: purify them from impurities. And what is reported contrary to that in its interpretation does not agree with the apparent language. And by his statement: 'Wring it, then rinse it, then wash it with water.' And he (peace and blessings be upon him) forbade prayer in the slaughterhouse and the dung heap. Thus the obligation to purify the garment is established by the wording of the text, and the body and place by implication of the text. This is because the purification of the garment is obligatory because prayer is a private conversation with Allah, so the one praying should be in the best condition, which is in his purity and the purity of what is connected to him. And the purification of the garment is obligatory despite the imperfection of its connection, because the garment stands with him and prayer is generally impossible without it. So the purification of the place is even more worth not being obligatory despite its complete connection, because it stands with him and prayer is conceivable without it. And 'impurity is purified' meaning its location, 'from the garment and body' by water according to the correct view, 'and by every liquid' that is pure according to the most correct view. It was said: if one washes a heavy impurity (mughallaz) with a light one (mukhaffaf), it becomes light; but the correct view is that it remains heavy, so it is not excused except the size of a dirham. So a pure remover is necessary. He restricted it to a remover (muzil) to exclude oils and juice, for they do not remove, because they do not come out by wringing. Likewise milk, even if churned, according to the correct view. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf: if one washes blood from a garment with oil, or ghee, or olive oil until its trace disappears, like vinegar and rose water. And the khuff and the like is purified by rubbing (dalk) from an impurity that has mass, even if it was wet... it is permissible, so a remover is necessary, like vinegar and rose water, and what is extracted from vegetables, due to the statement of Aisha: 'One of us used to have only one garment which she would wear during menstruation; if any blood got on it, she would wet it with her saliva, then scratch it with her nail.' i.e., rub it. And because a liquid is a remover by its nature, so it must effect purification like water, because we observe by necessity that it removes impurity little by little each time, and the color of the liquid changes with the impurity, and the impurity is finite because it is composed of finite substances, so when its parts end, the location remains pure, due to lack of adjacency. So it is established that its removal is rational. And the liquid is like water in removal and extraction of impurity, so the ruling is extended to it, unlike hadath, because in the location there is no impurity that can be removed by liquid; rather its impurity is hukmi, and its removal has been specified to water by clear text, so it does not extend to other than water, because it is the least available and no difficulty is involved in using it up.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah deals with the general ruling on purifying from najasah (impurity). It states that najasah can be removed from garments and body by water or any pure liquid that effectively removes the impurity. The author first gives a note about forgetting where the impurity is: if you wash a part without specifying, the entire garment is ruled pure on the chosen view, but if you later find impurity elsewhere, you must repeat any prayers done. The passage then discusses the requirement of wringing (asr) when washing in a vessel: the zahir al-riwayah requires wringing each time because it extracts the impurity, but a non-zahir narration says one wringing suffices, which is more practical. For running water, no wringing is needed. For three waters used sequentially, the degree of purification varies. Next, the author provides proof for the obligation of purification from the Quran (verse 74:4 and 25:48) and hadith. He explains that the obligation to purify the garment is from the explicit text, while the body and place are from implication. The discussion then moves to purification by liquids other than water: it is correct that any pure liquid that removes impurity (like vinegar, rose water) can purify, based on the hadith of Aisha and rational analogy (qiyas) because liquid removes impurity based on experience. The author distinguishes from hadath (ritual impurity) which cannot be removed by liquids other than water due to specific textual evidence. The relied upon view is that such liquids purify, though some say they only lighten the impurity. The passage ends with the special case of khuffs being purified by rubbing with a liquid like vinegar.

Key Terms
مائع مزيل (ma'i' muzil): A liquid that is itself an inherent remover of impurity, i.e., it has the property of removing najasah through washing and wringing.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted opinion from the early Hanafi masters (Imam Muhammad's six books), representing the authoritative position of the school.
عصر (asr): Wringing out the water after washing to extract the impurity from the fabric.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone washes a part of a garment without specification (taharri) to remove najasah, then prays, and later discovers the impurity in a different spot?
A) The prayer is valid because the garment was ruled pure.
B) The prayer must be repeated.
C) The garment remains impure and must be washed completely.
D) Only the discovered spot must be rewased.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that najasah is purified by any pure liquid that effectively removes it, with wringing once being sufficient.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
7 min
Evidence for Purification with Liquids: Textual and Rational Proofs
Provides the proof structure: primary texts, qiyas, and distinction from hadath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to cite the Quranic verses and hadith proofs for purification from najasah, explain how the Hanafi school extends the ruling to liquids other than water via qiyas, and distinguish from the case of hadath.

Core Idea
Quran and Sunnah prove purification; qiyas extends to other pure liquids, but hadath is restricted to water by text.

This passage provides the foundation for the ruling on purification. It cites direct Quranic commands and hadith instructions, then uses rational analogy (qiyas) to extend the ruling to liquids other than water, while carefully excluding the case of hadath (ritual impurity) which has its own specific evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary texts
Locate the Quranic verses: Al-Furqan 25:48 (sending down purifying water) and Al-Muddaththir 74:4 (command to purify garments). Also the hadith: 'Wring it, then rinse it, then wash it with water' and the prohibition of prayer in unclean places.
These texts establish the obligation and method of purification.
2
Understand the implication for body and place
The author argues that the verse 'purify your garments' implies purification of body and place by textual indication (dalalat al-nass) because prayer requires overall cleanliness.
This is a Hanafi usul principle: the text about the garment implies broader application.
3
Analyze the qiyas for liquids
The author draws an analogy: liquids like vinegar and rose water remove impurity just as water does; we observe removal; impurity is finite; so the ruling is extended.
The key points: liquids are inherently removers (muzil), and impurity is composed of finite substances that can be fully removed.
4
Exclude hadath
Hadath (ritual impurity) is distinguished: it is not a physical impurity that can be removed by liquids; it is hukmi (legal), and its removal is restricted to water by explicit text.
This shows the school's precision: qiyas works for physical impurity but not for legal impurity.
5
Synthesize the proof structure
Combine all evidences: Quran and Sunnah provide the obligation and method; qiyas extends to similar cases; the restriction for hadath is based on textual specificity.
This demonstrates a complete Hanafi istinbat (derivation) method.

Source Text

حرج في إفساده باستعماله.
And impurity is purified from the garment and body by water, and by every liquid that is a remover. Note: If one forgets the location of impurity, and washes a part of the garment without specifying (taharri), the garment is ruled pure according to the chosen view. But if he prays in it and then the impurity appears in a different place than what he washed, he must repeat the prayer, as in al-Khulasa. And the requirement of wringing in every wash is the 'zahir al-riwayah' because it extracts the impurity. In a non-principle narration: it suffices to wring once, and that is more appropriate. This is if he washes it in a vessel. As for if he immerses it in running water, it becomes pure. Likewise, what does not allow wringing; neither wringing, nor drying, nor repeated immersion is required. Similarly, a vessel when placed in the river, filled, and its water comes out, it becomes pure. The three waters differ in impurity: the first purifies what it touches by washing three times, the second by two times, the third by one time. And likewise the three vessels washed one after another. It is said: the third vessel is purified by merely pouring out (the water), the second by one wash, and the first by two washes. And the real impurity (najasa haqiqiyya), whether visible or invisible, is purified from the garment and body by absolute water (ma' mutlaq) by agreement, due to His statement: 'And We sent down from the sky pure water' [Al-Furqan 25:48], and by used water (al-musta'mal) according to the correct view. Know that the obligation of purification (tahara) is by His statement: 'And your garments purify' [Al-Muddaththir 74:4], meaning: purify them from impurities. And what is reported contrary to that in its interpretation does not agree with the apparent language. And by his statement: 'Wring it, then rinse it, then wash it with water.' And he (peace and blessings be upon him) forbade prayer in the slaughterhouse and the dung heap. Thus the obligation to purify the garment is established by the wording of the text, and the body and place by implication of the text. This is because the purification of the garment is obligatory because prayer is a private conversation with Allah, so the one praying should be in the best condition, which is in his purity and the purity of what is connected to him. And the purification of the garment is obligatory despite the imperfection of its connection, because the garment stands with him and prayer is generally impossible without it. So the purification of the place is even more worth not being obligatory despite its complete connection, because it stands with him and prayer is conceivable without it. And 'impurity is purified' meaning its location, 'from the garment and body' by water according to the correct view, 'and by every liquid' that is pure according to the most correct view. It was said: if one washes a heavy impurity (mughallaz) with a light one (mukhaffaf), it becomes light; but the correct view is that it remains heavy, so it is not excused except the size of a dirham. So a pure remover is necessary. He restricted it to a remover (muzil) to exclude oils and juice, for they do not remove, because they do not come out by wringing. Likewise milk, even if churned, according to the correct view. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf: if one washes blood from a garment with oil, or ghee, or olive oil until its trace disappears, like vinegar and rose water. And the khuff and the like is purified by rubbing (dalk) from an impurity that has mass, even if it was wet... it is permissible, so a remover is necessary, like vinegar and rose water, and what is extracted from vegetables, due to the statement of Aisha: 'One of us used to have only one garment which she would wear during menstruation; if any blood got on it, she would wet it with her saliva, then scratch it with her nail.' i.e., rub it. And because a liquid is a remover by its nature, so it must effect purification like water, because we observe by necessity that it removes impurity little by little each time, and the color of the liquid changes with the impurity, and the impurity is finite because it is composed of finite substances, so when its parts end, the location remains pure, due to lack of adjacency. So it is established that its removal is rational. And the liquid is like water in removal and extraction of impurity, so the ruling is extended to it, unlike hadath, because in the location there is no impurity that can be removed by liquid; rather its impurity is hukmi, and its removal has been specified to water by clear text, so it does not extend to other than water, because it is the least available and no difficulty is involved in using it up.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah deals with the general ruling on purifying from najasah (impurity). It states that najasah can be removed from garments and body by water or any pure liquid that effectively removes the impurity. The author first gives a note about forgetting where the impurity is: if you wash a part without specifying, the entire garment is ruled pure on the chosen view, but if you later find impurity elsewhere, you must repeat any prayers done. The passage then discusses the requirement of wringing (asr) when washing in a vessel: the zahir al-riwayah requires wringing each time because it extracts the impurity, but a non-zahir narration says one wringing suffices, which is more practical. For running water, no wringing is needed. For three waters used sequentially, the degree of purification varies. Next, the author provides proof for the obligation of purification from the Quran (verse 74:4 and 25:48) and hadith. He explains that the obligation to purify the garment is from the explicit text, while the body and place are from implication. The discussion then moves to purification by liquids other than water: it is correct that any pure liquid that removes impurity (like vinegar, rose water) can purify, based on the hadith of Aisha and rational analogy (qiyas) because liquid removes impurity based on experience. The author distinguishes from hadath (ritual impurity) which cannot be removed by liquids other than water due to specific textual evidence. The relied upon view is that such liquids purify, though some say they only lighten the impurity. The passage ends with the special case of khuffs being purified by rubbing with a liquid like vinegar.

Key Terms
مائع مزيل (ma'i' muzil): A liquid that is itself an inherent remover of impurity, i.e., it has the property of removing najasah through washing and wringing.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted opinion from the early Hanafi masters (Imam Muhammad's six books), representing the authoritative position of the school.
عصر (asr): Wringing out the water after washing to extract the impurity from the fabric.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key hadith used by the Hanafi school to extend purification to liquids other than water?
A) The hadith of wringing and washing with water.
B) The hadith of Aisha using saliva to remove menstrual blood.
C) The prohibition of prayer in dung heaps.
D) The verse 'And we sent down pure water.'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi proof for purification with liquids rests on Quranic obligation, hadith demonstration, and qiyas (analogy) based on the observable removal of impurity.

Purification by Saliva and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on purification by saliva and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on purification by saliva, identify the opposing views of Imam Muhammad and one narration from Abu Yusuf, and explain the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Saliva can purify specific body parts in the Hanafi school, but Imam Muhammad and one report from Abu Yusuf require water.

In the Hanafi school, there are exceptional cases where purification does not require water. This lesson examines the ruling on cleansing the breast, finger, or mouth from najasah using saliva. We will see that although the default ruling requires water, a dispensation exists due to necessity, and we will explore the disagreement within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is being purified and how? The passage mentions three cases: a breast after a child vomits (cleaned by suckling), a finger (cleaned by licking), and a wine drinker's mouth (cleaned by swishing saliva). In each case, the purification is by saliva.
Each case involves a body part that is naturally cleaned by saliva.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the relied-upon view, saliva is a valid purifier in these specific cases. Therefore, if a person prays after such purification, the prayer is valid.
The text says: 'so that if he prays, his prayer is valid.'
3
Identify the opposing view
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and one narration from Abu Yusuf hold that saliva does not purify because water is required. According to them, the prayer would be invalid.
The passage states: 'but according to Muhammad, it is not valid and purification by saliva is not ruled due to the requirement of water, and this is one of the two narrations from Abu Yusuf.'
4
Analyze the basis of the disagreement
The disagreement stems from whether the general requirement of water for purification (istishrat al-ma') applies to these exceptional cases. The first view relies on necessity (umum al-balwa) and istihsan, while the second clings to the general rule.
No explicit proof is given in the passage; the reasoning is inferred.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the first view: purification by saliva is effective, and prayer is valid. The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that if someone prays after using saliva, according to the first view the prayer is valid, but according to Muhammad it must be repeated.
The author presents the first view as the default, indicating it is relied upon.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific issue of purification using saliva. The author presents the relied-upon Hanafi view that saliva can purify certain body parts that come into contact with najasah, such as a baby's vomit on the breast (cleaned by suckling), a finger licked clean, or a wine drinker's mouth swished with saliva. According to this view, prayer performed after such purification is valid. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the companion of Abu Hanifa) and one of two reports from Abu Yusuf hold that saliva cannot effect purification because it is not water; rather, water is required. This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that if a person prays after cleaning with saliva, the prayer is valid according to the first view, but must be repeated according to Muhammad's view. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the first view, as it is presented as the primary ruling and supported by the phrase 'his prayer is valid.' The passage does not provide explicit proofs but implies the principle of necessity (umum al-balwa) or istihsan for allowing saliva.

Key Terms
ريق (riq): Saliva; used as a means of purification in these specific cases.
إحدى الروايتين (ihda al-riwayatayn): One of the two transmitted reports from Abu Yusuf. Indicates ikhtilaf within the school on this point.

Purification of Khuff by Rubbing: Wet Solid Impurity
Ruling on purifying leather socks by rubbing with earth, supported by hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying a khuff from wet solid impurity by rubbing and cite the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A khuff is purified by rubbing on the ground even if the impurity is wet — due to hadith and general need.

In this passage, the author presents the Hanafi position that leather socks (khuffs) and similar foot coverings can be purified by rubbing (dalk) on the ground, even when the impurity is solid and still wet. This ruling, preferred for fatwa by later Hanafi scholars, is supported by multiple hadiths and the principle of general need. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the reasoning behind this practical concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being purified? Khuff or similar foot coverings. What method? Rubbing (dalk) on the ground or earth. What condition of impurity? It has mass (jirm) and may be wet. The ruling: purification is valid (sahih).
The passage explicitly states 'even if it is wet'.
2
Locate the dalil
Three hadiths are cited: (1) 'When one of you comes to the mosque… let him wipe his sandals.' (2) 'When one of you steps on filth with his khuffs, their purification is the earth.' (3) 'Whoever wants to enter the mosque… let him wipe them on the ground, for the ground is purification for them.'
All are from Abu Dawud, some authenticated by Ibn Hibban and al-Hakim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadiths command wiping (مسح) and state that earth is purification (طهور). They do not differentiate between dry and wet impurity. The command is general, so it includes wet impurity.
Hanafi usul: an imperative (amr) indicates obligation unless contextualized.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) generally require washing if the impurity is wet, arguing that water is the primary purifier. The Hanafi response is that the hadiths are specific to khuffs and general need (umum al-balwa) supports the easier ruling.
The passage says 'al-mukhtar lil-fatwa' indicates this is the chosen view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author states 'al-mukhtar lil-fatwa wa 'alayhi akthar al-mashayikh' — this is the operative view for fatwa among later Hanafis. The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin.
This ruling is not from the zahir al-riwayah but from later jurisprudence.

And the khuff and the like (e.g., a sandal) is purified with water and with a liquid by rubbing against the ground or earth from an impurity that has mass, even if it acquired mass from something else, as is correct, like dust or ash that hit the khuff before it dried from a liquid impurity, and even if it (the solid impurity) is wet, according to the chosen view for fatwa and accepted by most mashayikh, due to general need and due to the general application of the hadith, which is his statement: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees on his sandals any filth or dirt, let him wipe them and pray in them.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and Ibn Hibban in his Sahih. And his statement: 'When one of you steps on filth with his khuffs, their purification is the earth.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim and he authenticated it. And his statement: 'Whoever wants to enter the mosque, let him turn over his sandals; if he sees on them any filth, let him wipe them on the ground, for the ground is purification for them.' Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on purification by presenting the ruling for khuffs (leather socks) and similar foot coverings. The author states that such items are purified by rubbing (dalk) against the ground or using earth, even when the impurity is solid (has mass) and even if it is still wet. This is the chosen position for fatwa (al-mukhtar lil-fatwa) and is adopted by most later Hanafi scholars due to widespread need (umum al-balwa). The evidence comes from three hadiths: (1) the Prophet (peace be upon him) told a man entering the mosque to look at his sandals and wipe them if dirty; (2) 'When one of you steps on filth with his khuffs, their purification is the earth'; (3) a hadith about turning over sandals and wiping them on the ground. The author notes that this ruling is specific to khuffs and sandals, not to garments or carpets, which require washing. The passage ends incomplete, but the intended point is clear: the ground acts as a purifier for khuffs via rubbing, even for wet impurity.

Key Terms
دلك (dalk): Rubbing or wiping the khuff against the ground or earth to remove impurity.
جرم (jirm): The physical body or mass of the impurity, i.e., visible and tangible, as opposed to moisture only.
المختار للفتوى (al-mukhtar lil-fatwa): The chosen or preferred ruling for issuing legal verdicts (fatwa), considered operative in the school.

Purification by Any Liquid: The Hanafi Position
Proves that any liquid removing najasah is a purifier with hadith and rational argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that any liquid removing najasah purifies, identify the hadith proof, and explain the distinction from hadath.

Core Idea
Any liquid that physically removes najasah purifies—only water removes ritual impurity.

You might assume that only water can make things pure. But the Hanafi school holds that any liquid—vinegar, rose water, even saliva—can remove physical impurity (najāsah) as long as it effectively cleans. This lesson examines the proof from the hadith of ʿĀʾishah and the rational analogy to water, and clarifies why ritual impurity (ḥadath) remains water-only.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? It is the purification of surfaces (like khuff, garment, breast) from physical najāsah using liquids like vinegar or rose water. The ruling is that such purification is valid.
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are given: (1) the hadith of ʿĀʾishah (ra) who used saliva to remove menstrual blood, and (2) rational observation that liquids remove impurity gradually until it is gone.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
From the hadith: ʿĀʾishah's action of moistening the stain with saliva and scraping it shows that any effective liquid can purify. From reason: liquids remove impurity by nature, analogous to water.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybānī holds that saliva does not purify because water is required; Abu Yusuf has two narrations. The relied-upon position is that saliva does purify, as prayer after such cleansing is valid.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The majority of mashāyikh adopt the broader view, supported by the hadith and analogy, and the prayer is accepted. This is the muftā bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويَطْهُرُ الخُفُّ، ونحوه، بالدَّلْكِ من نجاسة لها حِرْم، ولو كانت رَطْبةً.. جاز، فلا بد من مزيل، (كَالخَلّ ، وَمَاءِ الوَرْدِ) والمستخرج من البقول؛ لقول عائشة : « مـا كـانَ لإحدانا إلا ثوب واحـد تـحـيـض فيه، إذا أصابَهَا شيء من دم الحيض، بلتهُ بريقها، ثمَّ قَعَتْهُ بظفرهَا » (١) أي: حكته، ولأن المائع مزيل بطبعه، فوجب أن يفيد الطهارة كالماء، لأنا نشاهد بالضرورة إزالته النجاسة شيئاً فشيئاً كل مرة، ويتغير لون المائع بالنجس، والنجاسة متناهية لتركبها من جواهر متناهية، فإذا انتهت أجزاؤها بقي المحل طاهراً، لعدم المجاورة فثبت أن زوالها معقول، والمائع مثل الماء في الإزالة وقلع النجاسة، فيعدى الحكم إليه، بخلاف الحدث، لأنه ليس في المحل نجاسة تزول بالمائع، لكن نجاسته حكمية خصت إزالتها بالماء بالنص، فلا يتعدى إلى غيره، لأنه أهون موجود لا يلحق حرج في إفساده باستعماله. ويطهر الثدي إذا قـاء عـليـه الـولـد ثـم رضعه حتى زال الأثر، ويطهر الإصبع بلحسه من نجاسة حتى ذهب أثرها بالريق، وفم شارب الخمر ونحوه بترديد ريقه فيه مراراً، حتى لو صلى صحت صلاته وعلى قول محمد: لا تصح ولا يحكم بالطهارة بالريق لاشتراطه الماء، وهو إحدى الروايتين عن أبي يوسف.
And the khuff and the like is purified by rubbing from an impurity that has body, even if it is wet... it is permissible, so there must be a remover, (like vinegar, and rose water) and what is extracted from herbs; due to the saying of Aisha: 'None of us had except one garment in which she menstruated; when something of menstrual blood stained it, she would moisten it with her saliva, then scrape it with her nail' i.e., rub it. And because a liquid is a remover by its nature, so it must effect purification like water, for we observe by necessity that it removes impurity bit by bit each time, and the color of the liquid changes with the impure, and the impurity is finite because it is composed of finite particles; when its parts end, the place remains pure due to lack of contiguity; so it is established that its removal is reasonable. And a liquid is like water in removal and extraction of impurity, so the ruling is extended to it, unlike hadath (ritual impurity), because there is no impurity in the place that can be removed by liquid; rather its impurity is legal (hukmiyyah), and its removal is specified to water by text, so it does not extend to other than it, because water is the easiest available substance and there is no hardship in using it. And the breast is purified if the child vomits on it then suckles until the effect is gone, and the finger is purified by licking it from impurity until its effect is removed by saliva, and the mouth of a wine drinker and the like by repeatedly moving his saliva in it until if he prays, his prayer is valid. And according to Muhammad's saying: it is not valid and purification by saliva is not ruled due to the requirement of water, and this is one of two narrations from Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The author establishes that any liquid (mā'i') that physically removes najāsah can purify the surface, analogized to water. The proof is the hadith of Aisha (ra) who used saliva to remove menstrual blood from her garment, and rational argument that liquids remove impurity gradually until it is gone. The difference between physical najāsah (which has particles) and ritual impurity (ḥadath) is highlighted: ḥadath is only removed by water because it is a legal construct. Three practical examples are given: a child's vomit on the breast, a finger licked clean, and a wine drinker's mouth. A dispute is noted: Muhammad al-Shaybānī holds that saliva does not purify because water is required; Abu Yusuf has two narrations. The relied-upon position is that saliva can purify in these cases, as the prayer is valid if one prays after such cleansing.

Key Terms
المائع (al-mā'i'): A liquid that can physically remove impurity, like vinegar, rose water, or plant extracts.
الحدث (al-ḥadath): Ritual impurity (minor or major) that is not a physical substance but a legal state requiring wuḍū' or ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Any liquid that physically removes najāsah purifies; only water removes ḥadath.

Taharah — Purification

Purification of Khuff by Rubbing (Dalk)
Ruling on purifying khuff by rubbing, with conditions and hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying a khuff by rubbing, identify the condition that the impurity must have jirm, and apply the hadith proofs to justify permissibility even when the impurity is wet.

Core Idea
Rubbing on the ground purifies the khuff from any impurity with a physical body, even if wet, due to authentic hadith and widespread need.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that a khuff (leather sock) or similar solid footwear can be purified by simply rubbing it on the ground, without needing water. The passage explains the condition that the impurity must have a tangible body (jirm) and cites three hadiths to support this practice. It also clarifies exceptions for porous materials and the body. Understanding this ruling is essential for daily practice, especially when water is unavailable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act being ruled is the purification of a khuff (and similar items like sandals) from impurity. The ruling is that rubbing (dalk) on the ground or dust is sufficient, using water or other liquids. The khuff becomes pure by this method.
2
Locate the dalil
Three hadiths are cited: the first commands looking at sandals and wiping them before prayer; the second states that purification of khuff is by earth; the third explicitly states that wiping on the ground is sufficient because the earth is a purifier.
The hadiths are from Abu Dawud, Ibn Hibban, and Al-Hakim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to wipe (yamsah) indicates that rubbing on the ground removes impurity. The phrase 'the earth is a purification for them' (al-arḍu lahumā ṭahūr) directly establishes the suitability of earth for purification without water.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text notes a difference of opinion regarding wet impurity; the chosen position (mukhtār li al-fatwa) and majority of mashāyikh allow rubbing even when the impurity is wet, due to widespread need and the general applicability of the hadith.
Some earlier scholars may have required washing for wet impurities.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The muftā bihi (operative ruling) is that rubbing works for both dry and wet impurities on khuff. The tarjih is based on the general application of the hadith and the principle of 'umūm al-balwā (widespread need).
The text explicitly says 'on the chosen for fatwa and the majority of the mashāyikh'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَيَظْهرُ الخُفُّ ونحوه ) كالنعل بالماء وبالمائع (بالدَّلْكِ) بالأرض أو التراب (من نجاسة لها جرم)، ولو مكتسباً من غيرهـا على الصحيح، كتراب أو رماد أصاب الخف قبل جفافه . من نجاسة مائعة، ولو كَانَتْ) المتجسدة، من أصلها، أو باكتسابها الجرم من غيرها (رَطْبةً) على المختار للفتوى وعليه أكثر المشايخ، لعموم البلوى، ولإطلاق الحديث، وهو قوله : ( إذَا جَاءَ أَحَدُكُمُ الْمَسْجِدِ، فَلْيَنْظُرْ فَإِنْ رَأى فِي نَعْلَيْهِ أَذًى أَوْ قَدَرَاً فَلْيَمْسَحْهَما ، وَلْيُصَلِّ فِيهِمَا ) (۳). رواه أبو داود وابن حبان في صحيحه. ولقولـه عـلـيـه الصـلاة والسلام: ( إِذَا وَطِئَ أَحَدُكُمُ الأَذَى بِخُفَّيْهِ، فَطَهُورُهُمَا الشَّرَابُ ). رواه أبو داود والحاكم وصححه. وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (فَمَنْ أرَادَ أَنْ يَدْخُلَ المَسْجِدَ ، فَلَيَقْلِبْ نَعْلَيْهِ، فَإِنْ رَأَى بِهِمَا أَذَى، فليمسحهما بالأرض، فإنَّ الأرضَ لهما طهور ) (). وقيد بالخف احترازاً عن الثوب، والبساط، لأن لهما تخلخلاً، فيتداخلهما النجاسة، واحترازاً عن البدن، للينه ورطوبته، إلا في المني، فإنه يطهر بالفرك عنه.
And the khuff and the like, like the n'al (sandal), are purified by water and by liquid, by rubbing on the ground or dust, from an impurity that has body, even if it is acquired from other than it according to the correct view, such as dust or ash that hit the khuff before it dried, from a liquid impurity, even if it (the impurity) is congealed, originally or by acquiring body from other than it, (whether) wet, according to the chosen for fatwa and the majority of the mashayikh, due to the widespread need and the general application of the hadith, which is his saying: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees on his sandals any harm or filth, let him wipe them, and let him pray in them.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and Ibn Hibban in his Sahih. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'When one of you steps on harm with his khuff, then their purification is the earth.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and Al-Hakim and he declared it authentic. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever wants to enter the mosque, let him turn over his sandals; if he sees on them any harm, let him wipe them on the ground, for the ground is a purification for them.' And it is restricted by khuff as a precaution against cloth and mat, because they have looseness, so impurity penetrates them, and as a precaution against the body, due to its softness and moisture, except for semen, for it is purified by rubbing from it.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed ruling on purifying the khuff (leather socks) and similar items like sandals. The method is rubbing (dalk) on the ground or dust, using either water or any liquid. The condition is that the impurity must have a physical body (jirm), even if it acquired body from something else (like dust). The impurity can be dry or wet; the chosen fatwa permits rubbing even when wet, due to the general applicability of the hadiths. Three hadiths are cited, two from Abu Dawud, one from Ibn Hibban and Al-Hakim. The ruling is restricted to khuff and similar (like n'al) to exclude cloth and mat (which are porous and absorb impurity) and also the body (except semen, which is purified by rubbing). The body is excluded due to its softness and moisture, except semen which can be removed by rubbing.

Key Terms
الدَّلْك (al-dalk): Rubbing or wiping on the ground or dust to remove impurity, not necessarily with water.
الخُفّ (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the foot, typically made of thick leather.
جِرْم (jirm): Physical body or substance of the impurity, as opposed to traces that are not substantial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The impurity must be dry
B) The impurity must have a physical body (jirm)
C) The impurity must be from urine only
D) The khuff must be made of leather

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that khuff and similar solid footwear are purified by rubbing on the ground from any impurity with a physical body, even when wet, based on authentic hadith and the principle of widespread need.

Purification of Polished Surfaces by Wiping: Hanafi Rulings and Disputes
Ruling on purifying sword, mirror, and similar shiny surfaces by wiping, with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying polished surfaces by wiping, identify the disputed points between al-Isbijābī and al-Qudūrī, and apply the preferred view (al-Karkhī) to concrete scenarios.

Core Idea
Polished surfaces are purified by wiping; the preferred view is that wiping gives actual purity, even for wet urine and feces.

This passage from Imdād al-Fattāḥ addresses a practical question: how to purify non-porous items like swords, mirrors, and knives. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that recognizes wiping (masḥ) as sufficient for polished surfaces, based on the practice of the Companions. However, there is an internal dispute about whether wiping achieves actual purification or merely reduces impurity, and another about whether it applies to all impurities. This lesson will walk you through the ruling, the disputes, and the operative position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items covered by the ruling
First, recognize which items are included: swords, mirrors, knives, and any polished (ṣaqīl) non-porous surface. Excluded are rough, rusty, engraved items, and porous materials like wood and ebony.
ṣaqīl means polished to a shine, not merely smooth.
2
Understand the method and its effect
The method is wiping with dust, cloth, or wool. Two views exist: one (al-Isbijābī) says wiping gives actual purity, another (al-Qudūrī) says it only reduces impurity (taqlīl). Trace how each view affects permissibility: actual purity allows eating food cut with the sword; taqlīl does not.
The text sides with al-Isbijābī for actual purification.
3
Examine the dalīl: Companion practice
The proof cited is the action of the Companions who wiped their swords after battle and prayed with them. This is a Sunnah (ahad) and establishes the principle for swords; the ruling is extended to all polished surfaces via the underlying cause (ʿillah): non-absorption.
The dalīl is zannī in both transmission and indication, but strengthened by istiḥsān.
4
Analyze the dispute on impurities
Note the difference between al-Aṣl (Imam Muhammad) and al-Karkhī: al-Aṣl requires washing for urine and blood, while al-Karkhī extends wiping to all impurities, even wet urine and feces. The chosen fatwa follows al-Karkhī due to the generality of the ʿillah and Companion practice.
This is a later fatwa (nawādir) that takes precedence for practical application.
5
Apply to analogous cases
The passage mentions similar cases: rubbed semen, dried earth, tanned hide, well water returning, bricks removed. Understand the principle: non-absorbent surfaces can be purified by removal of impurity, not necessarily by water.
These analogies reinforce the same underlying rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويَطْهُرُ السَّيفُ ونحوه بالمسح. ويَظْهرُ السَّيفُ ونحوه من الحديد والصقيل، كالمرآة والسكين، واحترزنـا بـه عـن الخشن والمصدي والمنقوش، وكذا الصفر والأواني المدهونة، والخشب الخرائطي، والآبنوس) ونحوه والظفر، لأنها لا تتداخلها أجزاء النجاسة، فيحصل طهارتها ( بِالمَسْحِ ) بتراب، أو خرقة، أو صوف الشاة المذبوحة، أو غير ذلك، فلا يبقى بعد المسح إلا القليل، وهو غير معتبر، ويحصل بالمسح حقيقة التطهير، في رواية، فإذا قطع به البطيخ ونحوه، يحل أكله، واختاره « الإسبيجابي»، ويحرم على رواية التقليل، واختاره ( القدوري»، ومثل ذلك المني إذا فرك، والأرض إذا جفت، وجلد الميتة إذا دبغ دباغة حكمية؛ كتتريب وتشميس، والبئر إذا غارت، ثم عاد ماؤها، والآجر المفروش إذا تنجس، ثم قلع، وقد اختلف التصحيح في كل منهما، والأولى اعتبار الطهارة في الكل، كمـا تـفـيـده المتون، وملاقاة الطاهر الطاهر لا يوجب التنجيس، ولا فرق في طهارة الصقيل بالمسح بين الرطب، والجاف، والبول، والعذرة على ما ذكره ( الكرخي»، لشمول العلة، وذكر في «الأصل» أن البول والدم لا يطهران إلا بالغسل، وكذا العذرة الرطبة، والمختار للفتوى ما قاله « الكرخي»، لأن الصحابة كانوا يقتلون الكفار بسيوفهم ثم يمسحونها، ويصلون معها.
And the sword and the like are purified by wiping. And the sword and the like of iron and polished (ṣaqīl), like the mirror and knife, are purified, and we restrict by it against rough, rusty, and engraved (items), also brass (ṣufr), oiled vessels, turned wood (khurāṭī), ebony, and the like, and nail, because parts of impurity do not penetrate them, so their purification is achieved by wiping with dust, or a cloth, or the wool of a slaughtered sheep, or other than that; nothing remains after wiping except a little, and it is not considered. And purification is achieved by wiping as actual purification according to one narration; so if one cuts with it a watermelon and the like, it is permissible to eat it, and this was chosen by al-Isbijābī. And it is forbidden according to the narration of reduction (taqlīl), chosen by al-Qudūrī. And similar to that is semen if rubbed, and the earth if it dries, and the hide of a dead animal if tanned with a legal tanning such as dusting and sunning, and the well if its water sinks then returns, and baked bricks if they become impure then are removed. And the correct view has differed in each of them, and the first is to consider purification in all, as the texts indicate. And the meeting of pure with pure does not cause impurity. And there is no difference in the purification of polished surfaces by wiping between wet and dry, urine and feces, according to what al-Karkhī mentioned, due to the generality of the cause. And it is mentioned in al-Aṣl that urine and blood are not purified except by washing, likewise wet feces. And the chosen for fatwa is what al-Karkhī said, because the Companions used to kill unbelievers with their swords then wipe them and pray with them.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on purifying swords and other shiny (ṣaqīl) non-porous surfaces. The method is wiping (masḥ) with dust, cloth, or wool, which removes impurity particles since they do not penetrate such surfaces. There is a dispute: one narration (chosen by al-Isbijābī) holds that wiping achieves actual purification (taṭhīr ḥaqīqī), so the sword is genuinely pure and can be used to cut food. Another narration (from al-Qudūrī) says it only reduces impurity, so the sword remains impure. The preferred view is the first. The passage also mentions analogous cases: semen rubbed off, dry ground, tanned hide, well water returning, bricks removed, etc. Another dispute: al-Karkhī holds that wiping suffices for urine and feces even when wet, while al-Aṣl (the work of Muhammad) requires washing. The chosen fatwa is al-Karkhī's, based on the practice of the Companions who wiped their swords after killing and prayed with them.

Key Terms
الصقيل (al-ṣaqīl): Polished, shiny surface that does not absorb liquids, such as a sword, mirror, or knife.
المسح (al-masḥ): Wiping with a dry or slightly moist substance (dust, cloth) to remove impurity.
التقليل (al-taqlīl): The view that wiping only reduces the impurity, not removes it entirely, so the item remains impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, only water can purify
B) Yes, wiping works for all polished surfaces
C) Only if the impurity is dry
D) Only for urine, not feces

Key Takeaway
Polished non-porous items are purified by wiping, and the preferred Hanafi view holds that this achieves actual purity, applicable even to wet urine and feces.

Purification of Earth by Drying: Tahir vs Tahur
Ruling on purification of earth by drying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of earth by drying, identify the evidentiary basis, and distinguish between tahir and tahur in the context of tayammum.

Core Idea
Earth becomes pure (tahir) by drying but not purifying (tahur) for tayammum.

This passage explains how earth contaminated with najasah becomes pure for salah once the trace disappears and the earth dries. However, this purified earth cannot be used for tayammum because tayammum requires earth that is tahur (purifying), not merely tahir (pure). The text provides proofs from hadith and practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the purification of earth by drying; the ruling is that salah is permissible on such earth, but tayammum is not.
The ruling has two parts: permissibility for salah, prohibition for tayammum.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites sayings of 'Aisha, Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah, Abu Qilabah, a marfu' hadith in al-Mabsut, and practice of Ibn Umar.
These are ahadith and athar, all with zanni thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'زَكَاةُ الْأَرْضِ يُبُسُهَا' indicates that drying is the means of purification, analogous to zakat purifying wealth.
The word 'zakat' here means purification, not obligatory charity.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A riwaya from Abu Hanifa allows tayammum from such earth, but the relied-upon position (azhar) is that tayammum is not allowed.
The distinction between tahir and tahur is key.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a person cannot perform tayammum from earth that has dried after najasah, whereas salah is valid on it.
This affects purification options when water is unavailable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ذَهَبَ أَثَرُ النَّجَاسَةِ عَنِ الْأَرْضِ وَجَفَّتْ جَازَتِ الصَّلَاةُ عَلَيْهَا، لِمَا رُوِيَ عَنْ عَائِشَةَ وَمُحَمَّدِ بْنِ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ: «زَكَاةُ الْأَرْضِ يُبُسُهَا»، وَعَنْ أَبِي قِلَابَةَ: «جُفُوفُ الْأَرْضِ طَهُورُهَا»، وَفِي الْمَبْسُوطِ مَرْفُوعًا إِلَى النَّبِيِّ ﷺ: «أَيُّمَا أَرْضٍ جَفَّتْ فَقَدْ زَكَتْ». وَرُوِيَ عَنِ ابْنِ عُمَرَ: كُنْتُ أَبِيتُ فِي الْمَسْجِدِ فِي عَهْدِ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ ﷺ، وَكُنْتُ فَتًى شَابًّا عَزَبًا، وَكَانَتِ الْكِلَابُ تَبُولُ وَتُقْبِلُ وَتُدْبِرُ فِي الْمَسْجِدِ، وَلَمْ يَكُونُوا يَرُدُّونَ شَيْئًا مِنْ ذَلِكَ. دُونَ التَّيَمُّمِ مِنْهَا، وَيَطْهُرُ مَا بِهَا مِنْ شَجَرٍ وَكَلَإٍ قَائِمٍ بِجَفَافِهِ.
And when the trace of najasah disappears from the earth and it dries, salah upon it is permissible, due to what was narrated from 'Aisha and Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah: 'The purification of earth is its dryness,' and from Abu Qilabah: 'The drying of earth is its purification,' and in al-Mabsut raised to the Prophet (ﷺ): 'Any earth that dries has become pure.' And it was narrated from Ibn 'Umar: 'I used to spend the night in the mosque in the time of the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ), and I was a young unmarried man, and the dogs would urinate, come and go in the mosque, and they would not repel anything of that.' Without tayammum from it, and whatever is in it of trees and standing vegetation becomes pure by its drying.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the purification of earth by drying. The Hanafi position is that when najasah is on the earth and the trace disappears and the earth dries, salah is permissible on that spot. The drying itself is the cause of purification (istihala). Evidence includes sayings of the Companions and a marfu' hadith. However, tayammum is not allowed with this earth because tayammum requires earth that is tahur (purifying), while this earth is only tahir (pure). The vegetation growing on such earth also becomes pure by the drying of the earth.

Key Terms
الجفاف (al-jafaf): The drying of earth after najasah; it is the cause of tahara.
زكاة الأرض (zakatu al-ard): Purification of earth; metaphor for drying.
الطهورية (al-tahuriyyah): The quality of being purifying (used for tayammum).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After najasah dries on earth, is tayammum allowed with that earth?
A) Yes, because it is pure
B) No, because it is not tahur
C) Yes, if it is dry
D) No, because it remains najis

Key Takeaway
Earth purified by drying is tahir for salah but not tahur for tayammum.

Purification by Complete Transformation (Istihala)
Ruling with proof on istihala and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istihala and apply the distinction between accidental and intentional transformation.

Core Idea
A najasah becomes pure when its substance entirely transforms into something else.

The Hanafi madhhab holds that najasah is not a permanent attribute; it is attached to the specific substance. When that substance ceases to exist through complete transformation, purity is restored. This lesson explores the principle of istihala, its proofs, and a crucial exception based on necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi position: a najis substance becomes tahir (pure) when its essence completely changes (istihala).
The text says 'ala al-sahih' - this is the relied-upon view.
2
Understand the Dalil (Proof)
The ruling is based on the idea that the Shariah attaches the description of najasah to a specific reality. When that reality is negated (by transformation), the ruling is removed. Examples: dead flesh turning to salt, bones to ash; the development of nutfah to mudghah; wine becoming vinegar.
These analogies (qiyas) show that the ruling follows the substance, not the origin.
3
Analyze the Exception (Vapor Case)
The text discusses vapor from toilets/ stables. By qiyas, it would be najis. But by istihsan (juristic preference), it is pure due to necessity and difficulty of avoidance.
This is a classic Hanafi application of istihsan overriding qiyas.
4
Contrast Intentional Distillation
If one deliberately distils najasah (e.g., wine dregs to make 'araqi), the distillate remains najis because there is no necessity.
The illa (effective cause) for istihsan is necessity; without it, qiyas prevails.
5
Apply to a Branch Ruling
The passage mentions eggs of non-edible animals: two views exist (najis or pure). Consider how istihala might apply if the egg transforms after boiling?
This shows that not all cases are settled; the principle guides further reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَطْهُرُ نجاسة استحالت عينها، كأن صارت ملحاً، أو أحترقت بالنار، فيصير رمادها طاهراً على الصحيح، لأن الشرع رتب وصف النجاسة على تلك الحقيقة، وتنتفي الحقيقة بانتفاء بعض أجزاء مفهومها، فكيف بالكل. فإن الملح غير اللحم والعظم، فإذا صارت ملحاً، ترتب عليه حكمه، ونظيره في النطفة نجسة، ثم تصير علقة وهي نجسة، وتصير مضغة فتطهر، والعصير طاهر فيصير خمراً فينجس، ثم يصير خلاً، فيطهر، فعرفنا استحالة العين يستتبع زوال الوصف المرتب عليها. تنبيه: إذا ارتفع بخار الكنيف والإصطبل الذي فيه روث الدواب، فاستجمد في الكوة أو الجدار، ثم ذاب، وقطر، نَجَّس ما أصابه، لاجتماع الجمد من أجزاء النجاسة قياساً، ولا ينجس استحساناً، وكذا الحمام، إذا أُهريق فيه النجاسة فعرّق حيطانه وكواته فتقاطر، وجه الاستحسان الضرورة وعـــدم التحرز أو تعسره، فعلى هذا لو استقطر النجاسة، فالقاطر نجس، لانتفاء الضرورة، كالمستقطر من دردي الخمر - ويسمى بالعرقي في بلاد الروم- نجس حرام، كسائر المحرمات. فرع: بيض ما لا يؤكل، قيل: نجس كلحمه وقيل: طاهر.
And a najasah whose substance has changed (istihala) becomes pure, such as when it becomes salt, or is burned by fire, so its ash becomes pure according to the correct view, because the Shariah has attached the description of najasah to that reality, and the reality is negated with the negation of some parts of its concept, so how much more with the whole. Indeed, salt is different from flesh and bone, so when it becomes salt, its ruling is attached to it. Its analogy is the nutfah (semen) which is najis, then becomes a clot (alaqah) which is najis, then becomes a lump (mudghah) and becomes pure; and juice is pure, then becomes wine (khamr) and becomes najis, then becomes vinegar (khal) and becomes pure. So we know that change of substance entails removal of the description attached to it. Note: If vapor rises from a toilet or stable containing animal dung, and it condenses in a niche or wall, then melts and drips, it (the drip) najass what it hits according to qiyas, but does not according to istihsan. Similarly, a bathhouse if najasah is poured in it and its walls and niches sweat and drip, the basis of istihsan is necessity and lack of avoidance or its difficulty. Based on this, if one deliberately distils najasah, the distillate is najis because necessity is absent, like the distillate from wine dregs - called 'araqi in the land of Rum - it is najis and haram like all prohibited things. Branch: Eggs of what is not eaten: it is said it is najis like its meat, and it is said it is pure.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining istihala (complete transformation of substance). When a najis substance changes entirely into a different substance, it becomes pure. Examples: dead flesh turning into salt, or bones burning to ash. The reasoning is that the ruling of najasah is attached to the specific substance, so when that substance no longer exists, the ruling is removed. An analogy is the development of a fetus and the transformation of wine to vinegar. The note (tanbih) discusses a practical issue: vapor from toilets or stables condensing and dripping. By qiyas it would be najis, but by istihsan (juristic preference) it is considered pure due to necessity and difficulty of avoidance. However, if one deliberately distils najasah, the distillate is najis because there is no necessity. Similarly, distillate from wine dregs (araqi) is najis and haram. A branch ruling on eggs of non-edible animals: there are two views on their purity.

Key Terms
استحالة (istihala): Complete transformation of the essence of a substance into another substance.
التنبي (al-tanbih): A note clarifying an application of the rule.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference over qiyas due to necessity or equity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on the distilled liquid from wine dregs (al-araqi)?
A) Pure because it is not wine
B) Najis because it is distilled from wine dregs
C) Pure like all distilled liquids
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Complete transformation of a najis substance (istihala) renders it pure, but intentional distillation of najasah remains najis because necessity does not apply.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Purification of Dry and Wet Mani: Scraping vs Washing
Ruling on purification of semen by scraping when dry and washing when wet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of dry and wet mani, distinguish the method based on dryness/wetness, and identify the condition that requires washing instead of scraping.

Core Idea
Dry mani purified by scraping, wet by washing; prior urine without istinja' demands washing.

In Hanafi fiqh, semen (mani) is considered najis. The method of purification depends on whether it is dry or wet. This lesson examines the detailed ruling from Nur al-Idah and its commentary, including an intra-Hanafi difference and conditions that affect the ruling. You will learn the relied-upon view and its proof from the hadith of 'Aisha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is purification of mani. Mani is najis; the method varies by state: dry or wet.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from 'Aisha (Muslim) is cited: the Prophet used to wash mani from his garment.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to wash indicates najasah of mani and specifies the method for wet mani.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi: Abu Hanifa held that scraping does not purify the body; the Sahibayn and relied-upon view hold that it does due to necessity. Also a condition: if mani comes after urine without istinja', washing is required.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi is that dry mani is purified by scraping from both garment and body, as stated in zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطهر المني الجاف، سواء كان مني رجل أو امرأة على الصحيح، بفركه عن الثوب، ولو كان جديداً مبطناً، وعن البدن بفركه في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي حنيفة: لا يطهر البدن بالفرك لرطوبته، وظاهر الرواية طهارته، للضرورة، إذا خرج المني بعد الاستنجاء بالماء، أما لو بال، ولم يستنج، فلا يطهر المني إلا بالغسل، لعدمها، وقيل: لو بال ولم ينتشر بوله على رأس الذكر، أو انتشر، ولكن خرج المني دفقاً من غير انتشار على محل البول، لا يحكم بتنجيسه به، لأنه لم يوجد سوى مروره على أثر البول في مجراه، ولا حكم له في الباطن. ويطهر المني الرطب بغسله، لما روى مسلم عن عائشة: «أنه كان يغسل المني من ثوبه ثم يخرج إلى الصلاة وأثر الغسل فيه».
And dry mani (semen) is purified by scraping (fark) from the garment, even if it is new and lined, and from the body by scraping according to the apparent riwaya. And from Abu Hanifa: the body is not purified by scraping because of its moisture, but the apparent riwaya is its purification due to necessity. If the mani comes out after istinja' with water, [it is pure by scraping]. But if he urinated and did not perform istinja', then mani is not purified except by washing, due to lack of necessity. And it was said: if he urinated and his urine did not spread over the head of the penis, or it spread but mani came out gushing without spreading over the site of urine, it is not ruled that it is contaminated by it, because there is only its passing over the trace of urine in its passage, and there is no ruling in the interior. And wet mani is purified by washing, due to what Muslim narrated from 'Aisha: 'He (the Prophet) used to wash mani from his garment then go out to prayer while the trace of washing was in it.'
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the purification of mani (semen). Mani is considered najis in the Hanafi school. Dry mani can be purified by scraping (fark) from both garment and body, according to the apparent riwaya (zahir al-riwayah). The condition is that the mani came out after istinja' with water. If there was prior urine without istinja', scraping is insufficient and washing is required. A detail is given about possible mixing with urine. Wet mani requires washing, as proven by the hadith of 'Aisha. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa held that the body is not purified by scraping due to its moisture, but the relied-upon view (zahir al-riwayah) is that it is purified due to necessity.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniyy): Semen; considered najis in Hanafi school.
الفرك (al-fark): Scraping or rubbing dry to remove impurity.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The ruling transmitted in the six authoritative books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on dry mani on a garment?
A) It must be washed with water
B) It is purified by scraping
C) It is not najis
D) It requires sand

Key Takeaway
Dry mani is purified by scraping; wet mani by washing; if prior urine without istinja', washing is required.

Taharah — Purification

Purification of Earth by Drying and Ruling on Tayammum
Explains purification of earth by drying and ruling on tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purificatory status of earth that has become pure by drying and explain why tayammum from it is not valid according to the preferred view.

Core Idea
Earth becomes pure by drying, but tayammum is invalid—purifying power (tahuriyyah) is needed, not mere purity (taharah).

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains an important distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the difference between purity (taharah) and purifying capacity (tahuriyyah). While earth and plants become pure by drying, they are not purifying substances suitable for tayammum. The commentator provides a Quranic proof and notes an intra-school difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that earth and plants become pure (tahir) by drying, but tayammum from them is invalid. Understand the two-part ruling: purity is achieved, but purifying capacity is not.
The ruling is declaratory (sahih) not obligational.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the Quranic verse: 'فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيدًا طَيِّبَا' (seek pure earth for tayammum). This is the primary evidence.
The verse is from Surah al-Ma'idah:6 or al-Nisa:43.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse requires the earth to be 'tayyib' (pure). The Hanafi interpretation holds that 'tayyib' here means tahur (purifying), not just tahir. Earth dried from najasah is only tahir, so it does not satisfy the condition.
This is a zanni dalala (the meaning is not absolutely clear) but preferred in the school.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa reportedly allowed tayammum from such earth by analogy to prayer, which is valid on it. This is a minority view within the school.
The text says 'روي عن أبي حنيفة جواز التيمم منها'.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The preferred view (al-azhar) is that tayammum is invalid. The verse is taken as the stronger evidence, and the majority of later Hanafi scholars adopted this view.
The mufta bihi position is the first view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دون التيمم منها، ويطهر ما بها من شجر وكلا قائم بجفافِهِ. قال الشارح: فدل على طهارتها بالجفاف، ولأن الأرض من طبعها أن تحيل الأشياء، وتنقلها إلى طبعها، فتطهر بالاستحالة، وتجوز الصلاة عليها (دُونَ التّيممِ مِنْهَا) في الأظهر، لأن التيمم يفتقر إلى الطهورية. وبما رويناه ثبتت الطهارة لا الطَّهورية وروي عن أبي حنيفة جواز التيمم منها، كالصلاة عليها، والظاهر الأول، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيدًا طَيِّبَا الليلة : ٦﴾ ( ويطُهرُ مَا بِهَا)، أي: الأرض (مِنْ شَجَر وكلا)، أي: عشب (قائم)، أي: نابت فيها ( بِجَفَافِهِ) وذهاب أثر النجاسة تبعاً لِلأرض، على المختار، واحترزنا به عن قول من خص طهارته بالغسل.
Without tayammum from it, and what is on it of trees and grass becomes pure by its drying. The commentator said: This indicates its purity by drying, and because the earth by its nature transforms things and changes them to its nature, so it becomes pure by transformation. Prayer is permissible on it (without tayammum from it) in the most apparent view, because tayammum requires purifying quality. By what we have narrated, purity is established, not purifying quality. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa the permissibility of tayammum from it, like prayer on it. The apparent view is the first, due to the saying of the Most High: 'So seek tayammum with pure earth' (Surah al-Ma'idah or al-Nisa). And what is on it (i.e., the earth) of trees and grass (i.e., herbage) that is standing (i.e., growing in it) becomes pure by its drying and the disappearance of the trace of impurity, following the earth, according to the chosen view. And we caution with this against the saying of those who restrict its purification to washing.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the ruling that earth and plants become pure by drying. The author first states the matn: 'Without tayammum from it' meaning that although prayer is valid on such earth, tayammum from it is not permitted. The reason is that tayammum requires the substance to be purifying (tahur), but the earth after drying is only pure (tahir), not purifying. The commentator provides proof from the natural property of earth to transform impurities, and from the Quranic verse on tayammum. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa is reported to have allowed tayammum from it, but the preferred view (al-azhar) is that it is not allowed. The section also specifies that trees and grass growing in such earth become pure by drying, as they are attached to the earth.

Key Terms
طهورية (tahuriyyah): The quality of being purifying (mutahhir), i.e., capable of removing ritual impurity.
استحالة (istihalah): Transformation of the substance, here the earth changes the najasah to its own nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason that tayammum from earth purified by drying is invalid according to the preferred Hanafi view?
A) The earth is impure.
B) The earth lacks tahuriyyah (purifying quality).
C) The earth is not solid enough.
D) The drying process is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
Earth becomes pure by drying but does not become purifying; tayammum requires the earth to be tahur, not just tahir.
